maintenance are implemented.

2.2.2 Life Cycle Activities

The  narrowly  defined  “aging”,  or  “deterioration”,  can  be  controlled  by  engineers
through  properly  performing  “life  cycle  activities”.  In  brief,  the  life  cycle  of  an  asset
(group)  is  the timespan  from  its  creation to  its  disposal.  LC  Activities  can  be  roughly
classified
three  domains:  creation/acquisition,  utilization/maintenance,  and
replacement/disposal.

into

A  life  cycle can  refer to  an  asset  (group)  of  significantly  different  scales,  namely
subcomponents  (e.g.  a  ceramic  insulator),  components  (e.g.  a  circuit  breaker),  or
systems (e.g. a substation). The life cycle of a system can contain multiple life cycles of
its  components.  Similarly,  an  activity  “program”  on  the  system  [16]  is  composed  of
multiple activity “tasks” [16] on its components. The major ones within these tasks for
components, such as replacements, are frequently downgraded as a minor event for the
maintenance program for the system. This composition from tasks into programs, called

25

“maintenance  delivery” in [34],  can  be  optimized  by  service  providers to reduce  costs
and consumption of other critical resources.

When  components  are  managed  at  the  operational  level,  the  issue  of  aging,  or
deterioration  in  particular,  is  the  primary  consideration  by  both  asset  managers  and
service  providers  (to  be  defined  in  Section  2.2.5)  to  manage  the  three  domains  of  LC
Activities which have also appeared in Figure 2.2:

1)  In  the  maintenance  domain,  a  number  of  recovery  techniques  and
subcomponent  replacements  can  be  identified  for  each  component  type,
because of their capability to remove cumulative effects of deterioration, as
the green crosses show in Figure 2.2. Naturally, this capability is referred
as maintainability of a deterioration process or an aging mechanism.

2)   In

the  replacement  domain,  deterioration  processes  with  poor
maintainability, are likely to dominate the failure mechanism. Hence, these
dominant  deterioration  processes  are  necessary  to  be  diagnosed  to
determine  replacements.  The  time-point  of  diagnosis  and  replacement  is
shown  with the  yellow  and  red  signs in  Figure  2.2. The  diagnosis  can  be
derived  from  multiple  sources  including  off-line  measurements  within
maintenance or on-line monitoring during utilization.

3)  In  the  creation  domain,  asset  workers  need  to  adapt  the  configuration  of
new components to strategic life-ending conditions. Changes on operation
environments,  legal  requirements,  supply  chain  and  personnel  should  be
foreseen  decades  in  advance,  so  that  consequent  strategic  life-ending
conditions can avoided at the beginning of a life cycle (e.g. the left end of
Figure 2.2 where service life is zero).

2.2.3 Asset and Asset System

At  different  levels,  asset  management,  especially  its  decision-making  concerns
different  groups  of  physical  assets.  In  PAS  55,  the  asset  groups  concerned  at  the
operational, tactical and strategic level are respectively named as “asset” (items), “asset
systems” and “asset portfolio”. Technically, we recommend to describe an asset group
by using four features. They are:

static

information,

the
(sub)components, and, in many cases, location.
the function in operation;

including

the

type,

construction

and

•

•

26

•
•

the hazards: i.e. the scenarios to lose (part of) the functions;
the actions it requires to keep its function.

This  section  will  explain the  above  four features  for an  “asset”  at the  operational

level, and an “asset system” at the tactical level.

1)  High-voltage assets and asset systems
In general, assets are economic resources owned by a financial and legal entity to
support its business. Specifically, the “physical” category of assets is a subset of fixed
assets and distinguished from human, information, financial and intangible assets[16]. In
electrical  power  grids,  physical  assets  include  the  circuit  components,  the  monitoring
and  control  devices,  the  communication  systems,  the  data  processing,  storage  and
visualization systems, the civil works, etc.

This thesis focuses on the high-voltage asset systems, which serve as the backbone
of the AC2 electricity transmission and distribution networks3. These asset systems are
listed below, as well as their major composing asset (items).

•  Overhead  line  system:  composed  of  conductor,  insulator,  towers/poles,

groundings, etc.

•  Underground cable system: composed of cables, joints, terminals, etc.
•  Substation  system:  At  primary  side,  it  is composed of  groundings,  circuit
breakers,  disconnectors,  measurement  transformers,  power  transformers,
surge arrestors, etc. The substation system can be classified as air-insulated
and gas-insulated.

A  substation  system  also  contains  significant  number  of  assets  at  the  secondary  side.
These  assets  are  frequently  handled  as  a  separate  asset  group  called  secondary
equipment.

2 Applications  of  DC  in  electricity  network  is  increasing.  However,  this  thesis  will  not  discuss
them,  since  their  failure  mechanisms  and  failure  modes  are  not  yet  commonly  investigated  in

field conditions by the engineers.
3 Since  the  electricity  generation  has  been  separated  from  the  transmission  and  distribution
business in most EU countries, power generation plants will not be discussed in this thesis.

27

The  growth  of  power  grid  keeps  creating  new  types  of  asset  systems.  Secondary
equipment such as SCADA and ICT systems have expanded significantly in the last two
decades  [18]. With  the  introduction  of  HVDC  technology, HVDC  overhead  line/cable
connections [42-44] and HVDC converter stations [45] have become new types of asset
systems. Meanwhile, the emergence of renewable energy is giving rise to asset systems
such  as  collector  substations  for  wind  farms[46],  large  scale  energy  storage
installations[47], etc.

The scale of high-voltage asset system is smaller than the power system. As a part
of the power system, a substation or overhead line/cable connection are mainly studied
according to its circuit and electrical parameters. In contrast, when being treated as an
asset  system,  a  substation  or  connection  is  studied  from  all  technical,  economical  and
societal aspects.

Relevantly, an asset physically refers to a high-voltage apparatus. Since apparatuses
are components of asset systems, we call them high-voltage components or components
for short in this thesis. The term “asset” emphasizes the economic and societal attributes
as well. In contrast, the word “components” emphasizes that it is part of an asset system,
and it is composed of “subcomponents” such as spare parts.

2)  Static information of assets and asset systems
The static information of assets and asset systems are commonly organized in two
ways: a tree and a list. When static information of assets is organized as tree-shaped data,
different  levels  of  tree  nodes  indicate  the  system  (e.g.  gas  insulated  substation),
subsystems  (e.g.  bus),  components  (e.g.  circuit  breaker)  and  subcomponents  (e.g.  gas
pressure gauge). The tree-shaped data can present the composition relationships between
different levels of assets. In the daily operation and maintenance at the operational level
of AM, service providers can easily use the tree-shaped data to identify individual assets.

the  components  and

Compared  with  a  list-shaped  structure,  the  list-shaped  structure  of  asset  static
information  simply  categorizes
technical
specifications  in  different  data  tables.  The  list-shaped  asset  data  performs  two  roles,
mainly at the tactical level of AM. Firstly, it is convenient for asset analysts to learn the
performance (e.g. failure rate) of assets of the same type. Secondly, it can be applied to
a  “multi-site”  situation  in  which  a  component  is  installed  in  different  systems  at
different locations in its life cycle.

records

their

28

3)  Function, hazards and actions of assets at the operational level
The  operational  level  of  AM  arranges  LC  Activities  to  maintain  the  routine
functionality of assets. The main outcomes from the operational level are technical: the
reliability and availability of components. Therefore, a high-voltage asset item listed in
the subsection 1).

•

should perform a certain technical function after installation.

•
•  may  contain  subcomponents  and  frequently  loses  its  function  after
subcomponent  failure.  Hence,  hazards  of  an  asset  item  can  classified
through identifying failed subcomponents.
is  constructed  according  to  certain  technical  standards  ,  therefore  require
specialists  from  service  provider  to  perform  life  cycle  activates(e.g.
maintenance, diagnosis, etc.) on it. LC Activities are the technical part of
the actions on an asset item.
is purchasable and warehoused with standardized technical specifications.
Purchasing  and  inventory  are  the  operational  and  non-technical  part  of
actions on an asset item.

•

Handling  of  asset  items  is  assigned  to  service  providers,  under  assistance  of  a

purchase manager and a warehouse manager.

4)  Function, hazards and actions of asset system at the tactical level
The tactical level of AM decides on investments to ensure the long-term continuity
of the network to deliver power. Its performance is measured financially. This includes
not only the accounting records of expenditures, but also financially evaluated technical
events, such as LC Activities, outages, overloading and pollutions (see 2.2.3 for details).
An “Asset system” is a set of assets that interact and/or are interrelated so as to deliver
a required business function or service [16]. In other words, components/asset items are
investigated as a whole system in performance evaluation, risk assessment and decision-
making.

•  Functions: An asset system functions as an integrated unit of the circuit to
deliver power. To fulfill a number of requirements from the network:

o  The  system  capacity  is  calculated  from  its  component  ratings,

during which the bottleneck component can be identified.

29

o  Other  circuit  parameters,  such  as  impedance,  will  lead  to  energy
losses. In routine operation, these parameters are used to dispatch
the load.
In  an  electricity  market,  the  economic  value  of  a  capacity
bottleneck and annual energy loss can be assessed.

o

o  Upgrade  of  a  high-voltage  system  requires  a  redesign  at  the
network level [35]. In turn, the upgrades are constantly associated
with long-term network planning.

•  Hazards: An asset system often contains certain degree of redundancy or
reserve  capacity  to  limit  the  hazardous  effect  of  its  components.
Consequently,

o  The  system  has  its  own  historical  failure  record,  which  is
associated  to  the  interruption  of  power  delivery  service  and
summarized as the performance indicator “reliability”. [10]

o  The  system  has  multiple  failure  modes.  From  historical  failure
records,  the  criticality  of  a  failure  mode  to  the  reliability
performance can be derived. [41]

o  The  reliability  of  the  system  is  predicted  through  combining  the
probability  of  each  failure  mode.  And  the  probability  of  a  failure
mode  is  calculated  from  the  health  index  of  corresponding
components.

o  Multiple life cycle activity “tasks” on the same asset system can be
merged as a “program”[16]. The primary intention of this merge is
to minimize the scheduled outages.

•  Actions: An asset system has a life cycle not limited by component aging.
Within  the  life  cycle,  the  actions  on  the  asset  systems  are  managed  in
economic rather than technical ways below:

o  Decisions are made at the tactical level on AM plans to (re)invest

on the components.

o  Operation  and  maintenance  costs  spent  on  components  are
summed  as  the  life  cycle  cost  for  each  component,  or  the  system
maintenance cost for all components within a system.

o  The  disposal  of  a  complete  asset  system  is  more  often  than  not
because of strategic reasons, such as network planning, rather than
due to aging.

30

•  Additionally, since the location of a high-voltage asset system is normally
fixed,  its  geographical  environment  can  lead  to  the  information  below
regarding  the  functions,  hazards  and  actions  of  the  system  and  its
components.

o

o  Meteorological and geological conditions can influence functions
and  hazards  of  an  asset  system,  through  effecting  its  capacity
rating and failure modes.
Its  existence  can  be  easily  noticed  by  non-technical  stakeholders
such as the consumers, community and regulator, especially during
outages  and  maintenance.  Consequent  societal/political  issues  are
frequently  linked  with  certain  asset  systems.  This  leads  to  extra
actions, such as technical measures and financial investments, on
asset systems.

Accordingly, the asset manager, as the main decider at the tactical level, cooperates
with a wide range of technical and non-technical specialists to evaluate and improve the
performance  of  asset  systems.  These  characters  include  system  operator,  system
designer,  solution  supplier,  network  planner,  risk  manager,  finance  manager,  outage
manager  and  service  provider,  as  listed  above.  See  Section  2.2.4  and  2.2.5  for  the
introduction of performance indicators and characters.

2.2.4 Performance and Risks

Performance  indicators  are  the  primary  parameters  for  the  asset  manager  to
optimize  at  the  tactical  level  of  AM.  Currently,  the  performance  of  TSO/DSO  can  be
investigated  from  five  aspects.  According  to  a  Cigré  survey  [10],  these  aspects  are
reliability,  safety  and  finance  for  most  utility  companies,  as  well  as  compliance  and
image,  for  a  limited  number  of  utility  companies.  These  five  aspects,  named  as
“business  values”  in  [18],  are  actually  linked  to  OSP’s  and  ultimately  satisfying
stakeholder requirements [21].

When  power  delivery  was  a  monopoly  sector,  its  performance  depended  on  only
one  factor:  the  (1)  reliability  in  Table  2.2.  This  normally  meant  the  frequency  and
duration of outages (frequently referred as service quality), but, in serious incidents, the
(2)  personal  safety  is  another  critical  concern.  The  corporatization  of  electricity
transmission & distribution sector introduces financial incentives and, consequently, (3)
financial performance.

31

Meanwhile,  authorities  subject  the  network  operators  to  (4)  legal  liabilities  and
regulatory penalties. The former is applied when LC Activities break the law. The latter
is typically based on yardstick competition between operators with similar positions in
the network (e.g. transmission or distribution) regarding their public liabilities [48, 49].

Finally, the performance with respect to (5) public image is reflected in the media
reports. These reports respond not only to reliability and safety incidents, but also to a
wide range of other issues, such as environment protection.

To  investigate  the  influence  of  an  incident  or  an  investment  on  the  above  five
aspects, it is critical to build a performance metric system. A performance metric system
frequently  presents  itself  as  a  risk  consequence  matrix  in  Table  2.2.  It  is  constructed
with two essential parts.

The first  part is the  practical  standards  to  measure  the  consequences  of  particular
incidents and investments. Each column of Table 2.2 shows the metrics of performances
regarding the corresponding “business values”. To ensure the practicality of the metric,
the measuring units, such as blackout, damage, injuries, convictions and media reports,
should be well documented by TSO/DSO for decades.

The  second  part  is  the  conversion  between  the  rated  performance  regarding
different  “business  values”.  For  example,  in  Table  2.2,  each  customer  minute  loss  is
equivalent  with  €0.5  damage.  The  equivalence  depends  on  the  electricity  delivery
contract and the regulations. For example, in the standard Dutch power supply contract,
the liabilities to supply each client of 30 - 160 kVA through 220/380V network is €100
for  every  4  hour  blackout  [49].  Meanwhile,  a  50  kV  to  10  kV  feeding  transformer
typical in the Dutch distribution grid, is rated from 20 to 40 MVA. If a transformer fails,
which is considered as a medium voltage substation failure categorized as “moderate” in
service quality, hundreds of clients will have blackouts. Consequently, the total liability
will range from 10K to 100K euro, which equals to a “moderate” in finance (See Table
2.2).

The  measured  performance,  named  as  key  performance  indicators  (KPI’s),  plays
two roles within a utility company. Firstly, KPI’s are reported to the regulator annually.
In  Netherlands,  when  the  performance  of  one  company  is  poorer  than  the  other
competing companies, regulatory penalties will be imposed as a certain percentages of
the annual revenue, as shown in the compliance column in Table 2.2.

32

4 In Netherlands, a fine in the first, fourth or sixth category is respectively € 335, € 16,750 or €670,000 in
maximum.
5 The  asset  owner  is  obliged  to  comply  with  the  instructions  from  the  authority  in  a  certain  time  fame,
receive a penalty.

33

Secondly, gains/losses of KPI’s are predicted within the TSO/DSO under the theme
“risk management”. To achieve gains and to prevent losses of KPI are common reasons
to justify investments.

The general definition of a risk is a possibility that a stimulus (e.g. human actions,
events) leads to consequences that harm aspect of things that human beings value [50].
In AM at the tactical level particularly, these “valuable things” are the performance and
the “aspects” are the business values. Therefore, the performance metric matrix is more
frequently referred as “risk consequence matrix” as in the title Table 2.2.

The above definition means that investigations on a risk contain two elements: the
stimulus and the consequences. At the tactical level, the evaluation of consequences is
relatively simply completed through summing the five practically measureable KPI’s in
Table 2.2. Comparatively, more attention will be paid to the technical stimuli. For the
asset  system  failures,  a  globally  accepted  category  of  technical  stimuli,  the  study  is
performed on the root causes of a system failure and the probability of each root cause.
The  former  is  normally  a  major  part  of  FMEA  [41]  and  referred  to  as  a  “root  cause
investigation”  [34].  The  latter  can  be  derived  from  two  sources.  The  first  is  life  data
analysis, which investigates failure history of a population of assets (items or systems)
and  gives  one  failure  frequency  for  the  population.  The  second  source  is  condition
assessment,  which  relies  on  real-time  sensor  data  to  diagnose  the  reliability  for  each
individual asset (item or system). See Chapter 3 for the mathematical descriptions.

In  economics,  the  term  “utility” 6 refers  to  the  ability  of  a  good  or  a  service  to
satisfy  the  needs  or  wants  of  consumers.  The  utility  measures  the  willingness  of  a
consumer  to  buy  a  good/service.  Therefore,  in  a  market,  the  utility  of  a  good/service
strongly influences its price.

The potential consequences of a risk are mostly harmful. The value of such harm is
measured  as  negative  utilities  in  economics.  In  each  instance  of  such  consequences
(such as an incident), the negative utility can be investigated after the risk occurs, rather
than estimated predictively. In contrast, the utility of a risk needs to be predicted. Such

6  The  word  “utility”  in  “utility  company”  means  electricity  and  gas,  which  should  be
distinguished from the above-defined economic “utility” as a measure of satisfaction.

34

predictions  can  be  carried  out  in  many  different  ways.  Among  them,  the  risk-based
approach is widely accepted.

The risk-based approach believes that the expected loss of a risk is its utility [51]. It

means

Step (1): estimating consequences financially,
Step (2): predicting the probability of the risk, and
Step (3): calculating the expected loss through multiplying the probability with the
consequences.

In practice, step (1) above can be finished in the risk consequence matrix in Table
2.2.  After  step  (1)  and  (2),  their  results  respectively  determine  the  rows  and  columns
where a risk is located in the risk (assessment) matrix such as Table 2.3.

TABLE 2.3:  RISK (ASSESSMENT) MATRIX IN [12]

35

The  design  of  a  risk  matrix  allows  risk  analysts to  simplify  the calculation  of  the
expected  value  in  step  (3). The  consequence  (performance  metric  in  the columns  with
caption  “service  quality”, “finance”  or  “compliance”  in  Table  2.2)  and  the  probability
(frequency  in  the  third  row  of  Table  2.3)  estimation  are  in  the  same  10-based
logarithmic  scale.  Consequently,  for  diagonally  adjacent  grids  in  the  risk  matrix  of
Table 2.3, their expected risks are the same, because the expected risk is calculated from
multiplying  the  consequence  with  the  probability.  This  property  of  risk  matrix  is
indicated  with  different  colors  in  Table  2.3.  Therefore,  the  risks  falling  into  cells  of
same color are considered of similar priority to be solved.

The complete risk matrix combing Table 2.2 and 2.3 can assist to rate and prioritize
risks in a short time frame at the operational level of AM. In the medium time frame, the
risk-based  approach  is  an  essential  part  of  the  cost-benefit  analysis.  Based  on  cost-
benefit analyses, investment plans can be chosen according to their profits.

It should be emphasized that the KPI’s at the tactical level of AM are limited to the
several measurable “business values” in Table 2.2, which can easily be extracted from
available  asset  data.  However,  the  OSP’s  are  requiring  asset  managers  to  consider  a
much wider range of economic and societal impacts. Furthermore, in a long time frame
and socio-economic environment, the power grid is subjected to uncertainties of many
other  categories  other  than  above  discussed  technical-failure  oriented  risks.  The
existence  of  these  uncertainties  motivates  the  author  to  focus  on  the  AM  at  strategic
level introduced in Section 2.4.

2.2.5 Organizational Relationships within the Asset Management

corporations.  The

The  deregulation  of  electricity  sector  not  only  divided  the  ownership  of  network
into  multiple
electricity
transmission/distribution  business  were  also  assigned  to  multiple  organizations.
Regarding  the  physical  asset  management,  Cigre  Work  Group  C1.1  has  made  a
distinction between three major roles – asset owner, asset manager and service provider.
This distinction is listed below in italic. In the non-italic part, the original words in [52]
are replaced with their PAS 55 synonyms by us.

responsibilities

run

an

to

•  Asset  owner:  accountable  for  the  business  strategy  (or  OSP’s),  the
direction  of  the  network  company  (including  AM  policies  and  strategies)
and the overall financing of the investments on its asset portfolio;

36

•  Asset  manager:  accountable  for  implementing  risk-based  AM  strategy
through making investment decisions to balance asset/service performance,
financial performance and risks

•  Service  provider:  accountable  for  making  decisions  related  to  delivering
LC  Activities,  especially  maintenance  work  on  time,  within  budget
approved  in  AM  plan,  and  in  a  safe  manner  in  accordance  with  agreed
technical specifications in AM plan.

In future developments of AM, one or a few of these roles can be further divided

into more specific ones. However, we believe three roles are sufficient for this thesis.

The  accountabilities  of  the  asset  owner  and  manager  and  service  provider  as
decision makers are respectively fulfilled at strategic, tactical and operational level, as
Table 2.1 has shown.

In addition, these accountabilities are supported and implemented in a wide range
of  business  functions.  In  a  survey  from  Cigre  Task  Force  23.18[52]  on  its  member
companies,  asset  owner,  asset  manager  and  service  provider  are  associated  with  a
number of business functions around the circle of Figure 2.3. The position of squares in
the  radius  direction  of  Figure  2.3  indicates  the  percentage  of  utility  companies  in  the
survey  that  agrees  a  role  should  perform  a  business  function.  Obviously,  the  farther
away a square from the center, the more likely a role indicated by the color of the square
should perform the business function labelled outside the circle.

From a perspective of a business organization, the asset owner, the asset manager
or the service provider, indicated with brown, red and blue/cyan respectively in Figure
2.3,  frequently  refers  to  companies  rather  than  individuals.  As  described  below,  the
underlined  business  functions  around  the  circle  in  Figure  2.3  are  assigned  to  the
associated organization, such as a department or a company.

Figure 2.3 shows in the red line that within the organization of asset manager:

•  Risk manager performs the risk assessment and decision support processes

mentioned in 2.2.4 within asset management.

•  Asset  system  (Overhead  line/Cable/Substation)  managers  defined  in  [53]
are the division of core responsibilities of asset manager according to the
asset  systems.  They  decide  on  investment  plans  on  corresponding  asset
systems,  including  maintenance  strategies  and  replacements,  and  control

37

the  routine  expenditure  of  investment  plans.  Additionally,  the  knowledge
about physical assets, especially the failure analyses, are also acquired and
handled by asset system managers, because of their technical nature.
•  Maintenance management is another core division of asset manager whose
decisions  mainly  focus  on  maintenance  activities.  Maintenance  manager
contact  with  service  providers,  resource  managers,  outage  managers
routinely to arrange and supervise maintenance delivery.

•  Resource management is a section responsible for planning and allocation
of people, plants, tools and materials according to AM (investment) plans.
Because  of  its  traditional  existence  as  a  AM  function,  AM  has  been
regarded as a subset of Enterprise Resource Planning (ERP) [54].

Figure 2.3: Organizational relationship illustrating the responsibilities of asset owner, asset

manager and service provider, from page 16 of [18].

38

From the overlapping area between the red and brown line, it can be learned that

the asset owner cooperates with the asset manager in several cases:

•  Regulatory and legal affairs are normally a department of the asset owner
which  intends  to  collect  the  requirements  of  regulators,  community  and
other  stakeholders  on  the  asset  portfolio  at  the  strategic  level.  At  the
tactical  level,  this  department  should  ensure  the  political  permit/social
acceptance of decided AM plans.

•  Network  planner  is  a  department  of  the  asset  owner  which  traces  the
dynamics  of  electricity  market  and
the
requirements  to  expand  or  upgrade  the  network.  Realization  of  these
expansions  and  upgrades,  namely  network
requires
cooperation  between  the  asset  owner  and  the  asset  manager,  as  [35]
described.

regulations  and  derives

investments,

The  relationship  between  asset  owner  and  system  operator  varies  significantly  in
different networks. The European “transmission system operator’s” (TSO’s) are both the
asset owner and the system operator of their high voltage networks above 200 kV. In the
Netherlands,  Belgium  and  UK,  most  “distribution  system/network  operators”
(DSO/DNO’s)  are  the  asset  owner  and  the  system  operators  of  their  medium  and  low
voltage networks below 100 kV. However, in other EU countries such as Germany and
France, the municipalities hold the assets and commission the DSO/DNO to operate it.
In  the  Netherlands,  the  networks  of  110  kV  and  150  kV  are  owned  by  the  TSO  and
operated by the DSO’s, since the TSO bought them from the DSO’s in 2007. Generally
speaking,

•  System  operator  reacts  to  the  daily  time  frame  to  stabilize  the  electrical
parameters of the delivered power (e.g. current, voltage, phase, harmonics,
transients,  etc.).  Outage  management  is  normally  the  responsibility  of  a
system  operator.  In  Figure  2.3,  the  outage  management  has  been
categorized as an internal asset worker.

A  service  provider  commonly  refers  to  the  engineering  team  which  delivers  field
maintenance  on  components  of  power  grid.  However,  a  service  provider  can  also
perform several other roles listed below.

•  The contractor of civil works supplementary to creation, maintenance and

disposal of asset systems.

39

•  The testers on assets or asset systems, or the knowledge service providers

to interpret test results, perform diagnoses and support decisions.

•  The engineering designer of assets or asset systems, frequently as the pre-

sale or after-sale service team of component manufacturers.

•  The  maintainer  of  business  IT  systems  for  either  engineering  control  or

internal management.

One  critical  topic  of  organizational relationship  is the  management  of  contracting
and outsourcing [55]. For transmission grids, the Cigre survey at page 16 of  [18] shows
that most companies have an internal asset manager. “Internal” means that a TSO/DSO
does  not  outsource  finance,  project  supervision  and  decision-making  on  maintenance
strategies.  In  addition,  asset  data  and  knowledge  are  also  highly  recommended  to  be
accumulated internally.

However,  service  providers  are  frequently  offering  a  complete  RCM  solution
including  data,  knowledge  and  decision-making.  These  solutions  are  applied  in  asset
fleets which (1) are  poorly automated, therefore the switching is performed by service
providers  manually  in  field,  and  (2)  are  unfamiliar  to  internal  asset  workers,  thus
knowledge  about  failure  modes  and  diagnoses  are  unavailable.  This  situation  is  very
common in today’s distribution networks.

2.3 Risk-based Asset Management as Countermeasure to Asset

Aging

2.3.1 Decision-making Approach at the Tactical Level

Cigre  Working  Group  C1.1  has  emphasized  in  [18]  that centralized  key  decision-
making  is  the  main  approach  of  asset  management  to  balance  performance,  risks  and
expenditures.  Meanwhile,  it  also  noted  that  the  “key”  decisions  focus  on  achieving  of
business goals and keeping strategic directions rather than operational issues.

Within  the  triple  level  AM  model,  the  decision-making  processes  at  the  tactical
level  fulfill  the  above-stated  responsibilities.  In  brief,  the  decision-making  process  is
selecting between a number of technical scenarios which

•
•

are investigated with risk-based approach
requires a cash flow to retain

40

•

are feasible with available resources and complying with regulations

To  prepare  for  the  above  stated  information,  a  process  called  decision  support  is

needed. Regarding each of four aspects, typical decision support activities include:

Step 1: search for technical scenarios of:

a.  failure modes and their probability/frequency of occurrence
b.

technical solutions to tackle the failure modes, such as diagnostic
methods,  maintenance
refurbishment/replacement
strategies,
solutions, etc.

c.  outages  and  resources  (inventory,  personnel)  required  by  each

solution.

Step 2: perform risk assessment (see Section 2.2.4) on:

a.  each failure mode in case that each technical solutions applies
b.  risks accompanied with each technical solution (e.g. risks on safety

/environment due to civil works etc.)

Step 3: predict the cash flow of each technical scenario

a.  preferably  for  the  life  cycle  of  the  component,  normally  up  to

b.

several decades,
through collecting the economic consequences of risks and budgets
of technical solutions.

c.  The  economic  consequences  of  risks  are  calculated  through  the
expected value of the risks, during which the loss of non-economic
business values are converted to the loss of finance. See 2.2.4.
d.  The Net Present Value (NPV) is utilized to sum up a series of cash

flow occurring in the multiple years. [40]

Step 4: check  the  feasibility  and  compliance  of  the  technical  scenario  for  long-
term. In this case, there exists uncertainties that profitability, feasibility and
acceptance  of some  technical  solutions  can  diminish in  long-term.  In  this
case,  extra  budget  should be  invested  according  to  AM  strategy  to  tackle
these uncertainties. See Section 2.4, Chapter 4 and Chapter 5 for details.

41

An  example  of  the  complete  decision  support  model  is  shown  in  [11,  40].  The
above Step 1 is the technical information at the component level in Ben Quak’s model.
Step  2  and  3  are  the  economic  information  1  at  the  network  level.  And  Step  4  is  the
economic information 2 at the corporate level.

It  should  be  emphasized  that:  at  the  component  level  in  [40],  the  technical
information  on  the  probabilities  of  different  failure  modes  can  be  provided  through
condition  assessments,  by  service  providers  at  the  operational  level.  However,  the
technical information will be processed by the asset manager. If condition diagnosis is
unavailable, the failure modes can be derived from failure statistics. See Chapter 3 for
details.

Similarly,  the  corporate  level  in  [40]  is  an  example  of  the  border  between  our
tactical level and strategic levels. Ben Quak’s corporate level is using and implementing,
rather than deciding AM strategies. Decisions on AM strategies require participation of
asset  owner,  especially  specialists  in  fields  such  as  regulatory  &  legal  affairs,
community relationships, network planning, etc.

2.3.2 Asset Management Objectives and Plans

Documentation  of  physical  asset  management  includes  AM  strategies,  objectives
and  plans.  This  section  introduces  AM  objectives  and  plans,  as  the  main  documents
recording AM decisions at the tactical level. The current layout of AM objectives and
plans demonstrate the implementation of risk-based AM strategy as the countermeasure
to asset aging, which is introduced in the Section 2.4.

PAS 55 provides several optional definitions on AM objectives, among which the

one based on asset systems is adopted for this thesis:

An AM objective is a specific and measurable outcome or achievement required of
asset  system(s)  in  order  to  implement  the  asset  management  policy  and  asset
management strategy.

AM  objectives  are  typically  defined  for  a  population  of  asset  systems,  especially
those  of  similar  specifications,  or  those  within  a  regional  network.  The  outcomes  are
primarily  calculated  for  the  population  and  refined  for  individual  asset  system  in  AM
plans, in a layout presented below.

42

An AM plan is a document specifying activities and resources, responsibilities and
timescales  for  implementing  the  asset  management  strategy  and  delivering  the  asset
management  objectives  [16].  The  subject  of  an  AM  plan  is  the  AM  decision  at  the
tactical level, such as

•  Create a new asset system
•  Replace or refurbish an existing asset system.
•  Alter  the  maintenance  strategy  on  an  asset  system,  or  that  on  an  asset

population of same type and specifications.

•  Adjust  the  operation  standards,  especially  the  capacity  rating,  of  an  asset

system

•  Disposal of an existing asset system

The content of an AM plan includes:
•  Technical design part:
o  The

the
technical  design  of  any  new  asset  system(s)  or
modification on the existing asset system(s), especially those with
impacts on the capacity and reliability

o  The  geographical,  geological,  meteorological  and  ecological

environment of the installation
Integration of intelligent devices is an important addition
•  AM objective part, describing the realization of AM objectives:

o

o  The  measurable  risks  before  and  after  implementation  of  the  AM

plan;

o  The investment cash flow;
o  The  gain  of  performance  measured  in  different  “business  values”

and evaluated through conversion to financial profits

o  The immeasurable outcomes, which are constantly associated with
laws,  regulations  and/or

AM  strategies,  and  further  with
stakeholders. The outcomes can be either positive or negative.

•  Resource management part:

o  Readiness of the budget
o  Sustainable  supply  and  sufficient  inventory  of  components  and

spare parts

o  Land and space available/permitted for new installations
o  Sufficient and tolerable outage hours for life cycle activates

43

o  Human  resources  equipped  with  knowledge  of  LC  Activities,

especially for diagnosis and maintenance

o  Permission  from  law/regulator/community  for  LC  Activities,  e.g.
maintenance in densely populated/environment protection areas

•  Service delivery part:

o  The  stages  to  achieve  the  AM  plan,  including  not  only  the
technical programs, but also the political/legal/commercial actions
to acquire the necessary resources

o  The timeline of the stages, in accuracy of calendar years
o  The arrangement and investment of resources in each stage
o  The assignment of responsibility within the corporate, the contract

with external service providers

2.3.3 Risk-based Asset Management Strategy against Asset Aging

AM  strategy  is  a  long-term  optimized  approach  to  management  of  the  assets,
derived  from,  and  consistent  with,  the  organizational  strategic  plan  and  the  asset
management  policy  [16].  In  Section  2.3.1  and  2.2.4,  the  risk-based  decision-making
process can be generalized as the implementation of a (risk-based) AM strategy, which
illustrates that an AM strategy should provide information about:

TABLE 2.4:  CONTENTS OF ASSET MANAGEMENT STRATEGIES ILLUSTRATED IN THE CASE OF RISK-

BASED DECISION-MAKING

Content of an AM strategy
Consistent OSP’s
Rationale of the strategy
Target assets and life cycle phase
Approach to evaluate gain on each OSP
Approach to compare between different OSP’s  Financial evaluation of risk consequences in Table 2.2
Approach to extend analysis for a time frame
Optimization goals
Optional countermeasures

Risk-based AM strategy against asset aging
Business values, namely the five KPI’s in Table 2.2
Physical aging of assets in normal operation conditions
e.g. maintenance on the cables during their medium lives
Risk register, such as in Table 2.3

Net Present Value (NPV) for multiple fiscal years (mid-term)
Maximize the expected performance measured with currency
Feasible AM plans on asset system (s)
Technical scenarios (esp. probably of different failure modes)
after implementation of each AM plans

AM plans prioritized with their profits

AM objectives: expected contribution of decided AM plans to
each business value

Scenario

Implementation guide of the strategy at the
tactical level
Outcome of the strategy reporting to the
strategic level

44

Each  level  of  AM  focuses  on  a  different  aspect  of  an  AM  strategy.  For  the  risk-
based AM strategy, at the operational level, service providers are not only the deliverer
of  AM  plan,  but  also  the  enabler  of  an  advanced  form  of  risk-based  AM  strategy,
namely the “Condition-Based Risk Management” (CBRM) [4]. The CBRM means that
the probability of a failure mode is not estimated from failure history of the population,
but from the condition assessment on the specific components.

The  risk-based  AM  strategy  is  implemented  at  the  tactical  level  through  risk
register and prioritizing AM plans. Meanwhile, the total capital available for investment
on AM plans is determined by the asset owner, rather than the asset manager. Therefore,
it is critical that the asset owner should foresee the long-term trend of asset aging and
adjust the capital investment, so that a sufficient number of AM plans are performed to
avoid  the  “system  aging”  mentioned in  [35] (see  Section  2.2.1). These  responsibilities
imply that the outcome from AM strategies should be analyzed at the strategic level.

2.4 Asset Management at Strategic Level by Asset Owner

AM  strategies  needs  to  be  analyzed  and  decided  by  asset  owner  at  the  strategic
level of AM. As mentioned in Section 2.1, the information flow is still processed in four
stages,  namely  the  performance  evaluation,  the  risks  management,  the  solution  search
and  the  decision-making.  However,  the  strategic  performance  &  risk  information  are
significantly different from those at the tactical level, as Table 2.5 shows.

To tackle the “deep uncertainties”, a widely appreciated risk management method
[50] will be introduced in Chapter 5. The method is firstly applied to classify existing
challenges  and  risks  to  the  AM  of  electricity  transmission  &  distribution  business,
according  to  the  knowledge  on  its  consequence  and  probability.  Then,  for  each  risk
category, risk-based, precaution-based or discourse-based strategies can be developed.

The developed AM strategies need to be analyzed in a systematic way, so that the
interactions between them can be understood. Chapter 6 introduces the “system diagram”
approach,  in  order  to  realize  the  scenario  search  and  decision-making  at  the  strategic
level of AM.

45

making

2.5 Summary

This chapter the triple-level AM model from PAS 55 has been derived in Section
2.1. A number of AM terms, such as life cycle activities, asset item /system /portfolio,
AM  plans  /objectives  /strategies,  risks  and  performances,  have  been  associated  with
today’s AM practice and assigned to certain AM levels in Section 2.2. Based on these
associations and assignments, three results have been observed.

Firstly, the risk-based decision-making is performed at the tactical level of AM. It
implements the risk-based AM strategy. This strategy, at the strategic level, is used as
the  countermeasure  for  the  asset  aging.  Furthermore,  tackling  asset  aging  is  a  critical
OSP for TSO/DSO’s. Chapter 3 and 4 will further establish a reliability data model as a
tool to support this strategy.

46

Secondly, the KPI’s quantified in the risk matrix only compose one part of OSP’s
for  future  TSO/DSO’s.  Consequently,  when  stakeholder  requirements  other  than
reliability present themselves, AM strategies other than the risk-based maintenance are
likely  to  appear.  Chapter  4  will  extend  this  conclusion  through  introducing  emerging
OSP’s and strategies.

Thirdly,  the  developments and  amendments  of  AM  strategies is the  responsibility
of  the  strategic  level  of  AM,  which  is  applied  in  an  ad  hoc  way  in  very  few  utility
companies. The  methodology  to  develop  AM  strategies is  different from  the  decision-
making  at  the  tactical  level,  as  Table  2.5  has  shown.  Chapter  5  will  provide  asset
manager with a concrete approach to fulfill requirements in Table 2.5.

Conclusion

Risk  management  should  not  be  considered  as  the  only  AM  strategy  in  future
TSO/DSO’s.  Though  the  risk-based  approach  is  effective  and  efficient  at  the  tactical
level  of  AM,  other  asset  management  strategies  need  to  be  considered  at  the  strategic
level of AM. This extension at the strategic level is critical for TSO/DSO’s, when they
have OSP’s other than preventing asset aging.

47

48

Chapter 3 Failure and Life Data Analysis

on Asset Population

This chapter will be developed within the framework of today’s asset management,
regarding  a  widely  recognized  function  of  an  asset  manager  at  the  tactical  level:
controlling  of  the  technical  risks  originating  from  the  O&M  on  physical  assets.  In
Chapter  5,  such  risks  will  be  categorized  as  “normal  risks”  and  distinguished  from
“strategic risks”.

According to the review on maintenance management in Section 1.2.1, at least one
type  of  (normal)  risk  has  been  widely  investigated  before  the  appearance  of  asset
management: the failures. Failures of asset groups of different scales can have not only
technical,  but  also  economical  and  societal  consequences  to  the  business.  As
countermeasures  to  these  risks,  life  cycle  activities,  especially  maintenance,  are
performed to control the occurring frequency of failures on physical assets.

Facing an entire asset portfolio, the first concern of the asset manager about these
risks, is to obtain an overview on asset failures. This is because historical failure records
of asset populations have traditionally been recorded by the maintenance crew. When a
risk-based regime is firstly established for asset management, failure statistics are likely
to  be  more  available  than  other  data  sources  such  as  diagnostic  data  from  individual
assets. Therefore, statistical analysis on these failure records can identify the most risky
part  of  the  asset  population  during  the  initial  stage.  Afterwards,  additional  techniques
and tools can be used on the risks of certain subpopulations, so that the performance of
asset portfolio can be improved.

Based  on  the  above  reasoning,  this  chapter  will  focus  on  the  applications  of

statistical techniques in analyzing asset failure data.

Section  3.1  summarizes  the  generic  forms  of  failure  and  life  data.  This  summary
reveals that life data can have various degrees of completeness. Based on the particular
completeness  degree,  the  applicability  of  different  probabilistic  failure  models  in  each
case can be determined. Section 3.2 briefly reviews the probabilistic failure models, as

49

well as the  existing  approaches of failure and/or  life data  analysis.  More details  about
these approaches can be found in Appendix A and B. The introduction also provides a
theoretical background for our contributions to life data analysis in Section 3.3 and 3.4.

In Section 3.3, a new life data analysis approach is developed in order to extend the
applicability of life data analysis to the situation when installation data is partly missing,
especially  for  cables  and  its  accessories.  In  Section  3.4,  the  investigated  object  will
change  from  the  life  data  of  field  operating  components  to  that  of  laboratory  tested
specimens.  For  illustration,  we  apply  the  method  we  developed  on  a  case  of  winding
insulation of a transformer.

After  confirming  the  feasibility  of  the  newly  developed  statistical  approaches,
Section  3.5  discusses  the  required conditions to apply  these  approaches  and  forms  the
conclusion about their role in AM decision-making. In Chapter 4, this conclusion will be
extended to a complete decision-making process at the tactical level of AM.

3.1 Failure and Life Data

This section will answer two questions:
(1)  What is the difference between failure data and life data?
(2)  Which parameters can be used to summarize a collection of failure data or life

data?

Their  answers  lay  the  foundation  for  statistical  analysis  on  failure  and  life  data  from
Section 3.3.

Section  3.1.1  demonstrates  the  format  of  failure  data,  especially  the  necessary
content of a complete failure record. In section 3.1.2, two possible forms to interpret the
failure records, namely the failure data and life data, are defined. Meanwhile, the data
quality issues commonly seen in life data are, firstly, classified in terms of completeness
and censoring, and, secondly, rated with different degrees.

Afterwards, in Section 3.2, probabilistic models to analyze failure and life data are
summarized  through  describing  their  linkage  in  mathematical  expressions.  The
summarized models are introduced with more details in Appendix A and B.

50

3.1.1 Raw Failure Data

A  failure  is  defined  as  the  situation  where  a  certain  object  cannot  function  under
stated  conditions  for  a  specified  period  of  time.  Within  asset  portfolios,  a  failure  can
occur  at  many  different  levels,  such  material,  subcomponent,  component/asset,  asset
system  (including  secondary  equipment)  and  asset  fleet/portfolio.  When  failures  at  a
lower level lead to a failure at a higher level, the process is called a failure mechanism
or failure mode.

The raw format of failure and life data is a list of failure events. A complete record

on a failure event should include:

•  The  moment  of failure occurrence,  which  can  be further transformed  into

life data, as the next section will describe.

•  The object: In laboratories, this is simply a specimen or a tested object. In
the  field,  the  object  is  frequently  identified  with  an  asset  tag  on  each
individual asset/asset system.

Note: It is highly recommended that all objects are comparable regarding their

specifications,  such  as  rating,  material  and  construction.  Specifically,  for

components with variable sizes, e.g. cables, an object can be defined as a unit

size of it, e.g. 1 km of cable.

•  The  cause:  Failure  mechanism  (for  specimen  and  subcomponents)  or

failure mode (for asset systems).

Failures  resulting  from  the  causes  listed  below  are  treated  as  a  survival  or

suspended in statistics.
o  Failures caused by mechanisms which no longer exist in the system (e.g.
caused by a defected design which have been removed from asset fleet)

o  Failures caused by non-physical events (e.g. third-party damage)
Failures  at  high  level  can  initiate  from  those  at  lower  level  which  can  be

originally rooted in material aging. Root cause analysis, the task to find out the

initiations and roots, is knowledge-demanding.

•  The consequences (in the field): The technical consequences of the failure.
include  power  quality  problems,

technical  consequences

Examples  of

blackouts,  accidents,  etc.  As  mentioned  in  Section  2.2.4,  these  technical

consequences are further linked with KPI’s.

51

•  The countermeasure (in the field): The action that the service provider has
taken  proactively  to  prevent  the  upcoming  failure  and/or  reactively  to
recover from the failure.

If  a  failure  is  detected  just-in-time  and  just-on-site  before  its  occurrence,

preventive actions can be taken to avoid serious consequences of an occurred

failure.  This  case  should  be  treated  in  two  different  ways  regarding  two

different purposes:
o  For life prediction, a nearly-occurred failure is treated as a failure. Since
the  condition  is  so  close  to  breakdown,  it  is  recommended  to  treat  a

prevented failure as a failure which will occur within a certain period after

the countermeasure, as [56] did for power transformers.

o  For root cause analysis and failure mode analysis, a prevented failure is in
fact different from an occurred one. When a failure is prevented, its root

cause can be more easily located, and its failure mechanism can be more

precisely defined.

o  For performance analysis, the period between detection and occurrence is
critical. In general, earlier detection  means sufficient  time to prepare for

preventive measures (e.g. a repair), which will further reduce the costs.

This  chapter  will  only  focus  on  the  “moment”  and  “object”  of  failure,  while  the
“cause” will play a role in selecting relevant failure data in the case study. In Chapter 4,
the “cause”, the “consequences” and “countermeasure” will be respectively described as
“failure  mechanisms”,  “failure  modes”  and  “failure  scenarios”  in  the  reliability  data
model.

3.1.2 From Failure data to Life Data

From the raw format of failure records, two types of parameters can be derived as

the conclusion after statistical analysis. They are:

•  Failure  frequency:  Number  of  failures  occurred  within  a  population  of
objects in the period, caused by certain mechanisms. It is an observation of
failure rate, but the failure frequency should be distinguished from failure
rate. See the next section for details.

•  Lifetime  or  time-to-failure  (TTF):  TTF  means  the  period  between

installation and first (normally the only) failure of an object.

52

Time-between-failures  (TBF):  in  contrast  to  TTF,  TBF  means  the  period
between two failures of an object.

TTF and TBF can be distinguished from each other using the below aspects:
o  Normally, TTF is normally applied to an object with low maintainability
such  as  materials,  specimens  and  subcomponents.  They  need  to  be

removed or renewed after failure. Repairs are not applicable. In contrast,

TBF  is  applied  to  objects  with  high  maintainability,  such  as  systems.  In

other words, a system can recover despite failures of its components.
o  The “cause” of a failure mentioned in Section 3.1.1 is critical. For TTF, it
means  the  failure  mechanism.  For  TBF,  it  means  the  failure  modes,  in

which the component responsible for the failure should be specified.
o  TTF  and  TBF  can  both  be  applied  to  components,  with  different
meanings. TTF refers to “major failures” (noted as MF in capital letters)

which  are  not  repairable.  Normally  the  component  should  be  replaced

after  MF.  In  contrast,  “minor  failures”  on  components  are  repairable

(noted as mf in lower-case) through preventive, scheduled maintenance.
o  The  terms  major  failures  and  minor  failures  can  also  be  applied  in  asset
systems.  Normally,  a  MF  interrupts  a  system,  while  a  mf  only  requires

some  maintenance.  For  example,  a  MF  of  a  component  which  has  a

redundant backup can be regarded as a mf.

Additionally, calculation of TTF or TBF follows the follwoing rules:
o  As mentioned in “the countermeasure”, prevented failures are considered

as if they were occurred failures in life statistics.

o  Strictly speaking, TTF and TBF do not include the time-to-repair (TTR).
Usually  in  electricity  networks,  the  TTR  is  very  small  compared  to

TTF/TBF. Therefore, the TTR is not considered.

The  time  periods  associated  with TTF  and TBF should  not  be confused  with  calendar
time/year.  For  clarity,  in  this  thesis,  a  capital  T  always  refers  to  TTF/TBF  variables,
including the service/operating life of an object before its breakdown (i.e. the censored
lifetimes  defined  below).  In  contrast,  the  lowercase  t  can  be  used  to  note  calendar
time/year.

In  order  to  convert  time  series  of  failure  events  to  TTF,  the  following  extra

information is required:

53

•  The  population:  the  total  number  of  investigated  objects,  from  which  the

failure events have been collected from.

When  the  investigated  objects  are  significantly  different  in  their  physical  sizes,  size
information should also be included in population. (e.g. cables of different length)

•  The  installation/deployment:  the  calendar  date/time  at  which  each  object

within the population started to operate/be stressed.

figure from [35]) into failure frequency.

54

Figure  3.1  demonstrates  this  conversion  process.  The  raw  failure  data  normally
appears  as  “Format  1”:  installations  and  expected  failures/replacements  of  a  type  of
component distribute on the calendar. We use a figure from [35] as Format 1 to show
that  these  two  distributions  often  have  similar  shapes  in  which  the  peak  implies  a
replacement  wave. Then,  in  “Format  2”, failure frequency  can  be  calculated for  every
year.

Meanwhile,  for  those  failed  or  surviving  components  with  their  installation  time
recorded,  their  TTF  can  be  calculated  in  “Format  2”  of  Figure  3.2.  Accordingly,  in
“Format  3”,  the  life  data  of  asset  population,  as  a  sample  pool,  are  fitted  into  a
probabilistic model. As a result, the probabilistic distribution of lifetime is estimated as
the red curve shows in “Format 3”.

Figure 3.2:  Demonstration of the process to model failure data in lifetime models, in which the

failures with their installation time unknown are excluded.

Additionally,  for  both  laboratory  and  field  data,  the  moment  of  failure  may  be
observed or recorded with “data censoring”, i.e. the exact moment of failure is unknown,
but a possible range of the moment of failure can be deduced from relevant information
that actually is available.

•  Right  censoring:  the  object  is  surviving  in  the  last  inspection.  When  the
installation/deployment  time  is  available, it  means  the  TTF  is  longer  than
the duration from its deployment to the last inspection.

Right  censoring  can  be  indicated  with  an  arrow  pointing  to  the  positive

direction of time axis (which is normally to the right). Right censoring is very

common for completeness degree

3

, when a large number of components are

currently running in the network, as the Figure 3.1 shows at the right edge of
blue rectangle.

⟨

⟩

•  Left  censoring:  the  object  has  failed  before  an  observation,  but  the  exact
moment of failure is unknown. Using the observation there is a maximum
estimation for TTF.

Left censoring can be indicated with an arrow pointing to the negative direction

of  time  axis  (which  is  normally  to  the  left).  Left  censoring  is  common  for

subcomponents,  redundant  system  and  protection  system.  Failure  of  these

56

objects will not interrupt operation immediately. Otherwise, the exact moment
of failure would have been clear.

•

Interval  censoring:  when  the  failure  is  both  left-censored  and  right-
censored.

The  interval  is  the  period  between  two  arrows.  A  typical  case  of  interval  for

failure data is the period between two inspections.

For  field  data,  interval  censoring  can  also  be  applied  on  installation

/deployment time due to missing historical records. As a result, the lifetime is
also interval censored, as Figure 3.1 shows the third row of the green box.

In summary, this section gives recommendations for the data format in which asset
managers  should  record  the  failures.  The  current  raw  failure  data  format  can  be
converted  to  life  data,  but  this  conversion  is  frequently  encountering  data  quality
problems such as completeness and censoring.

The completeness of existing failure data presents a significant challenge to failure
and  life  data  analysts.  It  limits  the  application  of  statistical  models  and  the  quality  of
their output. The following two sections will discuss these models.

Compared  with  completeness,  censoring  will  not  change  the  analysis  procedure.
However,  information  lost  in  censoring  will  lead  to  wide  confidence  intervals  of
statistical estimations and will thus reduce the quality of analysis.

3.2 Probabilistic Models for Failure and Life Data – an Overview

Reliability  engineers  define  a  number  of  mathematical  functions  to  describe  the
uncertain behavior of failures. We will review these functions below before we start the
analysis of the failure and life data in the remaining part of this chapter. Please note that
the definitions are applied to non-repairable systems.

•  Reliability R(T): is the probability that an object survives until the moment T.

•  Unreliability F(T): is the probability that an object fails before the moment T. It is
also  the  cumulative  distribution  function  (CDF)  of  lifetime,  which  is  commonly

noted as F.
R(T) and F(T) are simply related to each other via R(T) + F(T) = 1

Obviously,  these  functions  are  all  related  to  the  service  life  T.  When  discussing
these parameters, the object of failure needs to be specified. Without doing so, problems
such as “size enlargement effect” in Section 3.4.4 will be left unnoticed.

Figure 3.2 gives an overview of the probabilistic models for failure and life data.
The  models  are  grouped  in  blocks  and  linked  together.  The  bold  lines  label  the
parameters  shared  by  two  models.  The  arrow  lines  mean  that  one  model  contains
parameters in addition to the other model.

The failure rate models and the life models in Figure 3.3 have been widely applied
in managing reliability of high-voltage components in the last decade [57]. Appendix A
and B review essential properties and applications of these models. From the review, the
following can be learned.

(1)  Determine the form of the Weibull distribution into which the life data will
be fitted. Appendix B.1 introduces the two parameter Weibull distribution
to be used in Section 3.3 and 3.4.

(2)  Estimate the parameters of Weibull distribution. Appendix B.3 introduces
the maximum likelihood method for complete life data, while Section 3.3
will develop a new method for incomplete data.

(3)  Calculate the failure frequency for a given calendar year from the estimated

(4)  (Optional) Calculate the confidence intervals for the estimated parameters,
failure  frequencies  or  a  percentile  of  life.  Appendix  B.5  introduces  the
Fisher information matrix method for parameters. Section 3.4.4 will use the
Fisher method to calculate the percentile of life.

In equation (3.1):
t is the calendar year ranging from tMIN to tNOW.
tNOW is the calendar year for which the number of failures will be estimated;
tMIN is the calendar year since which the asset population started to be installed;
Ninstall(t) is the number of components installed in calendar year t and operating in tNOW;
n(t) is the (expected or estimated) number of failures in t.
h(T) is the hazard rate function. When the life distribution is estimated, the hazard rate
function  h(T)  will  be  determined.  Appendix  B.5  specifies  the  h(T)  for  Weibull
distribution.
The * between N and h is the discrete convolution operator.

3.3 Incomplete Life Data Analysis

3.3.1 Definition of the Problem: Partly Missing Failure or Installation Data

In  normal  life  data  analysis,  we  typically  assume  that  the  TTF’s  are  independent
and identically distributed. This assumption will also be applied in our incomplete life
data analysis. Additionally, in normal life data analysis, fitness tests will be applied to
the collected TTF’s to select a suitable probability distribution. Since the main concern
of this section is not the fitness tests but finding a fitting method, we will avoid this step
and  assume  that  the  TTF’s  follow  the  two  parameter  (2-p)  Weibull  distribution  (See
Appendix B.1).

Given  that  the  TTF’s  are  independent  and  identically  distributed  in  2-p  Weibull,
maximum  likelihood  estimation  (MLE)  is  widely  applied  to  estimate  Weibull

60

parameters, as Appendix B.3 summarizes. The basic MLE should be applied on the life
,  while  the  time-constant  failure  rate  model  is

as completeness degree

Failure data are often collected in a period much shorter than asset life cycle. The
installation  time  of  assets  failed  in  this  period  or  survived  after  this  period  is  known.
However, the TTF of a significant proportion of in-service assets were missing, because
they failed before this period.

2.5

One solution to apply the basic MLE on this

 degree is simply neglecting the
missing data, as performed in [58]. Obviously, the observed data do not have the same
distribution with the complete data, because the neglected failures belong to the smaller
part  of  the  real  complete  TTF  data  pool.  As  a  result,  the  mean  and  deviation  of  the
observed TTF’s will be respectively larger and smaller than their real values. In terms of
the Weibull model, the MLE of η and β will be respectively biased to the upper side and
lower side. Based on these parameters, the hazard rate will be underestimated for young
assets, but overestimated for old assets. In this situation, it is difficult to decide whether
the failure frequency predicted from equation (3.1) is underestimated or overestimated.

⟩

There  are  two  alternative  solutions  which  can  take  the  missing  data  into
consideration. The first solution is to apply MLE on the conditional distribution of the
observed  data  given  how  the  data  is  missed.  However,  in  our  life  data  analysis,  the
analytical form of this conditional distribution is very complicated. For example, if the
failure data is observed since calendar year 2000, the TTF’s of the assets installed at the
calendar year CY follows a Weibull distribution with its left tail cut from 0 to 2000-CY.
And the PDF of all observed TTF’s will be a weighted sum of the PDF’s of tens of such
Weibull distributions with different CY’s. Consequently, the likelihood function of the
observed TTF’s will be very difficult to calculate.

The  second  solution  considers  the  contribution  of  missing  data  to  the  likelihood
function.  Instead  of  performing  MLE  only  once,  we  will  build  an  algorithm  which
iteratively approximates the missing part of likelihood function and performs the MLE.
In the next section, the general principle of this algorithm will be introduced.

61

3.3.2 General Principles of Our Iterative Approximation & Maximization

Algorithm
Our method was inspired by the Expectation Maximization (EM) algorithm, which
is  well-known  for  dealing  with  missing  data  in  statistics  [59].  This  section  will  firstly
describe  the  theoretical  EM  solution  to  our  problem,  and  then  introduce  our  solution,
named Iterative Approximation & Maximization (I-AM) method.

The EM method iterates the E-step and the M-step below, until the estimated θ(n)
converges. The converged value of θ(n)  is the EM estimation of θ. The convergence of
parameters within EM iterations is theoretically proven in [60].

•  E-step:

Calculate equation (3.6), which is the expected value of the log likelihood
function Λ in equation (3.5), with respect to the conditional distribution of
Tmis, given Tobs under the Weibull parameters estimated in the previous M-

62

step, noted as θ(n). In the first iteration, the θ(1) is set to any possible values
of θ.

(3.9)

In  our  cases  of  incomplete  life  data  analysis,  the  log  likelihood  function  of  2-p
Weibull distribution does not have the form in which the H function in equation (3.9)
can be analytically calculated. Therefore, we replace the E-step with an approximation
step (A-step). In our A-step, the conditional distribution of Tmis with respect to Tobs and
θ(n) will be approximated with a generated data array Tmis. Accordingly, our method is
named as Iterative Approximation and Maximization method (I-AM) method.

Similar  to  the  EM  method,  our  I-AM  method  iterates  the  A  step  and  the  M  step
below,  until  the  estimated  θ(n)  converges.  The  converged  value  of  θ(n)  is  the  EM
estimation of θ.

•  M-step:

Maximize  the  complete  likelihood  function  in  equation  (3.5)  and  get  an
MLE  of  θ.  Note  the  θ  estimated  from  the  nth  iteration  as  θ(n).  The  Tmis  is
obtained from the A-step in the last iteration. Set Tmis as a null vector in the
first iteration.

•  A-step:

63

Generate  a  Tmis.  The  length  of  Tmis  is  calculated  from  that  of  Tobs  with
respect  to  θ(n),  while  the  values  of  Tmis  are  satisfy  the  conditional
distribution with respect to θ(n) and Tobs.

This is the general form of our I-AM algorithm. In the following sections, the A-
step will be further specified according to different ways that failure and/or life data is
missing.

3.3.3 I-AM in Incomplete Life Data Analysis: Missing Life Data

This  section  will  simulate  a  simple  life  data  set  of  completeness  degree
.
Subsequently, we will develop our A- and M- step to demonstrate the effectiveness of
our I-AM method.

2.5

⟨

⟩

The life data are simulated as follows:
For similarity, the calendar year of installation are not simulated. In each simulation,
1000 TTF data are generated from a Weibull distribution with predetermined η and β.
All  TTF’s  below  a  predetermined  threshold  TTH  years  are  removed.  The  data  and
knowledge used by the I-AM algorithm are: kobs life data array Tobs below the threshold
TTH, as well as the TTH value.

Meanwhile, we develop the following I-AM loop:

•  M-step

Get  MLE  [η(n)  ,  β(n)]  from  data  array  T=[Tmis,Tobs].  The  Tmis  is  obtained
from the A2-step in the last iteration. Set Tmis as a null vector in the first
iteration.

•  A1-step

•  The iteration stops when [η(n) , β(n)] stabilizes to a range smaller than 1% of

In  both  A-steps,  F(T)  and  R(T)  are  the  unreliability  function  (or  CDF)  and
reliability  function  of  the  Weibull  distribution  of  parameter  [η(n)  ,  β(n)],  and  F-1  is  the
inverse unreliability function (inverse CDF).

Before applying an I-AM algorithm, the convergence of parameter estimations [η(n) ,
β(n)]  should  always  be  examined.  We  ran  the  above  I-AM  program  on  tens  of
combinations of η ranging from 20 to 80 (years), β ranging from 1 to 5, and TTH ranging
from 30% to 70% reliable life. An empirical conclusion was reached that less than 100
iterations were needed for the convergence.

Then, to demonstrate the effect of I-AM, we fixed η to 50 (years), β to 2.5 and TTH
=  40  (years).  These  values  are  commonly  seen  in  high  voltage  assets  [61,  62].  The
complete simulation process, including generating TTF data and iterating the loop, has
been repeated 1000 times.

Specifically, the TTF data are generated with the MATLAB function “wblrnd” in
this  section  and  Section  3.3.4.  According  to  the  documentation  of  MATLAB,  the
function  uses  the  method  described  in  [63]  to  generate  independent  and  identically
distributed data series.

Figure 3.4 shows the histogram of different type of estimations. The frequency is
collected from 1000 simulations. The grey bars with red edges are from of the MLE’s of
complete  data  pools.  It  reflects  the  “real”  Weibull  parameters  that  I-AM  should
converge to. The bars with green dashed edges are the outputs from the I-AM program
after its convergence. The bars with blue edges are the MLE’s from the observed data.
They neglect missing data, as mentioned in 3.3.1.

From Figure 3.4, we conclude that our I-AM program has reached its goal. Above
all, the I-AM estimator is much less biased than the MLE from the observed data. The I-
AM estimations are distributed around our predetermined parameters η = 50 and β = 2.5,
as well as the MLE from the complete data in Figure 3.4. The larger deviation of I-AM

65

estimations can be explained by the fact that the sample size of incomplete data is only
56%  of  that  of  the  complete  one.  Additionally,  the  simulation  empirically  proves  the
effectiveness  of  our  I-AM  program,  including  the  validity  of  using  medium  ranks  to
calculate the approximate missing data in our A2-step.

Finally, the bias of I-AM estimations will be tested with different combinations of β
and TTH. 1000 data pools will be generated for each combination of β and TTH, and each
data pool contains 1000 data points. The η and β estimated with I-AM from incomplete
data  will  be  compared  to  those  estimated  with  MLE  from  complete  data.  Their

66

differences  are  regarded  as  the  errors  of  I-AM.  Table  3.2  shows  the  average  and
standard deviations of these errors.

Note 2: If the bias is positive, it means that the I-AM estimation from incomplete data is larger
