From Table 3.2, empirical conclusions can be formed regarding the applicability of

I-AM.

Firstly,  the  I-AM  requires  much  more  iterations  to  converge,  if  the  proportion  of
missing data is large. In our simulation, the number of iterations needed for convergence
is increased from around 40 to around 140, when the proportion of missing data increase
from 45% to 63%.

Secondly, the relative error of β is also mainly decided by the proportion of missing
data.  The  2nd  and  7th  row  in  Table  3.2  show  this  relationship.  In  the  2nd,  3rd  and  5th
column,  the  relative  error  and  the  proportion  of  missing  data  are  respectively  5%  and
63%, while in the 4th and 6th column they are respectively 2.5% and 45%.

Thirdly,  the  error  of  η  depends  on  both  β  and  TTH.  The  standard  deviation  of  the
error of η reach a considerable number of 19, over 40% of η=50. This implies that the
small β=0.5 can create significant error of the estimation on η. This complies with the
experience that the life data collected from an aged population does not reflect the infant
mortality (when β <1).

67

In  summary,  our  simulations  show  that  our  I-AM  can  effectively  reduce  the  bias
caused  by  missing  data.  However,  when  the  short  TTF’s  are  missing,  the  infant
mortality  can  hardly  be  studied  with  our  I-AM  method.  In  addition,  the  proportion  of
missing  data  plays  an  important  role  our  I-AM  method:  it  reduces  the  speed  of
convergence and increases the errors of both η and β.

3.3.4 I-AM in Incomplete Life Data Analysis: Missing Installation and Failure

Data
In  last  section,  our  I-AM  method  is  applied  to  a  simplified  case  of  life  data  with
completeness  degree
.  The  application  shows  the  effectiveness  of  I-AM  itself,  as
well as the validity of using medium ranks in the approximate step (A2-step in the last
section).

2.5

⟩

⟨

This section  aims  to adapt  I-AM  to real life  data.  As  introduced  in  Section  3.1.2,
life data is usually converted from failure and installation data indexed by calendar years.
In reality, asset information is usually missing for a calendar period, because of business
policies such as mergers of companies, change of contractors, etc. Therefore, for AM, it
is more practical to develop I-AM estimators for “missing failure data and installation
data in certain calendar years”, rather than for “missing life data below a certain age”.

The Problem of Incomplete Data from Cable Joints

The incomplete data tackled in this section comes from the cable joints (referred as
“components”  below)  operating  in  a  distribution  network.  The  detailed  technical
background is described in [64]. The most significant facts for this life data analysis are:

(1)  The analysis was performed in 2010.
(2)  The  investigated  category  of  components  started  to  be  introduced  into  a
distribution network during the 1950’s. The installation continues until the
investigation.

(3)  All  failures  that  have  occurred  since  2004  have  been  recorded.  However,
the  installation  year  data  before  2004  is  seriously  lacking.  For  failed
components, their TTF can only be judged from their types, configurations
and  service  provider  information.  In  other  words,  all  TTF’s  available  are
interval data. These intervals are: 0 - 1,1 - 2, 2- 3, 3 - 4, 4 - 5, 6 - 10, 10 -
20, 20 - 40 and 40 - 50 years.

(4)  The  age  distribution  of  components  surviving  at  2010  is  known.  In  other

words, the left censoring (suspended) data is complete.

68

(5)  No component in failure or suspended data is older than 50 years.

As mentioned in Section 3.3.1, there is always a “natural” solution which neglects
the components failed before 2004. Outputs from this “natural” solution, namely the life
distribution  estimated  with  ML  from  the  observed  (incl.  suspended)  TTF  data,  will
create the conflict below.

When a failure analyst uses this Weibull distribution and the recorded installation
data  to  calculate  the  failure  frequencies  between  2004  and  2009,  the  calculated
“expected” failure frequencies will differ significantly from the observed number.

This  reduces  the  credibility  of  the  failure  frequency  predicted  for  the  upcoming
years.  This  conflict  will  be  solved  by  the  I-AM  algorithm  we  developed,  in  A3-step
particularly.

In  next  subsections,  the  algorithm  which  solves  the  above  problem  will  be
completely  described.  Those  who  are  not  interested  in  reproducing  the  simulation  can
skip these two subsections and continue at the subsection entitled “Testing the I-AM in
simulated data” in page 77.

Notations Used in the I-AM Algorithm

Both  the  problem  stated  above  and  the  I-AM  algorithm  contains  a  number  of

variables. They are noted as below:

•  CY: Calendar Year

o  CY0:

The  calendar  year  before  the first  year  of  installation.  Since

the  components  failed  in  2004  can  be  in  maximum  50  years  old  and

earliest installed in 1954, CY0=1953.

o  CY1:
o  CY2:

The calendar year when failures record starts. CY1=2004.

The calendar year when failures record stops. CY2=2009.

•

IY: Relative Year of Installation. Equals to CY - CY0.
o  We note IY1=CY1-CY0, IY2=CY2-CY0.
•  FY: Relative Year of Failure record. Equals to CY - CY1+1.

o  FY ranges from 1 to CY2-CY1+1

•  T:  LifeTime/age or TTF (if failed) of components. T is always a integer.

69

o  TI: Lifetime interval, note TIL,i and TIU,i respectively as lower and upper

bound of the ith interval.

o  Tmax:

The maximum lifetime in current record. Tmax =50= IY1-1.

•
IN(IY):
•  FP(T):

row vector

Number of components Installed in year IY. IN is a column vector.

Probability that a component Fails between age T-1 and age T. FP is a

o  Please  note  FP  is  not  the  failure  rate,  but  the  probability  that  lifetime  is

between T-1 and T, or CDF(T)-CDF(T-1).

Vectors and matrices are in bold, such as like IN and FP. For them, a number of

matrix operators are introduced:

o

IN(IY1:IY2) is the column vector of numbers of components installed from
CY0+ IY1 to CY0+ IY2. (IY1<IY2)

o  FP(T1:T2)  is  a  row  vector  of  probabilities  that  a  component  fails  at  ages

o

ranging from T1 to T2 years.
If  we  flip  of  FP(T1:T2)  left  to  the  right,  we  note  the  result  as  FP(T2:T1)
(T1<T2).  This  operation  is  frequently  used  when  multiply  row  vector  FP
with  column  vector  IN  to  estimate  failure  frequency,  as  equation  (3.1)

did.

o  Matrix transverse will also be used. Transverse of A is noted as AT.
•  FF(FY, i):  Frequency (number) of Failures that (1) occur, or should occur in year
FY, and (2) its TTF falling into the ith interval of TTF. FF is a matrix with its rows
indexing FY and columns indexing TTF intervals.

•  Subscripts:

o  Afail:
CY2.
o  Asus:
o  Aobs:

variable  A  is  describing  components  failed  between  CY1  to

variable A is describing components surviving at CY2.

variable  A  is  derived  from  the  observed  data,  therefore  it  is

describing components failed after CY1 (including the surviving ones).

o  Amis:
o  Aall:

variable A is describing components failed before CY1.

variable A is describing all components

•  Variables use locally in a single A-step will be subscripted with the step index. The
lower and upper bounds on a non-vector variable  used in  a single  A-step  will be

further noted with L and respectively. For example, the lower and upper bound of

CY used in A1-step will be noted as CYA1,L and CYA1,U.

•  Superscript “(n)” means the variable is from the result of the nth iteration.

70

Based  on
mathematically as:

the  notation,

the  problem  of  cable

joints  can  be

interpreted

•  Given  6  years  of interval-censored  failure  record in matrix FFobs,  and the
number of installations in history which survived after CY2=2009 in matrix
INsus, estimate the life distribution;

•  Assuming  that  the  lifetimes  are  mutual  independent  and  identically

distributed in 2-p Weibull;

•  Estimate Weibull parameters of maximum likelihood.

The I-AM Algorithm

The  solution  to  the  above  problem  is  to  iterate  the  below  I-AM  iteration  loop,

which contains an M-step, four A-steps and three after- steps.

M-step for the first iteration

Set initial values [η(1) , β(1)]. The result is not sensitive to these initial values. In our
calculation,  we  neglect  the  missing  data,  and  input  FFobs  and  INsus  into  a  likelihood
function  developed  for  censoring  data[65].  After  maximizing  this  likelihood  function,
the [η(1) , β(1)] is gotten.

M-step for the other iterations

Get MLE [η(n) , β(n)] from data array T=[Tmis, Tfail, Tsus]. The Tmis is obtained from

the A-step in the last iteration.

After-M-step

Update FP

•  FP(T)=  F(T)-F(T-1),  where  F  is  the  CDF  of  the  Weibull  distribution  with

parameter [η(n) , β(n)] estimated from the M-step. T=1,…,CY2

A1-step

Purpose: Estimate the installation year for each failed component. Count them in a

number-of-installation vector INfail.

Procedure: Before the step, clear the INfail to 0. Then do the below sub-steps for all

FFobs (FY,i) in FFobs.

71

•  FFobs(FY,  i)  is  the  failure  frequency  at  CY=CY2+FY  -1.  Therefore  these
failed components are possible to be installed from IYA1,L=IY2+FY-TIU,i -1
to IYA1,U=IY2+FY-TIL,i-1.

•  Find  out  failure  probability  FP(TIL,i  :  TIU,i).  Flip  this  row  factor  as

FPA1=FP(TIU,I : TIL,i),.

•  Find out the total number of installation in history INA1=INall(IYA1,L: IYA1,U)
from the last iteration. For the first iteration, set all elements of INA1 to 1.

•  Get the element-by-element multiplication product of FPA1

T and INA1, note
as WA1. And further get the value of matrix multiplication product S=FPA1∙
WA1.

•  Set INfail,FY,I = WA1* FFobs (FY, i)/S.
•  Add INfail,FY,i to INfail(IYA1,L:IYA1,U).

Note 1: The above process uses a priori distribution of number of installation INA1. The
final result of I-AM is not sensitive selection of this estimation. If the elements of INall
do not vary much, INA1 can be set directly to 1.
Note 2: Keep the non-integers in INfail for A3-step.

After-A1-step

Get INobs, which is the (estimated) installation history of the components that fails

after CY1.

•

INobs=INfail+INsus

A2-step

Purpose:  Calculate  the  FFexp,  which  describes  the  failure  frequencies  expected

from the INobs and [η(n) , β(n)].

Procedure: Given INobs, the (observed) components installed from IYA2,L=IY2+FY-
TIU,i -1 to IYA2,U=IY2+FY-TIL,i  -1 are possible to cause failures at year CY=CY2+FY-1,
i.e. contribute to FFexp(FY, i).

•  Therefore,  FFexp  can  be  calculated  through  repeating  the  below  sub-steps

for all FY, and i ranging from 6 to 9.

•  Firstly, acquire failure probability vector FP(TIL,i : TIU,i);
•  Then, flip it into FP(TIU,i : TIL,i)

72

•  Meanwhile,  acquire  the  relevant  observed  number  of  installation  vector

INobs(IYA2,L: IYA2,U)

•  Finally, apply equation (3.1) in a matrix multiplication:
FFexp(FY, i)= FP(TIU,i : TIL,i) ∙ INobs(IYA2,L: IYA2,U).

Note 1: The range of i is based on one fact: Components failed with TTF longer than 6
years are installed before CY1=2004. Therefore TTF data in the intervals 6-10, 11-20,
21-40 and 41-50 are relevant to the missing installation population.
Note 2: A1-step is “distributing” the occurred failures into installation history. From
each failure in FFobs , exactly one installation will be added to the sum of INfail and
INobs. In contrast, A2-step is “expecting” the failure occurrence from (observed)
installation history. Each installation in INobs will only increase in FFexp for much-less-
than 1 expected failures, because the probability FP has been multiplied.

After-A2-step

Get  FFmis,  which  is  the  (estimated)  failure  frequencies  expected  from  a  missing
installed  population.  This  missing  population  probably  failed  before  CY1,  and  will  be
estimated in A3-step.

•  FFmis = FFobs - FFexp

A3-step

Purpose: Find out a non-negative INmis from which the expected failure frequencies

between CY1 and CY2 is FFmis.

Procedure:  INmis    is  a  IY2=56  by  1  column  vector  to  comply  with  INobs.
Nevertheless,  all  installations  after  CY1  will  be  either  failed  or  suspended  at  CY2,
therefore, included in INfail or INsus. Therefore INmis(IY1:IY2)=0. For convenience, the 1
by IY1-1=50 INmis(1:IY1-1) is noted as (INmis)50×1

(INmis)50×1 is a non-negative solution to linear equations:

Note:   when FFmis contains negative elements, the exact solution of INmis to can also

A4-Step

Purpose:   Calculate  the  (approximate)  complete  data  T,  from  INsus,  FFobs,  INmis

and [η(n) , β(n)].

Procedure:

When k TTF’s is observed or expected in interval (TL : TU), we

expect the TTF array can be generated through

F

k

(3.16)

where F(T) is the CDF of the Weibull distribution of parameter [η(n) , β(n)].

The above process can be applied to generate the three part of T, namely Tfail, Tsus

and Tmis:

•  For Tfail:

a.  Sum FFobs in its row direction and get the total number of TTF’s in

each interval.

b.  Use these numbers as k and corresponding interval (TIL,i : TIU,i) as

(TL : TU) in equation (3.16).

c.  Repeat sub-step b for the 9 TTF intervals.
d.  Merge the 9 generated data sets together, and that is Tfail.

•  For Tsus:

a.  INsus(IY) gives the number of components suspended at age IY2 –
IY+1 at CY2. Use INsus(IY) as k and (IY2 - IY+1 : infinite) as (TL :
TU) in equation (3.16).

b.  Repeat sub-step a for all IY’s ranging from 1 to IY2.
c.  Merge the IY2 generated data sets together, and that is Tsus.

•  For Tsus

76

a.  INmis(IY) gives the number of components failed before age IY1 –
IY+1 at CY1. Use INsus(IY) as k and (1 : IY1 – IY+1) as (TL : TU) in
equation (3.16).

b.  Repeat sub-step a for all IY’s ranging from 1 to IY1.
c.  Merge the IY1 generated data sets together, and that is Tsus.

Note: This step is using the medium rank approximation we empirically proved in
Section 3.3.3

End of the I-AM program and outputs

After iterating the above M-step and A-steps for a number of times, the [η(n) , β(n)]
will  converge,  if the  program  complies  with  the  EM theory.  This  convergence will  be
checked in the tests.

Apparently, the converged [η(n) , β(n)] is the I-AM estimator of Weibull parameters.
In addition, the missing installation INmis can be another output. These outputs can be
further used to predict the failure frequency for the upcoming years after CY2.

Testing the I-AM Algorithm with Simulated Data

As was done in Section 3.3.3, simulated TTF data is used to test the performance of
the I-AM program we developed. The simulated TTF data is used instead of real TTF
data,  mainly  because  the  bias  and  confidence  level  of  I-AM  estimator  can  be  tested
during  simulations.  As  E-3  step  suggested,  the  estimated  Weibull  distribution  and
missing  installation  data  will  match  the  observed  failure  frequencies  after  I-AM
iterations. Therefore, the quality of I-AM estimator seems unlikely to be further verified,
when the real life distribution is unknown in cases of real TTF data.

The  test  will  be  performed  in  several  stages.  In  each  stage,  different  numbers  of
simulations are performed. For each simulation, a sample pool of installation and failure
data is  generated from  predetermined  Weibull  parameter  [ηsim,  βsim].  The  simulation  is
generated with below statistical features:

•  Number of installations in each calendar year ranging from 1954 to 2009 is

an integer uniformly distributed between 38 and 62.

77

•  TTF  of  each  component  is  an  integer  ceiled7 from  a  Weibull  distribution

[ηsim, βsim]. Accordingly, the year of failure can be calculated.

•  For  components  which  (suppose  to)  fail  after  year  2009,  their  installation
years are exactly known in INsus. Their TTF’s are suspended at their own
age at the end of 2009.

•  For components which failed from 2004 to 2009, their TTF’s are interval-
censored. The intervals are 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6 to 10, 11 to 20, 21 to 40 and 41 to
50 years in integers. Failure frequency  matrix FFobs can be summed from
these failures.

Test Stage 1: Convergence of the I-AM program

The first stage of our stimulation tests aims to examine the convergence of I-AM
algorithm. Hundreds of simulations have been performed on different combinations of
[ηsim, βsim].

These simulations firstly prove that 100 iterations are sufficient. Figure 3.5 shows
two typical cases of I-AM outputs. The [η(n), β(n)] from each loop are plotted in blue lines
in comparison to the simulation parameter [ηsim=50, βsim=2.5]. The blue lines approach
quickly to the red lines within 20 iterations.

However, different behaviors are observed after 20 iterations. In Figure 3.5(a), both
parameters are converging to a stable level. This is expected with the ideal situation of
EM  theory.  Unfortunately,  only  25%  of the simulations  will  reach such a  stable  state.
For  the  other  simulations,  the  outputs  follow  “limited  cycles”,  i.e.  jumping  between
several fixed points (see [67] for details). Figure 3.5(b) shows a case where the [η(n), β(n)]
will reach the same value after a period of 16 iterations. In other cases, periods of 2, 4 or
8 are observed. According to [67], the limit cycles of [η(n), β(n)] is probably caused by the
nonlinearity within A-steps. It is noteworthy that the limited cycles disappear when A3-
step does not group the equations.

Nevertheless, our estimation does not need to eliminate this limit cycles. Since the
several fixed points of I-AM outputs are within a small interval in our simulations, we
suggest  simply  smoothing  the  fluctuation  by  employing  the  moving  average  method.

7 The ceiling of a real number is the smallest integer larger than it. E.g. ceiling of 1.1 is 2.

78

This means, for each simulation, the I-AM steps will iterate 100 times, while the mean
of [η(n), β(n)] from the last 32 iterations will be treated as the final estimation.

(a) I-AM outputs are stable.

Figure 3.5:  Typical converging processes of Weibull parameters in I-AM iterations.

(b) I-AM outputs have limited cycles.

79

Test Stage 2: The distribution of I-AM estimation Error

As  Figure  3.5  has  shown,  the  moving  average  of  the  I-AM  iteration,  as  the
smoothed I-AM estimator, does not fully agree with the [ηsim, βsim]. In this test stage, the
difference  between  I-AM  estimator  and  predetermined  Weibull  parameters  [ηsim=50,
βsim=2.5],  called  errors,  will  be  visualized.  Accordingly,  the  shape  of  confidence
intervals of I-AM estimations can be revealed.

Firstly, we performed 100 simulations to validate “grouping equations in A3-step”.
In  each  simulation,  the  I-AM  is  performed  with  A3-step  with  or  without  grouping
equations.  Meanwhile,  the  MLE  from  complete  data  (i.e.  TTF  occurring  on  all
components  installed  from  1954  to  2009)  is  also  calculated,  to  show  an  example  of  a
good estimation.

Figure  3.6  shows  the  frequency  distribution  of  estimated  parameters.  The
distribution  of  bars  in  each  color  indicates  the  confidence  intervals  of  corresponding
estimator. Obviously, the I-AM estimator with grouping equations shows much less bias,
because,  in  Figure  3.6,  the  bars  of  blue  margins  are  overlapping  with  the  bars  of  red
margins. In contrast, the I-AM estimator without grouping equations is seriously biased
to the left. Therefore, it can be concluded that the A3-step should group equations, even
though it creates chaotic behavior in convergence of parameters.

After deciding the A3-step, 1000 additional simulations are performed to study the
distribution of estimation error more precisely. The results shown in Figure 3.7 reveal
that:

•  The  estimated  η  and  β  are  slightly  biased  to  the  left  side  of  MLE  from
complete data, or the predetermined value [ηsim=50, βsim=2.5], but this bias
is less than 5% for the majority of simulations, therefore it is acceptable.
•  The deviation of frequency distribution indicates how wide the confidence
bounds of our I-AM estimator are. This is the parameter to be tested in the
next stage.

•  The  frequency  distributions  of  either  β  or  η  are  left-skewed.  This  result
complies with the theory in Section 3.3.3 that conditional distribution of β
and η estimators with given data are log-normal.

80

In summary, the estimation errors of β or η are normally or log-normally distributed.
Therefore,  the  estimation  quality  of  β  or  η  can  be  parameterized  to  the  mean  and  the
standard deviation of estimation errors.

Test Stage 3: I-AM estimation Error for Different Parameters

The  results  from  test  stages  1  and  2  are  only  showing  the  features  of  our  I-AM
algorithm for [ηsim=50, βsim=2.5]. In this stage, ηsim= 20, 30, 40, 50, 60 or 70, and βsim = 1,
1.5,  2,  2.5,  3,  4  or  5  will  be  used.  For  each  pair  of  [ηsim,  βsim],  100  simulations  are
performed. During each simulation, [η, β] are estimated using the I-AM program  with
grouping equations in A3 steps. After acquiring 100 samples of estimated [η, β], mean
and standard deviation of their errors can be calculated, as Figure 3.8 to 3.11 shows.

84

As  the  titles  of  the  figures  suggest,  the  mean  of  error  indicates  the  bias  of  I-AM
estimator.  Positive  bias  means  that  the  estimation  is  larger  than ηsim.  In  the  meantime,
the  standard  deviation  is  proportional  to  the  width  of  confidence  intervals  of  I-AM
estimations. The estimator is considered good when both values are close to zero.

From  Figure  3.8  to  3.11,  a  general  conclusion  can  be  reached  that  the  bias  and
deviations  are  closer  to  zero  in  the  middle  range  of  our  tested  parameters,  namely
ηsim=1.5 to 4 and βsim=30 to 60. See the summary in Figure 3.12.

The  areas  in  color  indicate  that  our  I-AM  estimator  will  not  reach  statistical
estimations  with  good  quality  (small  bias  and  narrow  confidence  interval).  The  poor
estimation quality is caused by several factors hidden in our simulations. These factors
are summarized below:

η is biased to positive

or negative

β is biased to positive

 and

 at  the  top  of

Figure 3.12 where βsim is close to or below 1. In this situation, components fail in their
early  age  rather  than  their  late  age.  Consequently,  a  large  number  of  installations  are
estimated  as  missing  decades  before  2004  in  A3-step.  Since  their  lifetimes  differ
significantly from their approximations in A-4 step, these differences lead to biases on
the Weibull parameters.

β and η are with wide C-I

 and

β is biased to negative

 at  the  right  bottom

side of Figure 3.12 where ηsim is larger than 70. Since the collected TTF are in maximum
50  years,  the  proportion  of  TTF  data  recorded  between  2004  and  2009  is  low.  This
proportion  will  be  even  reduced  by  a  larger  βsim  ,  because  large  βsim  means  smaller
variances.

β is biased to a uncertain side at the left bottom side of Figure 3.12, where ηsim is

close  to  20  and  βsim  is  larger  than  5.  This  is  the  situation  created  by  the  interval
censoring of collected TTF data. Since the TTF distribution is concentrating around 20,
over 95% of TTF are expected to fall into the interval 10 - 20 years and 20 - 40 years.
The ranges of these intervals are large compared to ηsim. Consequently, the variance of
TTF, as a parameter with strong relationship with β, cannot be precisely estimated.

According  to  the  discussion  above,  empirical  conclusions  are  finally  drawn  from

test stage 3, regarding to the applicability of our I-AM program.

•  Our  I-AM  program  is  not  suitable  for  infant  mortality  (β<1)  or  random
failures  (β=1).  Fortunately,  the  time-constant  failure  rate  model  can  be
applied, when the β estimated from Weibull models is close to 1.

•  EM  algorithm  is  an  effective  approach  when  the  TTF  are  not  completely
recorded.  However,  the  confidence  interval  of  our  I-AM  estimator  is  too

86

•

wide  when  a  low  portion  of  the  TTF  is  recorded  in  the  complete
population. We recommend this portion to be larger than 5%.
I-AM  algorithm  can  be  applied  when  the  recorded  TTF  are  all  interval-
censored. However, the distribution of TTF data should not concentrate in
one or two censoring intervals. Quantitatively, when k values are recorded,
none of the two censoring intervals should contain 0.9k TTF data points in
total. Otherwise, estimations on β will have serious biases.

3.3.5 Summary of Applying I-AM Algorithm for Incomplete Life Data

Conclusions on the case

The  entire  Section  3.3  has  performed  in-depth  investigations  into  the  statistical
analysis on incomplete life data. The missing installation data in the early stages of asset
life cycle prevent normal ML approaches from obtaining unbiased estimations. To solve
this  problem,  we  developed  a  program,  called  I-AM  method,  based  on  computer
iterations. As a result, the I-AM program has been tests in two simulated cases. The tests
prove the following:

Firstly, I-AM is an effective approach for completeness degree

2.5

, namely when

installation and failure data are lost for a past period as long as several decades.

⟨

⟩

Secondly, the developed I-AM program can be effectively applied to an aging asset
population  (from  which  the  Weibull  shape  parameter  is  expected  to  be  larger  than  1).
However, to acquire a satisfying estimation quality, two requirements should be met:

•  Failures from more than 5% of the population should be recorded.
•  When k failures are recorded, none of the two intervals contains 0.9k TTF
data  points  in  total,  so  that  a  serious  bias  of  estimations  on  β  can  be
avoided.

Conclusion on the analysis approach

The investigation is a supplement to the life data analysis. The functionality of life
data  analysis  remains  as  Section  3.2  stated.  However,  the  applicability  of  life  data
analysis is enlarged. It no longer needs to be limited to cases in which installation and
life data are recorded since the introduction of an asset type. Instead, analyzing an asset
population in their medium ages has become feasible.

87

3.4 Accelerated Life Data Analysis

There  are  many  categories  of  accelerated  life  tests.  As  its  name  suggests,  the
stresses  on  the  test  object  are  raised  to  a  higher-than-operation  level,  so  that  the
consequent aging process is accelerated and the test duration is shortened. The tests are
commonly named according to the acceleration stress, such as voltage endurance tests,
impulse test, repetitive transient test, thermal endurance test, thermal cycling test, etc.

In each of the accelerated life tests, TTF and the applied stresses are recorded for
each test object. Essentially as a category of TTF data, accelerated life data analysis is
based  on  the  Weibull  model  introduced  in  Section  3.3.  The  purpose  of  this  section,
however,  is  (1)  to  combine  the  Weibull  model  with  stress  vs.  lifetime  relationship  in
joint probabilistic models, and (2) to adjust statistical techniques for these joint models,
so that they can be applied to predict life for full scale component operating in the field.

This  section  uses  a  case  study  to  illustrate  the  joint  probabilistic  model  and
corresponding  statistical  approaches.  The  case  is  a  voltage  endurance  test  on  a
transformer winding. The contents are arranged as follows:

Section  3.4.1  introduces  the  generic  tasks  to  be  accomplished  by  accelerated  life
data  analysis.  Section  3.4.2  reviews  existing  findings  on  the  voltage  vs.  lifetime
relationships  of  the  investigated  insulation,  as  necessary  preparation  to  establish  the
joint probabilistic lifetime model. This model, named “Inverse Power-Weibull”, will be
mathematically  introduced  in  Section  3.4.3.  The  introduction  includes  its  maximum
likelihood estimator and its Fisher information confidence intervals.

The estimators in Section 3.4.3 cannot achieve interval estimation on service life of
full-size  components in  which  the  stress  is  not  uniformly  distributed. Thus,  in Section
3.4.4,  we  will  develop  a  solution  based  on  the  Inverse  Power-Weibull  model.  As  an
example of these components, a transformer winding is described in Section 3.4.5 as our
test  object.  Also,  the  test  procedure  we  developed  will  be  described.  By  applying  the
interval  estimation  approach  in  3.4.4  on  the  TTF  data  acquired  from  accelerated  life
tests, section 3.4.5 reaches the conclusion on the reliability of the transformer winding.

It should be noted that in order to focus on the statistical approach, detailed aging
mechanisms and test designs will be skipped in Section 3.4.2 and 3.4.5, since they can
be found in [68, 69].

88

3.4.1 General Procedure to Analyze Accelerated Life Data

Generally speaking, accelerated life tests are destructive. Therefore, they are of the
same category of type tests, rather than routine tests or diagnostic tests. In accelerated
life tests, a number of size-reduced specimens will be tested under multiple stress levels
higher than operation ratings until they break down.

To support AM decisions, accelerated life data analysis should solve one problem
for  the  testers:  how  can  the  life  data  obtained  from  size-limited  specimens  in  the
laboratory be utilized to guarantee the service life of the large scale solid insulation in
the field?

This problem contains three subtasks.

•  Stress extrapolation: extrapolate the results from the accelerated test stress

level(s) to the operating stress level. See Section 3.4.2 for details.

•  Reliability  estimation:  predict  the  life  with  a  certain  reliability  at  the
operating  stress  level,  by  using  probabilistic  lifetime  models,  such  as
Weibull distribution in Section 3.3.

•  Size  enlargement:  transform  the  life  of  a  specimen  to  that  of  a  full-scale

high voltage component, which is the focus of Section 3.4.4.

Traditionally, the three subtasks are completed stepwise as described below.

•  Firstly,  lifetime  data  obtained  at  different  test  stress  levels  are  fitted  to  a
Weibull  distribution.  From  the  best-fitted  Weibull  parameters,  the  Mean-
Time-To-Failure (MTTF) at each test stress level can be calculated.

•  Secondly,  the  MTTF  is fitted  into  a function  of stress  through  techniques
such  as  linear  regressions.  This  function  of  stress  is  called  the  stress  vs.
lifetime relationship model. From the regression analysis, the MTTF at the
operating voltage is calculated.

•  The size enlargement step is frequently not quantitatively performed. Test
designers are simply choosing relatively large-scale samples to reduce this
effect.

Figure 3.13 shows this stepwise procedure with thin arrow lines:

Obviously,  in  the  “reliability  estimation”-step  in  the  above  procedure,  significant
statistical  information  is  lost  about  the  data,  such  as  the  variance  of  the  lifetime.  To
tackle  this  problem,  in  a  joint  probabilistic  model,  the  Weibull  scale  parameters  is

89

regarded  as  a  function  of  stress,  so  that  “reliability  estimation”  and  “voltage
extrapolation” can be contained simultaneously. This model is named “Accelerated Life
Test Analysis” (ALTA) as the bold line in Figure 3.13 shows.

Figure 3.13: Procedure of statistical life prediction through utilizing accelerated life data.

From Section 3.4.2 to Section 3.4.5, we applied ALTA [70] to predict the service

life of a transformer winding. In the application,

•  The  advantages  of  “ALTA”  to  the  traditional  stepwise  procedure  are

illustrated. Specifically,

o  ALTA  enables  the  size  enlargement  step,  through  acquiring  a
Weibull scale parameter for multiple test stresses (voltages in this
specific case).

o  ALTA  enables  calculation  of  the  confidence  interval  of  predicted

service life.

•  The life is predicted for a full-scale transformer within which the electrical
stress  distributes  unevenly.  We  adapted  the  size  enlargement  and
confidence interval calculation to this situation.

90

3.4.2 Background Knowledge about Voltage vs. Life Relationship of the

Investigated Dielectric
This  section  provides  a  brief  review  of  the  voltage  vs.  lifetime  models  of  epoxy

resin, the investigated insulation material in our case study.

In the past decades, large numbers of accelerated life tests have been performed on
organic solid insulating materials. In the 1960s, Kreuger shows the now widely accepted
inverse power law that relates voltage and life in the below equation [71].

T CV −
=

n

(3.17)

where  T  is  the  lifetime  at  electrical  stress  V,  and  C  is  a  constant.  The  n  is  the  life
exponent indicating the acceleration effect of electrical stress. Log voltage vs. log time
sheets are normally used to plot the inverse power law relationship, because it appears
as a straight line with slope 1/n.

Nevertheless,  in  later  research,  lower  electrical  fields  were  applied  together  with
thermal  stresses  (constant  or  cycling),  humidity  and  high  frequencies  [72-75].  From
these tests, at lower fields, an electrical threshold stress was observed for epoxy resin in
the 1980s [74-80]. Below this threshold, the breakdown voltage changes little with time
and  very  long  lifetimes  can  be  observed.  At  high  fields  far  above  the  threshold,  the
Eyring V-t models achieve a better fit to the test data [73, 81].

According  to  these  findings,  the  voltage  vs.  lifetime  (V-t)  characteristics  have  a
“flat z” shape when plotted on a log. voltage vs. log. life plot. As Figure 3.14 shows, the
V-t curve in solid line consists of three regions:

•  Region I, at high stresses: this is the area where the inverse power law was
originally  discovered  and  the  V-t  curve  is  a  straight  line.  Time-to-
breakdown in this area ranges typically up to one hour for epoxy resin [74,
82].

•  Region  II,  at  medium  stresses:  the  V-t  curve  bends  downwards.  This
indicates that  the  inverse  power  law  is  no  longer  valid.  Many  researchers
recommend  an  exponential  V-t  relationship  for  both  Region  I  and  II  [77,
78, 83].

•  Region  III,  the  threshold effect:  at  low  fields, the V-t  curve  flattens. This
indicates the presence of a threshold electrical stress below which very long

91

lifetimes  can  be  obtained.  The  stress  level  in  normal  service  is  in  this
region.

Region I

Region II

Region III

L
o
g
–
v
o
l
t
a
g
e

Prediction 2

Prediction 3

Estimated range of operating voltage

Prediction 1

Log – life

Legend

───

Voltage vs. life

curve;

- - - -

Inverse power

life predictions

Figure 3.14:  Demonstration of voltage vs. lifetime characteristics of epoxy resin insulation.

The threshold electrical stress is a good reference for component design. However,
it is difficult to predict lifetime for full-scale components from the threshold electrical
stress. Firstly, few data are available at low fields to obtain a precise estimation of the
threshold. For XLPE insulated cables, the  mostly studied solid insulation with “flat-z”
V-t  curves,  the  threshold  values  calculated  from  physics  still  differ  considerably  from
those  calculated  from  the  test  data  in  [77].  Secondly,  the  threshold  value  depends  on
temperature  and  humidity.  These  two  environment  variables  can  vary  largely  in  an
operation  environment.  Thirdly,  the  uniformly  or  regularly  distributed  electric  fields
applied in material tests seldom appear in full scale insulating constructions.

In practice, manufacturers keep applying the inverse power law in life prediction,

by taking the following empirical findings into account:

First  of  all,  a  prediction  from  fast  breakdown  data,  such  as  the  “Prediction  1”  in
Figure  3.14,  is  no  longer  acceptable.  The  concave  V-t  curve  in  Region  II  makes
Prediction 1 to be biased to the right, i.e. overoptimistic.

The “Prediction 2” in Figure 3.14 can be a solution. In “Prediction 2”, lifetime data
ranging from 1 hour to several months are used (e.g. in [84]). Since data from Region II
and III are used, the prediction line is biased to the left of the V-t curve at lower voltages
in Figure 3.14. Therefore, the prediction is pessimistic, i.e. a positive conclusion about
the insulation can be supported if the predicted life is longer than the design life, namely

92

50  years.  However,  a  negative  conclusion  is  difficult  to  be  formed  even  though  the
predicted life is shorter than 50 years, because of the bias of “Prediction 2”.

Thus, we propose to only use lifetime data obtained at low electrical fields close to
the  threshold  electrical  stress,  namely  Region  III,  as  the  “Prediction  3”  line  in  Figure
3.15 shows. Since the V-t curve is convex in Region III, the “Prediction 3” line will still
be to the left of real life curve, i.e. pessimistic, when extrapolated to operating voltage.
However,  the  bias  will  be  much  less  than  “Prediction  2”.  Consequently,  both  positive
and negative conclusions are better supported by “Prediction 3”.

Since few life data points at low voltage levels are available for “Prediction 3”, the
tester needs  to (1) fully  utilize  the  information  contained  in the  available  data and  (2)
indicate the effect of a limited data pool. These requirements are satisfied in the ALTA
models in the next section.

3.4.3 Probabilistic Model for Accelerated Life Data

Inverse Power – Weibull Model

As mentioned in Section 3.4.1, ALTA establishes joint probabilistic models which
combine the life distributions and the stress vs. life relationships. The model that ALTA
applies  to  voltage  endurance  tests  is  the  Inverse  Power-Weibull  model.  As  its  name
suggests, it assumes that:

1.  The lifetime data at any single voltage level satisfy a 2-parameter Weibull

distribution whose CDF is introduced in Appendix B.1

2.  The Weibull shape parameters at different voltage levels are the same.
3.  The Weibull scale parameter obeys the inverse power law as below:

3.4.4 Size Enlargement Effect

Calculating Size Enlargement Effect for Weibull Models

The  size  of  the  specimens  in  type  tests  is  normally  smaller  than  the  real  high-
voltage  components  in  order  to  reduce  the  costs  of  the  test.  The  effect  of  this  size
difference  on  insulation  life  has  been  discussed  in  various  literatures,  under  slightly
different names such as size effect [85], volume effect [86], enlargement law [87], etc..
In these discussions, high voltage cables are used as the case in which distributions of
the electrical field in different segments are kept constant.

In this situation, assuming that the length of the full-scale insulation is m times that

of a specimen (m > 1):

Solution: Calculating Confidence Interval for Full-Scale Components

The core problem of our simulation is to generate the multivariate distribution of [β,

K, n], which has:

97

•  Lognormal,  normal  and  lognormal  marginal  distribution  for  β,  K  and  n,
respectively.  Parameters  of  the  marginal  distributions  are  estimated  with
ML in Section 3.4.3.

•  Covariance matrix Σ from equation (3.22).

We  solve  this  problem  with  the  following  procedure.  In  Step  1,  samples  are
generated of β, K and n according to their marginal distribution. In Step 2 to 5, a non-
parametric  algorithm  is  introduced  to  create  the  rank  correlation  between  the
independent samples of β, K and n generated in Step 1. This algorithm was originally
discovered by [88]. We use the notations in [89] to describe the algorithm.

•  Step 1:

Calculate the conditional distributions and covariance matrix Σ for β, K and

of S, which is the same order as Σ, are the sample vectors of β, K and n,
respectively.

mixed  Van  der  Waerden  scores

3.4.5 Case Study: Voltage Endurance Tests on the Insulation of Transformer

Winding
In this section, the probabilistic model and statistical method introduced in Section
3.3.3 and 3.3.4 will be illustrated in a case. This section will firstly describe the voltage
endurance tests on the insulation between transformer winding conductors, then analyze
the result from the test with ALTA method.

Test Purpose and Specimen

The insulation samples studied were taken from the windings of a 590 MVA/400
kV  power  transformer.  A  winding  bar  consists  of  (1)  a  conductor  realized  by  twisted
copper  wires,  (2)  an  epoxy  resin  layer  of  0.075  mm  thickness,  bonding  the  twisted
copper  wire  to  strengthen  the  conductor,  (3)  13  layers  of  paper  of  0.7  mm  thickness,
wrapped around the epoxy-coated conductor.

The  winding  is  impregnated  with  oil  and  the  paper-oil  performs  as  the  main
insulation in the transformer. However, the epoxy resin layer is also electrically stressed.
When the transformer is in operation, windings of a total length of 590 m are subject to
the  maximum  electrical  potential  difference  between  their  neighboring  winding
segments.  This  maximum  potential  difference  across  the  entire  insulation  layer  is  2
kVrms.  In  addition,  1033  m  of  winding  insulation  is  subjected  to  1.83  kV,  and  another
1033 m winding insulation is subjected to 1.5 kV.

The  electric  field  distribution  in  the  insulation  was  determined  numerically.
According  to  the  analysis, the  voltage  across the  epoxy  layer is 0.25  kV. The  average
electric field in the epoxy layer is 3.3 kV/mm and the maximum field is 4 kV/mm, if the
interfaces between different materials are assumed smooth.

99

In the routine tests, partial discharges (PD), an aging mechanism of solid insulation,
were  detected  in  a  new  transformer.  It  have  been  explained  in  [68]  that  the  PD’s  are
caused by the epoxy layer.

Two types of specimens have been produced to investigate the aging mechanism.
Each  sample  of  either  type  is  a  segment  of  winding  wire  cut  from  the  new  590
MVA/400  kV transformer.  For  each  sample  20  cm  can  be stressed  under  AC  voltage.
The main difference is that Type 1 samples do not contain the 0.7 mm thick paper layer,
but Type 2 samples do.

In summary, the purpose of the test was to:

Estimate the 90% 1-sided lower confidence bound (abbreviated to 90% LCB latter)
on the life of RΣ = 99% reliability for the transformer. The size enlargement and stress
distribution of the transformer are m1 = 2950; V2/U0 = 11/12, m2 = 5165; V3/U0 = 3/4, m3
= 5165. For Type 1 specimen, U0 = V1 = 0.25 kV. For Type 2 specimen, U0 = V1 = 2 kV.

Test Procedure on Type 1 Specimen

Long-term accelerated life tests were applied with the two types of specimens. For

Type 1 samples, life data was collected at multiple voltages as follows:

A  step-up  breakdown  test  of  1  hour  duration  was  first  carried  out  on  7  samples
before the long-term tests. The mean breakdown voltage was 3.75 kV, approximately 15
times the operation voltage.

The step-up test was followed by aging tests at 1.8 kV and 1.5 kV. The lifetimes of
samples  aged  at  these  two  voltages  are  mostly  below  100  hours.  After  that,  the  test
voltage was significantly reduced to 0.9 kV, 3.6 times the operation level.

As a preliminary analysis, lifetimes at 1.8 kV, 1.5 kV and 0.9 kV were first fitted to
a  2-parameter  Weibull  distribution.  Table  3.3  lists  the  estimated  parameters.  The
correlation coefficients ρ are high, which indicates a good fit.

The lowest test voltage level was selected carefully as 0.75 kV. This is an optimal
value  balancing two  factors: since the TTF’s  are  expected to  be thousands  of  hours, a
higher test voltage will reduce the proportion of suspended data in given test period and
consequently  narrower  the  confidence  interval.  However,  a  lower  test  voltage  will
reduce the extrapolation distance and consequent errors.

100

Test Procedure on Type 2 Specimen

In  parallel  with  the  tests  on  Type  1  samples,  12 Type  2  samples  were  stressed at
voltages above 3.6 U0. After every 500 hours of aging, partial discharge measurements
were performed on all 12 samples. The inception voltage of PD keeps 0.5 U0 and 0.6 U0.
The stress was applied for more than 6000 hours. No breakdowns were observed.

This  duration  is  much  longer  than  the  MTTF  of  Type  1  samples  at  3.6  U0.  After
6000 hours, the paper layers from these samples were removed, the epoxy layers were
examined, but none of them had failed.

Life Prediction for Type 1 Specimen

To  start  the  accelerated  life  test  analysis,  we  first  input  the  18  lifetimes  at  0.9  or
0.75 kV into the Inverse Power – Weibull model. The result of the maximum likelihood
estimation is shown in Table 3.4. The 3×3 covariance column is the covariance matrix
estimated from the Fisher matrix. It can be used to calculate the confidence bounds of
parameter β, n and K as shown in the last two columns.

Table  3.5  shows  the  advantage  of  the  Inverse  Power  –  Weibull  model  to  the
traditional  stepwise  procedure.  The  underlined  parameters,
interval
estimations at test voltages and the point estimations at U0, are calculated with narrower
confidence intervals. For example, the confidence intervals of MTTF at 3.6 U0 and 3 U0

including

103

are  (202,340)  and  (1891,  4100),  in  the  reliability  estimation  step  of  the  stepwise
procedure. They are much wider than the confidence intervals of MTTF at 3.6 U0 and
3U0 in Table 3, because failure data at 3.6U0 can contribute to the estimation of MTTF
at  3U0  Inverse  Power  –  Weibull  model  and  vice  versa.  Furthermore,  the  shaded
parameters in Table 3.5, namely the CB’s at U0, are not possible to be estimated with the
stepwise procedure.

Confidence  bounds  in  Table  3.5  are  all  90%  1-sided.  They  are  calculated
analytically  through  equations  (3.26)  and  (3.27).  The  last  column  is  the  bound  ratio
indicating the bias of lower confidence bounds from MLE.

At each voltage, the η has the smallest bound ratio. The MTTF at VH = 3.6 U0 and
the  VL  =  3U0  have  similar  bound  ratios.  The  extrapolation  to  U0  increases  the  bound
ratio  significantly  to  12.72.  The  high  reliability  also  enlarges  the  contribution  of  the
variance of β on the bound ratio.

Table 3.6 shows the lifetime of a full size transformer and its 90% LCB obtained
with  the  simulation  approach  we  developed  in  Section  3.4.4.  The  CB’s  are  calculated
through simulation of 1x106 [β, n, K] samples. The exact value of the CB depends on the
seed  of  simulation,  but  the  error  is  controlled  within  1%  due  to  the  large  number  of
samples.

Life Prediction for Type 2 Specimen

Type 2 samples survived much longer than Type 1 samples. This is probably due to
the preparation process of the Type 1 electrode which produces an unsmooth surface of
the  epoxy  layer,  and  causes  high  local  electric  field  and  accelerates  the  aging.

Meanwhile, we kept the distribution of β and n as derived from Table 3.4, because they
are independent from the voltage in the Inverse Power – Weibull model.

With  106  samples  of  β  and  n,  106  lifetime  samples  can  be  generated.  The  105
smallest values of the 106 simulated lifetimes will be the 90% 1-sided lower confidence
bound.

From the above calculation, the LCB on TR=99% was obtained to be 648 years with

90% confidence, 222 years with 95% confidence.

Conclusion on the Case

Based  on  these  numbers,  the  conclusion  can  be  drawn  that  that  the  investigated
epoxy  and  paper  insulation  can  survive  under  long-term  AC  electrical  stress  after  the
designed lifespan of a transformer.

105

3.4.6 Summary of Accelerated Life Data Analysis for Life Prediction

Conclusion on the case

In this section 3.4, the Inverse Power – Weibull model was introduced for an epoxy
and paper transformer winding insulation system, in order to analyze a limited number
of life data obtained at multiple low electrical stress levels. With this model, an interval
estimation  can  be  made  of  the  insulation  life  with  a  certain  reliability.  Based  on  the
Inverse  Power  –  Weibull  model,  this  paper  extends  the  existing  model  of  the  size
enlargement  effect  from  a  uniform  to  a  non-uniform  stress.  Since  it  is  difficult  to
calculate  the  size  enlargement  factor  with  analytical  methods,  we  developed  a
simulation  procedure  to  obtain  reasonably  pessimistic  confidence  bounds.  With  these
methods, the usage of accelerated life test analysis is extended to the non-uniform stress
situation.

To show the applicability of the method, we applied the whole analysis method on
the epoxy resin insulation of a transformer winding. Using this analysis, the insulation
life of the transformer winding was guaranteed with 90% confidence and 99% reliability.
This  guarantee  can  ensure  that  the  partial  discharges  in  the  epoxy  layer  are  not  the
dominant aging mechanism for the studied transformer winding.

Conclusion on the analysis approach

Accelerated  life  tests  study  the  lifetime  of  materials  and  subcomponents,  under
configured stress levels. These stress levels are determined by the assumption that only
one certain aging mechanism is dominant.

Accelerated life data analyses are limited by two factors:

•

•

the studied aging mechanism is dominant only in a limited range of stress
levels, and
the testing is limited by the size of specimen and the duration.

Because of these two factors, resulting confidence intervals of service life of full-
scale  components  will  be  very  wide.  Therefore,  the  maximum  likelihood  estimation
becomes  not  sufficiently  convincing  for  assessing  risks  of  failures.  Instead,  only  the
lower confidence bounds are used as the pessimistic lifetime in design.

106

3.5 Discussion and Summary

The chapter has performed extensive studies on statistical analysis on failure data
from high voltage assets. Based on the summary on the time-constant failure rate model
and Weibull life model in Section 3.2, Section 3.4 and 3.4 develop respectively,

•

•

Iterative  Approximation-Maximization  estimation  for  Weibull  life  model,
when failure and installation data are partly missing
Interval estimation for Inverse Power-Weibull model for life vs. stress data

Despite their similarities on probabilistic models, the conditions on when to apply

these techniques vary significantly:

•  Failure rate model

o  Has low requirements on data completeness
o  Can predict time-constant failure probability for surviving assets,
o  Cannot study aging of asset population
o  Can study generically for assets and asset systems, especially when

a number of failure modes coexist.

o  Collected  from  field,  therefore  based  on  existing  operating

conditions

•  Life data analysis (Weibull lifetime model, with MLE or I-AM estimators)

o  Require installation data.
o  Require complete failure records for a decade
o  Can predict the time series of failure frequency, therefore can study

aging of asset population

o  Cannot be applied when asset population is young, or other cases
when a low proportion of assets within the population has failed.
o  Recommended to be applied on failure data resulted from a single

failure mode/mechanism

o  Collected  from  field,  therefore  based  on  existing  operating

conditions
•  Accelerated life test analysis

o  Performed in laboratory on specimens by specialists
o  Can  provide  warranty  on  lifetime,  but  cannot  estimate  their

lifetimes precisely.

o  Study one dominant aging mechanism
o  Can study stress factors in further operating conditions

107

The  above  conditions  decide  that,  failure  rate  model,  life  data  analysis  and
accelerated  life  test  analysis  should  be  applied  in  different  stages  of  asset  life  cycle.
Table  3.7  summarizes  the  stage  when  a  method  is  applicable  in  grey  shade,  and
highlights  the  mostly  effective  moment  with  tests  (in  bold).  In  addition,  Table  3.7
emphasizes the range of assets that each method is applied to.

Based on Table 3.7, the conclusion of this chapter is reached as below:

Conclusions

Economic  and  societal  consequences  can  be  triggered  technically  by  failures.
Therefore,  predicting  failure  frequency  or  service  lifetime  is  vital  to  study  the
performance of asset portfolios. Such predictions can be achieved for asset populations
through statistically analyzing failure and life data. Of the available statistical models,
three models can be applied, whose roles are mainly as follows:

Time-constant failure rate model
•

It  supports  FMEA  on  the  entire  asset  portfolio,  or  a  population  of  asset
systems. Due to its low requirements on failure data, FMEA can be applied
for  the  complete  lifecycle,  or  even  internationally  across  many  utility
companies.

108

•

It supports risk-based AM strategy, through

o  providing an overview on the risks in asset portfolio, and
o

identifying most critical/risky components within asset systems.

Life data analysis
•

It studies the growth of failure frequency due to system aging mentioned in
Section  2.2.  It  is  mostly  effective  in  the  end  stage  of  life  cycle.  It  only
needs to be applied to critical components.
It supports risk-based AM strategy, through

•

o  optimizing maintenance intervals in maintenances planning, and
o  predicting the number of replacements for several years.

Accelerated life tests
•  They are applied to study a specific aging mechanism on a certain type of
subcomponents.  Such  aging  mechanisms  and  subcomponents  need  to  be
known in advance.
It is most effective:

•

o
o

in the infant stage when the aging mechanism is unclear, or
in the “obsolescence” situation when additional aging mechanisms
emerges.

•

It  cannot  estimate  failure  rate  or  lifetime  precisely.  Therefore  it  is  not  of
use  for  risk-based.  Instead,  its  outputs  reveal  the  critical  stress  factors
which need to be prevented in O&M plans.

109

110

Chapter 4 Data and Knowledge for
Managing Reliability of Assets

Reliability  is  the  most  widely  accepted  key  performance  indicator  (KPI)  of  asset
management  in  electrical  infrastructure  [10].  Currently,  regulators  typically  use
customer  minute  loss  and  interruptions  of  asset  systems  as  the  yardstick  of  service
quality  of  TSO/DSO’s.  However,  reliability  data  should  not  only  be  applied  to
benchmark  the  performance  of  asset  portfolio,  but  also  in  a  proactive  way:  when  the
failure  rates  of  assets  and  asset  systems  are  predicted,  asset  managers  can  make  risk-
based decisions on investments for  replacements or expenditures on maintenance.

Many researchers have proposed solutions to utilize reliability data to support risk-
based AM [4, 40]. However, the earlier research tends to describe the end state in which
the risk-based AM has already been established, rather than the roadmap to establish the
risk-based AM.

Faced with this problem, this chapter will concentrate on the roadmap towards risk-
based maintenance, as an essential part of risk-based AM. To describe the intermediate
state  while  the  risk-based  AM  is  not  fully  established,  this  chapter  will  develop  the
reliability  data  model  and  the  knowledge  maturity  model.  Specifically,  the  reliability
data  model  focuses  on  the  availability  of  decision-support  information,  while  the
knowledge maturity model focuses on the feasibility of decision making methods.

The sections of this chapter are organized as follows.

We  develop  the  reliability  data  model  from  Section  4.1  to  4.3.  As  necessary
knowledge  preparation,  Section  4.1  describes  the  process  in  which  defects  develop  to
cause  interruptions  of  asset  systems.  From  these  processes,  reliability  data  can  be
collected  in  four  different categories,  as  we  reveal in  Section  4.1.3.  In  Section 4.2  the
techniques to acquire each data category are explained in general terms. The easiness of
acquiring each category of data implies its availability to asset managers. Accordingly,
in  Section  4.3,  the  contributions  of  the  four  data  category  to  risk-based  AM  are
identified, as the conclusion of our reliability data model.

111

In  Section  4.4.,  we  develop  the  knowledge  maturity  model  for  maintenance
management  as  one  of  the  main  contribution  of  this  thesis.  In  Section  4.4.1  and
Appendix C, our model explains that knowledge drives maintenance scheduling at the
operational  level.  Therefore,  the  amount  of  available  knowledge  is  used  to  rate  the
maturity  score  for  risk-based  AM  at  the  operational  level.  This  role  of  knowledge  in
maintenance  scheduling  further  motivates  us  to  investigate  maintenance  planning  (i.e.
the  methods  to  establish  knowledge  with  failure  data).  In  Section  4.4.2,  maintenance
scheduling methods are summarized in the second maturity score. The scoring rules for
maintenance scheduling in Section 4.3.7 and those for maintenance planning in Section
4.4.2 form the main contribution of this chapter.

Based on the knowledge maturity model, Section 4.5 concludes this chapter with a
discussion  of  the  limits  of  risk-based  AM  strategy.  The  conclusion  leads  to  further
developments  on  the  risk-based,  precaution-based  or  information  AM  strategy  in
Chapter 5.

4.1 Technical Background of Asset Reliability

Theoretically,  reliability  of  an  asset  is  defined  as  the  probability  that  it  functions
under stated conditions. According to this definition, the “unreliability function” is the
symptom of the cumulative probability function of failure in Section 3.2.

In risk-based AM, asset managers are interested in the reliability of assets in service.
In  this  thesis,  the  term  “reliability  data”  refers  to  the  data  from  which  asset  workers
estimate  the  reliability  of  assets  in  service.  In  practice,  the  most  common  form  of
reliability  data  is  the  failure  and  life  data,  as  well  as  the  output  from  failure/life  data
analysis, as Chapter 3 has introduced.

However,  the  reliability  estimated  statistically  from  failure  records  cannot
sufficiently represent the reliability of assets in service. Within the long service life of
infrastructure  assets,  the  design,  manufacturing  process,  operation  circumstance  and
maintenance quality often varies. Such variations, typically appearing as the object, the
cause  and the countermeasure  as  Section  3.1.1  has  introduced, are  often  missing  from
raw failure records or not studied in failure/life data analyses.

112

In  order  to  fully  describe  the  reliability  of  assets  in  service,  the  reliability  data
should  include  not  only  the  final  failures  of  assets,  but  also  the  intermediate  states  in
which  assets  are  developing  towards  their  failures. These  intermediate  states,  together
with  the  final  failures,  are  classified  into  different  “aging  and  failure  mechanisms”
which  reveal  the  physical  reality  of  the  reliability  data  from  the  perspective  of  asset
management. This section firstly introduces aging and failure mechanisms. Then, based
on the introduction, four categories of reliability data will be defined.

4.1.1 Aging Mechanisms

Physically,

the  condition  or  reliability  of

long-living  high-voltage  assets
deteriorates  in  processes  called  “aging  mechanisms”.  As  mentioned  in  Section  2.2.1,
aging mechanisms are mostly studied for materials. In laboratory conditions, one aging
mechanism can be studied separately without interference from the other mechanisms.
These laboratory studies reveal three critical factors in aging mechanisms. They are:

•  The stress factors: often they come from the operation circumstances.

Example:  electric  field, temperature,  humidity, tension,  etc.  Aging  models
can  also  be  established  on  multiple  stress  factors,  e.g.  thermal-electrical
aging.

•  The  reactions:

the  physical,  chemical,

thermal  and/or  mechanical
phenomena  which  gradually  weaken  the  strength  of  equipment  against
stress  factors.  A  certain  reaction  can  be  accelerated  by  a  certain  stress
factor.
Example: partial discharges in solid dielectrics, polymerization of paper.
•  The products of the reaction: they are frequently the direct cause of failures
in a (sub)-component. When the failure is still developing, these products
are called “defects” or “hazards”.
Example: electric trees in solid dielectrics, dissolved gas of oil insulation.

During  field  operation,  multiple  aging  mechanisms  occur  simultaneously  with
complex mutual relationships. Figure 4.1 demonstrates some typical situations in which
two  aging  mechanisms  A  (in  the  left  column)  and  B  (in  the  right  column)  are  taking
place simultaneously.

113

Operation Circumstance

Figure 4.1:  Two aging mechanisms occur simultaneously with various mutual relationships

114

The simplest situation is when each mechanism is developing independently from
the  others,  e.g.  in  Figure  4.1(a)  and  (b).  When  a  mechanism  can  cause  a  final  failure
directly, it can be called the failure mechanism. Mechanism B is a failure mechanism
in both Figure 4.1(a) and (b). In contrast, mechanism A in Figure 4.1(a) only develops
local  defects  with  little  possibility  to  cause  final  failures,  therefore  is  not  a  failure
mechanism. However, mechanism A can potentially become a failure mechanism, when
the effect of local defects is significantly enlarged by certain technical conditions, as the
red  dashed  connector  in  Figure  4.1(b)  indicates.  In  both  cases,  mechanism  B  will  be
described  as  the  “dominant”  aging/failure  mechanism,  when  it  contributes  to  a  large
proportion of final failures of assets.

Unfortunately, boundaries between different aging mechanisms cannot be precisely
described in  the field.  Commonly,  one aging  reaction  can  stimulate  another.  In  Figure
4.1(c),  when  the  red  connector  is  applied,  “aging  product  A”  performs  as,  or  leads  to
concentration  of  the  “stress  factor  B”.  Therefore,  mechanism  B  can  be  successive  to
mechanism  A.  In  this  situation,  analysts  tend  to  regard  B  as  the  dominant  failure
mechanism, especially when “aging product A” or “stress factor B” is detectable.

Example: electrical treeing, the direct cause of breakdown of many solid insulation,
can be triggered from water trees, or voids created by chemical reactions or mechanical
movements [39, 90, 91].

In  even  more  complicated cases,  coexisting  mechanisms  accelerate  each other,  so
that  the  contributions  of  individual  mechanisms  to  the  final  failures  are  difficult  to
quantify. In Figure 4.1(d), when both red connectors from aging effects to stress factors
are  valid,  researchers  cannot  conclusively  determine which  mechanism  is  “dominant”.
Instead, failure analysts tend to recognize A and B as one failure mechanism.

Example:  In  solid  dielectrics,  the  mutual  acceleration  relationship  exists  between

partial discharge and space charge phenomena [92].

4.1.2 Failure Mechanisms and Failure Modes

Aging mechanisms describe the root causes of failures, but not the consequences of
them.  In  contrast,  failure  mechanisms,  failure  modes  and  failure  effects  respectively
describe the failure consequences on assets, asset systems and asset portfolio.

115

Among  them,  failure  mechanisms  focus  on  the  failed  asset  itself.  The
consequences are technical effects on the component itself and, including the functional
part and the side-effect part:

•  The  functional  part:  if  the  asset  is  not  completely  lost,  then  which
subcomponent  is  lost  and, correspondingly,  which  function is disabled  by
this failure.
  Asset  functions  are  not  necessarily  completely  lost  in  many  situations
including:

o  Some  of  the  functions  of  the  asset  do  not  rely  on  the  failed

subcomponent.
E.g.  failure  on  the  electric  drive  of  switchgear  prevents  it  from
switching, but it can still carry load.

o  The failed subcomponent can be repaired or partially replaced

E.g.  leaking  oil  containers  for  oil-insulated  transformers  can  be
patched.

o  The failure can self-recover

E.g. flashover occurring between overhead conductors and a tree
will  remove  the  overhead  line  from  service  for  a  short  period.
However,  the  operation  can  be  resumed  after  removing  the  tree
branches.

•  The side-effect part: the effects and products of a failure which can damage
the  surrounding  assets,  or  harm  the  environment,  personnel,  community,
etc.
  Failures can lead to violent incidents such as fire, explosion, leakage of
fluids  and  gases,  flashover,  etc.  Their  (potential)  damages  will  be further
specified in failure modes and effects.

Failure  mode  describes  the  manner  in  which  a  failure  occurs  in  the  system.  A
failure mode is subsequent to a failure mechanism. In a failure mode, the two parts of
failure mechanisms are further extended to the scale of an asset system. Specifically, the
extended contents are:

•  The  functional  part  studies  how  the  loss  of  a  failed  component  interrupts
the  functions  of  system.  The  system  design  should  be  considered  in  the
study, on the two aspects below:

116

o  The redundancies, i.e. whether the failed component has a backup
o  The  protections,  i.e.  how  the  system  isolates  the  failed  part  and

switches to its backups

•  The side-effect part describes how the side-effects of a failure can damage
the  surrounding  components  in  the  system,  or  harm  the  environment,
personnel, community, etc. It typically includes:

o  Subsequent failures of other assets, which should be studied again

in the functional part.
o  Subsequent  hazards

in  other  assets,  which  requires  extra

maintenance to remove.

o  Visual,  odorous,  acoustic  or  tactile  effects  presenting  in  an
to  personnel,

unpleasant,  harmful,  or  even  dangerous  way
community or natural environment.

The reason why we distinguish between the functional part and the sub-effect part
is that the functional part tends to be available earlier than the side-effect part in asset
life cycle. This difference on availability will be used in the knowledge maturity model
in Section 4.4.

Generally  speaking,  the  functional  part  can  be  logically  achieved  through,  for
example,  Fault  Tree  Analysis  (FTA).  Therefore  they  are  likely  to  be  available  in  the
early stage of asset life cycle and for individual asset systems. In contrast, side-effects of
incidents  are  highly  diverse.  This  prevents  them  from  being  logically  modeled  for
individual asset systems. Instead, side-effects are learned empirically learned from asset
populations,  even  across  multiple  asset  portfolios.  Consequently,  the  side-effect  part
tends to be available later than the functional part.

Failure mechanisms and failure modes describe, respectively, how a failure affects
assets and asset systems in sequence. Based on them, failures can be classified through
investigating  their  root  causes  after  they  occur.  In  contrast,  the  reliability  data,  or
condition  data  in  specific,  should  contain  more  proactive  indicators  of  failures,  as
Section 4.1.3 will summarize.

4.1.3 Four Categories of Reliability Data

The  physical  process  that  occurs  during  aging  and  towards  component/system
failure  can  be  successively  described  as  aging  mechanisms,  failure  mechanisms  and

117

failure modes. Figure 4.2 demonstrates the causal chain from aging to failures, without
discussing  the  complex  relationships  between  aging/failure  mechanisms  mentioned  in
Section 4.1.1.

118

Nowadays, asset managers focus on major failures of asset systems as shown at the
bottom  of  Figure  4.2.  Nevertheless,  major  failures  of  asset  systems  usually  find  their
origin in minor failures of assets and aging within materials. As the foundation of a risk-
based  AM  strategy,  the  prognosis  of  minor  failures  and  aging  is  realized  through
interpreting  the  “reliability  data”,  which  consists  of  four  data  categories.  These
categories are (1) operation stress data, (2) accelerated life test data, (3) diagnostic data
and (4) service failure statistics. The process to apply a reliability dataset in AM can be
divided  into  three  steps:  acquisition,  interpretation  and  decision  support.  They  will  be
introduced respectively as follows:

Acquisition:
•  The  acquisition  of  reliability  data  is  realized  using  different  methods  and
qualities.  Accordingly,  the  form  of  acquired  data,  called  raw  data  in  this
thesis,  is  highly  diverse.  In  Section  3.2,  the  raw  service  failure  data  were
introduced. In Section 3.4, our accelerated tests demonstrated the raw form
of  test  failure  data.  Section  4.2.3  will  further  compare  them  with  the
acquisition of operation stress data and diagnostic data.

Interpretation:
•  The raw data need to be “interpreted” into a standard form to comply with
certain AM strategy. Statistical methods in Chapter 3 act as the preliminary
interpreters  of  the  two  life  data  categories.  A  widely  accepted  interpreted
form is by looking at condition indicators, see Appendix C.2.

Decision making:
•  Condition  indicators  can  further  support  maintenance  scheduling  –  an
essential type of decisions made by the asset manager. Appendix C.5 will
review the generic process of maintenance scheduling.
In  addition  to  maintenance  scheduling,  maintenance  planning  can  also  be
performed  risk-based,  with  the  support  from  service  failure  statistics.  See
Appendix D for details.

•

Chapter  3  has  introduced  service  failure  statistics  and  accelerated  life  test  data.
Section 4.2.1 and 4.2.2 will briefly introduce how to acquire operation stress data and
diagnostic data.

119

4.2 Acquisition of Operation Stress Data and Diagnostic Data

4.2.1 Operation Stress Data

Besides  physical  aging,  operation  stresses  are  the  origins  of  aging  mechanisms.
Aging  can  initiate  from  the  load  that  a  component  carries  (e.g.  electric  current  and
consequent heat), the operation behavior that a component performs (e.g. switching and
consequent  overvoltage),  or  the  circumstances  in  which  a  component  is  located  (e.g.
wind  and  consequent  tension).  Thus,  records  of  this  information  form  the  operation
stress data. Apparently, a hidden precondition to apply operation stress data is that the
failure mechanisms are correctly identified.

As the word “operation” suggests, operation stress data are collected from the field.
The  data  can  be  acquired  automatically  via  sensors,  or  manually  by  maintenance
personnel. The acquired data supports two types of AM decisions, namely maintenance
scheduling and dynamic capacity rating.

Performing  manual  measurements  is  the  simplest  way  to  acquire  operation  stress
data. They are frequently performed together with periodic inspections. It can be applied
to  environmental  factors  which  changes  relatively  slowly,  such  as  temperature  and
humidity.

Since aging mechanism is  a cumulative effect, periodically sampled circumstance
data  can  still  optimize  maintenance  scheduling  in  a  qualitative  way.  The  assets  which
are operating in worse circumstances should be inspected more frequently. According to
our experience in [40, 93, 94], such an optimization depends heavily on the experience
of maintenance personnel.

Enabled  by  advanced  information  and  communication  However,  the  ICT
technologies  also  add  to  the  cost  of  automatic  sensing.  As  a  typical  example,
Supervisory Control And Data Acquisition systems (SCADA) help the network operator
to  record  the  load  and  operation  behaviors.  Meanwhile,  the  environmental  data  are
frequently  received  from  public  sectors  (such  as  meteorological  stations)  or  purchased
from  specialists  (such  as  geographical  information  systems,  GIS).  The  data  collection
has  a  higher  sampling  frequency.  This  reveals  more  details  on  operating  stresses  and
enables more individualized maintenance service on assets. Unfortunately, such a large
volume of data cannot be permanently stored. To reduce data volume, patterns critical to
asset  aging  need  to  be  recognized.  If  such  critical  patterns  are  unknown  in  the  early

120

stages  of  asset  life  cycle,  automatically  acquired  stress  data  will  be  no  better  than
manual records regarding their relevance to asset aging.

In addition to maintenance scheduling, automatic sensors enable dynamic capacity
rating–  a technology  to  optimally  load the assets,  especially  those  at the  bottleneck  of
network  capacity,  to  their  maximum  capabilities.  For  example,  ampacity  ratings  on
cables  and  overhead  lines  from  manufacturers  are  designed  to  limit  conductor
temperature  below  a  certain  threshold  for  the  worst  cooling  conditions.  Based  on
monitoring ambient conditions or conductor temperature directly, larger currents can be
carried when the cooling condition is better compared to the worst-case scenario applied
in the design [27, 95].

Accordingly,  dynamic  capacity  rating  technologies  can  optimize  load  while
maintaining the temperature of components under the rated level [96]. Within the load
optimization, the probability of the components overheating can be calculated [89]. For
overhead  lines,  overheating  triggers  failures  instantly  and  directly.  The  low  sages  of
overheated  conductors  lead  to  the  flashovers  to  the  earth.  However,  for  other
components  (cables,  transformers,  etc.),  failures  are  not  instantly  triggered  by
overheating. Over a long period of thermal aging, the cumulative effect of overheating
on  the  failure  rate  is  influenced  by  other  stress  factors  which  are  not  recorded  (e.g.
electrical or mechanical stresses). Therefore, a direct relationship between overheating
and failure is difficult to establish for field operation. Accordingly, from the perspective
of AM, dynamic capacity rating is regarded as a preventive measure to control thermal
aging rather than a tool to manage the risks of failures.

Meanwhile, there are two limits on the applicability of operation stress data:

When  applied  to  asset  population,  operation  stress  data  need  to  be  used  together
with  other  data  sources:  when  the  operation  stress  profile  is  changing  drastically,  it  is
possible  to  trigger  new  failure  mechanisms  and,  consequently,  increase  the  failure
frequency.  However,  such  effects  need  to  be  confirmed  in  accelerated  life  tests  or
evaluated from field failure statistics, before being considered as risks.

For individual assets, operation stress data cannot support the risk assessments on
lifetime.  Because  of  the  complexity  of  aging  mechanisms,  theoretical  models  to
calculate lifetime from cumulating stresses, in practice, are almost never applied yet. In
maintenance  management,  the  maintenance  interval  can  be  adapted  according  to

121

operation  stresses.  We  regard  such  adaption  as  an  upgraded  version  of  time-based,
preventive  maintenance  rather  than  condition-based  maintenance.  In  condition-based
maintenance, diagnostic data is providing information which is much more relevant to
the final failures of individual assets. See Section 4.2.2 for details.

We conclude from the above introduction the following regarding the application of

operation stress data can be learned.

•  The precondition to apply an operation stress dataset is that it is related to a

dominant aging mechanism.

•  With  using  operation  stress  data,  operation  and  maintenance  can  be
improved through maintenance scheduling and dynamic capacity rating.
•  Decisions on these improvements are made on individual assets, rather than

on the asset population as a whole.

•  The improvements cannot be quantitatively associated with remaining life

or reliability.

4.2.2 Diagnostic Data

In general, diagnosis on assets measures the magnitude of reactions, the volume of
aging products/effects and/or the scale of defects, to evaluate the progress of an aging
mechanism,  as  Figure  4.2  has  shown.  Diagnostic  methods  and  procedures  are  highly
diverse.  They  need  to  be  specified  for  each  asset  type  and  the  associated  aging
mechanisms.

Diagnostic  methods  can  roughly  be  categorized  into  visual  inspections,  offline
measurements  and  online  monitoring,  according  to  their  sensor  and  communication
technologies.  Generally  speaking,  the  advanced,  online  sensors  can  detect  physical
variables  which  are  very  relevant  to  the  final  failure  due  to  certain  aging  mechanism.
However, this relevance needs to be confirmed in maintenance planning, see Appendix
D for details.

From  the  aspect  of  data  processing,  the  formats  of  raw  diagnostic  data  depend
heavily on the measurement methods. They can be Boolean, numeric, categorical, verbal,
or graphical. The update frequencies of raw data also vary significantly. The periods of
visual inspections or offline measurements are typically several years. However, online
monitoring  sensors  can  provide  data  at  real-time  level.  In  order  to  use  them  in  a
repeatable and standardized way across an asset population, raw diagnostic data need to

122

be converted into condition indicators as will be introduced briefly in Section 4.3.1 and
in details in Appendix C.2.

4.2.3 Acquisition Methods of Four Categories of Reliability Data

Table 4.1 summarizes the acquisition methods of the four categories. It shows that
the acquisition systems with higher measurement frequency and more advanced sensors
tend to be applied in smaller proportion of assets, simply because they are higher priced.
As a result, each category of data is available from a different subpopulation of assets.
