123

The availability of data sources implies an important fact in decision-making. Some
decisions,  such  as  maintenance  planning,  are  applied  to  a  population  of  assets/asset
systems. To support these decisions, determining the general status of the investigated
population is sufficient. Accordingly, the reliability data categories with wide coverage
and high availability should be used. In contrast, other decisions, such as maintenance
scheduling,  are  applied  to  individual  assets  and  asset  systems.  They  can  be  better
supported with component-specific reliability data categories, especially diagnostic data.

The  usage  of  reliability  data  categories  in  maintenance  management  will  be

introduced with details in Section 4.3 and 4.4, and be summarized in Section 4.5.

4.3 Usage of the Four Categories of Reliability Data in Asset

Management Strategies
The four categories of reliability data defined in Section 4.1.3 and Section 4.2 play
different roles in asset management. Table 4.2 uses a quadrant table as a rough summary
of  these  differences. The  two  dimensions  to  distinguish  each  quadrant  are (1)  the  AM
strategy each data category contributes to, and (2) the level of AM each data category
primarily uses. Section 2.1 has explained the difference between the operational and the
tactical level of AM. Decision making at these two levels will be further demonstrated
in the examples of maintenance scheduling in Section 4.4 and maintenance planning in
Section 4.5.

There are two representative methodologies to apply reliability data in AM:

•  use easily available data sources to support precaution-based decisions, or
•  build data sources from which the probabilities and consequences of risks

can be estimated, and perform risk-based decisions thereafter.

Risk-based  AM  strategy  has  been  briefly  introduced  in  Section  2.3  and  will  be
further  discussed  in  Section  5.4.  It  searches  the  optimal  balance  between  performance
and costs, through predicting them quantitatively. It presumes that data and knowledge
are  sufficient  for  such  prediction  and  quantification.  In  contrast,  the  precaution-based
AM  strategy (to  be  defined  in  Section  5.3) tends  to take  actions  (e.g.  maintenance) to
prevent potential risks, when the risk level cannot (yet) been quantified.

124

TABLE 4.2:  THE ROLE OF FOUR DATA SOURCES IN DIFFERENT LEVELS AND STRATEGIES ASSET

MANAGEMENT

At  the operational  level  (in  the  2nd  row  of Table  4.2),  condition indicators can be
estimated  from  diagnostic  data,  operation  stress  data  and  service  failure  statistics,  as
Appendix C.2 shows. Section 4.4 and Appendix C will further show that only diagnostic
data  are  supporting  the  risk-based  AM  strategy,  i.e.  revealing  the  probability  that  a
certain failure mechanism can occur on a specific asset. Comparatively, service failure
statistics are applied to asset populations rather than individual assets. Operation stress
data have weaker correlation with final failures. Therefore, service failure statistics and
operation  stress  data  are  applied  in  a  precaution-based  way,  i.e.  recommending  some
“safe zones” for operation and maintenance. These “safe zones” can be further enlarged,
if sufficient diagnostic data supports to do so.

Accordingly, in this thesis we define condition data as the subset of reliability data
which  can  be  specified  for  individual  assets.  Three  among  the  four  categories  of
reliability data can be used as condition data:

125

•  Multiple  sets  of  diagnostic  data 9  which  are  acquired  with  different

methods.

•  Remaining life, as the difference between

o  MTTF, estimated statistically from an asset population
o  Service  life  or  period  since  last  maintenance  of  the  investigated

asset.

•  Operation stress data, used as supplement to the remaining life. It describes
how much the historical operations of the investigated asset accelerated its
aging.

In  contrast,  accelerated  tests  are  applied  on  specimens  of  reduced  sizes  in
laboratory  conditions.  Therefore,  their  results  cannot  be  applied  to  estimate  the
reliability of any operating components.

At the tactical level (in the 1st row of Table 4.2), the role of service failure statistics
is risk-based, i.e. to quantitatively reveal the distribution of risks within the entire asset
portfolio. FMECA based on failure statistics needs to be performed regularly, so that the
need for updating certain maintenance plans can be revealed.

Meanwhile, accelerated life tests, together with other type testing techniques, play
an  important  role  in  root  cause  studies.  And  root-cause  studies  provide  essential
knowledge  on  failure  mechanisms,  which  can  further  ensure  the  effectiveness  of
preventive maintenance (PM) plans. Unfortunately, the nature of accelerated life tests is
such that its life/reliability estimators will have wide confidence intervals. Therefore, we
recommend using accelerated life tests in a precaution-based way.

9 This thesis defines diagnostic data as a subset of condition data. However, in practice, the term
“condition  data”  is  frequently  regarded  as  the  synonym  of  “diagnostic  data”.  This  is  because

diagnostic data reveals details regarding the difference between individual assets. Comparatively,

remaining  life  estimations  can  be  applied  to  individual  assets,  but  they  cannot  distinguish  one

asset  from  others  within  a  population.  Operation  stress  data  can  be  collected  for  individual

components, but its correlation with failure risk is weaker than diagnostic data, as was mentioned

in Section 4.2.1.

126

In  practice,  risk-based  decision-making  methods  are  normally  evolved  from
precaution-based  ones.  In  the  next  section,  we  will  define  a  score  called  “knowledge
maturity”  for  maintenance  management,  in  order  to  indicate  the  degree  to  which  a
decision-making process is risk-based. Meanwhile, our knowledge maturity model can
be used a roadmap for asset manager to improve his decision-making  method towards
the risk-based one.

4.4 Knowledge Maturity Model for Maintenance Management

In  this  section,  we  suggest  to  apply  the  Capability  Maturity  Model  (CMM)  to
maintenance  scheduling  and  planning.  CMM  has  been  applied  by  [97]  to  indicate  the
effectiveness  and  efficiency  of  AM  as  a  whole  in  the  water  infrastructure  sector.
According to [97], AM is organized in 7 accountabilities (called departments in [97]),
namely:
•
Information management
•
Internal coordination
•  External coordination
•  Market approach
•  Risk management
•  Process and roles
•  Culture and leadership.

One  main  usage  of  reliability  data  is  to  deal  with  risks  initiated  from  aging  and
failures.  Thus  it  is  mainly  accountable  for  the  risk  management.  Accordingly,  in  this
section we will only discuss the application of CMM on the so-called risk management.
In CMM, each department will be scored with a “maturity level”. The widely accepted
scoring  levels  range  from  “Ad-hoc”,  “Repeatable”,  “Standard”,  “Managed”,  to
“Optimal”.

In  our  maturity  model,  two  types  of  decisions  made  in  maintenance  management

can be scored. They are maintenance scheduling and maintenance planning.

Maintenance  scheduling  is  the  decision-making  process  performed  by  service
providers at the operational level of AM. As its name suggests, decisions are made on
when  to  perform  certain  maintenance  methods  specified  by  maintenance  plans  on  an
individual asset.

127

Maintenance  scheduling  should  be  distinguished  from  maintenance  planning.
Maintenance plans are decisions made at the tactical level of AM. They formulate which
maintenance and diagnosis methods should be applied to a certain type of asset. From
the technical perspective, maintenance planning is frequently considered to be a part of
standardization of assets and corresponding LC Activities.

Appendix C.1 will further explain their difference between replacement scheduling

and maintenance strategies.

4.4.1 Maintenance Scheduling

General Description

Maintenance  scheduling  can  be  regarded  as  a  necessary  part  of  preventive
maintenance  (PM)  which  has  been  introduced  in  Section  1.2.1.  As  an  optimization
process,  the  feature  of  a  maintenance  scheduling  process  should  specify  its  goal  and
constraints.    According  to  Table  4.3,  the  methods  of  maintenance  scheduling  can  be
roughly  classified  into  condition-based,  reliability-centered  and  risk-based.  Appendix
C.5 will describe each method in details.

Table 4.3 shows that the range of assets optimized in the scheduling are enlarged
from condition-based to risk-based. Therefore, additional analytical steps are needed in
the risk-based method than in the reliability centered method and in the condition-based
method, as Table 4.4 shows.

TABLE 4.3:  FOUR MAINTENANCE SCHEDULING METHODS CAN BE IDENTIFIED ACCORDING TO

The  maturity  scores  from  CMM  will  be  adapted  to  indicate  the  evolvement  of

maintenance scheduling method. Specifically,

•  When maintenance scheduling is not performed, the score is “ad-hoc”.
•  Condition-based method is scored as “repeatable”.
•  Reliability-centered method is divided into two stages, scored respectively

as “standard” and “managed”.

•  Risk-based method is scored as “optimal”.

We name the scores as “knowledge maturity”, since we believe that each analytical
step in Table 4.4 is enabled by several knowledge domains. The domains are condition
indexing,  failure  mechanisms,  maintenance  methods,  failure  modes  (functional  part),
failure  modes  (side-effect  parts)  and  failure  effects  (criticality).  Table  4.5  summarizes
the  requirement  of  each  maturity  level  on  the  readiness  and  completeness  of  the
knowledge  in  these  domains.  The  scoring  standards  in  Table  4.5,  especially  the
underlined text, will be further explained in this section.

Knowledge on Condition Indexing for Condition Assessments

Knowledge on  condition indexing guides how condition indicators (CI’s) can be
interpreted from different sources of raw condition data. The formats of raw condition
data are highly diverse. They can be Boolean, numeric, categorical, verbal or graphical.
The  update  frequencies  of  raw  data  depend  on  the  measurement  system  and  vary
significantly. Periods of visual inspections or offline measurements are typically several
years. However, online monitoring sensors can provide data up to per second level.

129

TABLE 4.5:  USING READINESS OF KNOWLEDGE TO SCORE THE MATURITY OF MAINTENANCE

be further applied in our system diagram method in Chapter 6.

An ideal condition indexing is completely probabilistic, i.e. raw condition data are
interpreted  as  ordinal  categorical  CI,  while  each  CI  value  means  a  certain  probability
that  the  component  can  fail  due  to  certain  failure  mechanisms  in  the  short  term.  This
“short term” is the interval for periodic diagnoses. [40] illustrates such CI’s from partial
discharge  measurements.  When  the  CI  is  updated  more  frequently  e.g.  from  online
monitoring,  the  “short  term”  is  determined  by  the  preparation  period  in  which  a
maintenance task can be properly prepared without extra costs.

In  an  ideal  condition  indexing,  the  boundaries  between  CI  values  are  statistically
estimated  from  just-in-time  diagnostic  data,  which  are  acquired  from  assets  exactly
before their failures. The collected raw diagnostic outputs can be fitted into probabilistic
models. The percentiles of the best-fit probability distribution are used as the boundary
between different Diagnostic Condition Indicator (DCI) values.

130

In practice, condition indexing is normally pessimistic. This means evaluating the
condition  poorer  than  reality,  so  that  service  provider  will  perform  slightly  more  than
necessary maintenance as precautions mentioned in Section 4.3.

A pessimistic condition indexing can exist in three situations:

Firstly,  a  CI  without  any  input  from  diagnostic  data  should  be  considered  as
pessimistic. Appendix C.2 introduces that each CI can be derived from multiple sources
of  data.    The  introduction  reveals  that  only  the  CI  derived  from  diagnostic  data  can
quantitatively interpreted as the failure probability of individual assets.

Secondly,  just-in-time  diagnostic  data  are  only  available  from  online  monitoring
sensors. The relatively high costs of online systems limit the volume of such data. For
periodic on-site measurements or inspections, diagnoses are usually performed on aged
but surviving assets. The conditions of these assets are fairly better than the failure level.

Thirdly,  the  CI  describes  an  aging  mechanism  which  stimulates  another  aging
mechanism  rather  than  triggers  the  failures  directly.  Section  4.1.1  has  described  this
situation in details. In this situation, the exact moment of failure depends heavily on the
final failure mechanism and has weak relationship with the CI. In this situation, the role
of  diagnostic  data  is  similar  to  that  of  operation  stress  data  –  they  are  used  as
precautions which prevent assets from being overloaded.

 Because  of  the  wide  range  of  diagnosis  method,  establishing  knowledge  on
condition indexing is a demanding job. Therefore, the completely probabilistic condition
indexing is one of the symbols of reaching the highest maturity level “optimal”.

Knowledge on Failure Mechanisms for Condition Assessments

Knowledge  on  failure  mechanisms guides how CI’s can be assembled as health
index (HI) which indicates the overall condition of an asset. Appendix C.2 introduces a
generic process of this assembly. The proportion that a failure mechanism contributes to
final  asset  failure  frequency  differs.  The  higher  this  proportion  is,  the  more  attention
should be paid to the CI detecting the causing mechanism. A well-defined knowledge on
failure mechanism should balance between multiple CI’s.

A  completely  probabilistic  knowledge  rule  is  established  from  statistical  analysis
on failure data. Typically, the HI is the weighted sum of CI’s. The weight of each CI is

131

proportional  to  the  failure  frequency  due  to  corresponding  mechanism.  It  should  be
emphasized  that  this  frequency  does  not  consider  the  difference  between  individual
assets,  but  the  number  of  failures  within  asset  population.  Therefore,  the  failure
frequency  is  statistically  estimated,  commonly  through  applying  the  time-constant
failure rate model. Essentially, this process is performing a failure mode analysis at the
tactical  level.  In  the  analysis,  failure  mechanisms  are  treated  as  failure  modes,  and
failure effect is limited at a small scale as a component failure.

When  failure  data  is  not  available,  the  knowledge  yield  to  a  pessimistic  version
described  as  follows.  The  HI  is  simply  determined  by  the  worst  CI,  regardless  of
criticality  of  the  detected  aging  mechanism.  This  norm  is  pessimistic  because  more-
than-necessary maintenance is performed when minor failure mechanisms are prevented.

Knowledge on Maintenance Methods

Knowledge on maintenance methods should be used together with knowledge on
condition  indexing  and  failure  mechanisms,  in  order  to  guide  the  design  of  technical
scenarios  (defined  in  Appendix  C.3).  Specifically,  a  certain  CI  value  should  be
identified as the threshold below which a corresponding maintenance method should be
applied for. Within this linkage, two parameters should be specified:

•

•

the  preventability:  it  records  which  maintenance  method  can  repair  or
recover the component from a certain failure mechanism. If presented in a
completely probabilistic way, it should provide the probability that a failure
mechanism is prevented.
the maintenance interval: it records in which period a maintenance method
can achieve the above preventive effect. In addition, the interval should be
adjustable, according to CI values.

Knowledge for Reliability Assessments on Failure Mods

A failure mode is linking the failure mechanisms of assets with the technical failure
consequences  on  asset  systems.  Knowledge  on  failure  modes  are  developed
empirically from root-cause studies on occurred failures. It should include the collection
of  failure  modes  which  are  most  likely  to  occur  in  current  asset  portfolio.  Since
instances of failure modes are collected in a large asset population, knowledge on failure
modes is the responsibility of asset manager at the tactical level of AM.

132

As mentioned in Section 4.1.2, the technical consequences of failure modes contain
a functional part and a side-effect part. It is a noteworthy fact that reliability assessment
is  frequently  only  using  the  functional  part  of  failure  modes.  This  is  understandable
because  the  reliability  performance  only  concerns  the  functioning  of  assets  and  asset
systems.

A  completely  developed  functional  part  of  the/a  failure  mode  should  be
individualized,  i.e.  modified  for  individual  asset  systems  according  to  their  circuit
redundancy,  as  mentioned  in  Section  4.1.2.  However,  in  practice,  insufficiently
developed knowledge on the / a failure mode only identifies the most critical assets, i.e.
those responsible for the majority of asset system failures.

Knowledge on Failure Effects for Criticality Analysis

Comparatively,  knowledge  on  failure  modes  should  be  extended  considerably
before  application  in  the  criticality  analysis  and,  in  general,  risk-based  maintenance
scheduling. The extension should be achieved in three aspects:

1.  Escalation/Spreading Side-Effects
The  side-effects  of  a  failure  mode  are  frequently  spreading  to  neighboring
subsystems. This effect is included in escalation as Appendix C.3 defines in Table C.2
of.  The  probability  that  escalation  occurs  should  be  provided  by  statistical  analysis
performed at the tactical level of AM. Data samples of escalation are rare, because the
degree  of  escalation  depends  highly  on  the  configuration  of  protection  systems  and
safety devices.

2.  Harmful Side-effects
The  side-effects  of  a  failure  mode  are  likely  to  harm  economic  and  societal
performance  directly,  even  when  the  operation  is  not  interrupted.  They  should  be
investigated with collaborative efforts of the asset manager and stakeholders. Otherwise,
failure effects on some stakeholders will be missing from the KPI’s.

3.  Economic Yardsticks of Failure Effects
Both  functional  losses  and  side-effects  subsequent  to  asset  failures  need  to  be
assessed  financially  in  criticality  analysis.  To  achieve  this,  business  values  and  KPI’s
should  be  both  measurable  and  financially  convertible,  as  mentioned  in  Section  1.2.2
and  2.2.4,  so  that  they  can  be  used  as  yardstick  for  failure  effects.  Since  KPI’s  are
maintained at the strategic level of AM, the asset manager needs to cooperate with the

133

risk manager and asset owner to ensure that the economic assessment process complies
with OSP’s and AM policies.

Appendix  C.3  gives  some  examples  of  escalation.  Section  4.1.2  described  side-

effects and failure effects with examples.

Summary: evolvement of maintenance scheduling towards risk-based

Three of the four categories of reliability data, called condition data, are applied in
maintenance scheduling. The goal of using condition data is to assess failure probability
of individual assets, regarding specific failure mechanisms. This goal can be gradually
reached in four stages which correspond to the maturity levels defined in Table 4.5.

•  Condition-based maintenance scheduling is a repeatable risk management
process. Its performance is supported by knowledge on condition indexing,
failure  mechanisms  and  maintenance  methods.  The  knowledge  can  be
established in pessimistic ways.

•  Reliability-centered  maintenance  scheduling

is  a  standardized  risk
management  process.  In  addition  to  the  knowledge  required  at  the
repeatable  level,  knowledge  on  failure  modes  should  identify  on  which
asset failures should be prevented at the standardized level.

•  Reliability-centered  maintenance  scheduling  reaches  the  “managed”  level

of maturity, when:

o  Knowledge  on  failure  mechanisms  is  probabilistic.  HI  is  a
weighted sum of CI, and the weight of CI reflects the contribution
of corresponding failure mechanisms.

o  Preferably, detectability of diagnosis methods and preventability of

maintenance methods are available probabilistically.

o  Circuit redundancy of individual asset systems is considered when

performing the reliability assessment.

•  Risk-based maintenance or the “optimal” maturity level can be established

based on “managed” reliability centered maintenance, when:

o  Side-effects of failure modes are identified. These side-effects will
not  interrupt  operation,  but  can  reduce  reliability  of  other  assets
and harm AM performance.

134

o  Performance  benchmarking  system  is  available,  which  provides
economic yardsticks of functional effects and side-effects of failure
modes.

o  Condition indexing is completely probabilistic.

4.4.2 Maintenance Planning

Principles to Design a Maintenance Planning Procedure

In Table 4.5, knowledge is used as the yardstick of the maturity of risk management
in maintenance scheduling. From the perspective of tactical level of AM, the acquisition
of  “knowledge”  is  prerequisite  to  improve  maintenance  practice  towards  a  risk-based
methodology. However, acquisition and preservation of knowledge require considerable
investments  on  human  resources  and  technical  tools.  Therefore,  these  investments
should  be  decided  systematically  at  the  tactical  level  of  AM.  In  the  scope  of
maintenance  management,  these  decision-making  processes  are  called  maintenance
planning.

Two principles need to be followed when the procedure of maintenance planning is
designed.  The  first  is  “top-down  decisions”  in  which  the  planner  should  firstly  study
asset system failures, then trace its root causes at component level, and finally develop
maintenance  plans  at  subcomponent  level.  The  second  is  “evolvement  of  procedure”
where  the  planning  procedure  should  be  extendable.  Its  extended  part  can  integrate
additional  maintenance  methods  and  scheduling  (especially  diagnosis)  methods.  Such
evolvement will finally be reflected as the growth of capacity maturity.

Following  the  “top-down  decision”  principle,  we  propose  to  divide  maintenance

planning into three steps:

Step 1: Identify failure modes as risks at the asset system level;
Step 2: Find  the  components  on  which  these  failure  mode  will  occur  and  the

maintenance plans to tackle the risky failure modes;

Step 3: Establish the maintenance scheduling process.

Among  the  four  categories  of  reliability  data,  the  failure  statistics  are  essential  to
maintenance planning, especially its step (1). This is due to the fact that failure statistics
are acquired more easily than the other categories of reliability data, and therefore most
available  across  the  asset  portfolio,  as  Table  4.1  has  summarized.  On  one  hand,  wide
availability  ensures  that  the  risk  analysis  can  be  performed  across  the  entire  asset

135

portfolio. In a top-down risk analysis, this balances the risks distributed in different asset
populations. On the other hand, easy acquisition enables the planning to start early in the
lifecycle of assets. This lays a foundation for continuous improvements.

Evaluating the Knowledge Maturity of Maintenance Planning

We explain in detail how the three steps of maintenance planning evolve to achieve
higher maturity level in Appendix D for compactness of the text.  As we have done to
maintenance  scheduling,  we  divide  the  evolving  process  of  maintenance  planning  into
several  stages  named  by  the  maturity  levels.  Table  4.6  summarizes  Appendix  D  and
shows  how  the  three  steps  of  maintenance  planning  should  be  performed  at  different
maturity levels.

They will comply be further applied in our system diagram method in Chapter 6.

137

Based  on  Table  4.6  and  Appendix  D,  we  draw  conclusions  on  how  to  introduce

risk-based maintenance planning as below.

(1) In  Step  3  of  Table  4.5,  performing  risk-based  maintenance  planning,  or  more
generally  a  risk-based  AM  strategy,  does  not  mean  that  all  maintenance  activities  are
scheduled  via  the  risk-based  method.  Instead,  it  leaves  the  minor  failure  modes  being
maintained correctively, but optimizes the large investments on critical failure modes.

(2) FMECA  based  on  failure  statistics  plays  a  key  role  to  initiate  maintenance
planning. It provides asset managers with an important tool to reveal potential benefits
of  investments  in  new  maintenance  plans  and  diagnosis  methods.  These  investments
will  also  contribute  to  the  accumulation  of  knowledge  necessary  to  enable  advanced
(mature) maintenance scheduling methods.

(3) The  evolvement  of  maturity  of  maintenance  planning  is  limited  by  the  below

factors:

•  Ad-hoc

towards

Standard:

The availability of failure data across the asset portfolio

•  Repeatable

towards

Managed:

Technical capabilities to reveal the nature of failure mechanisms

•  Managed

towards

Optimal:

The procedures to assess the economic value of failure modes and predict
the costs of maintenance plans

4.5 Limits of the Risk-based Asset Management Strategy

Following  the  principle  of  risk-based  AM  strategy,  Section  4.4,  together  with
Appendix  C  and  D,  have  developed  the  procedures  to  schedule  and  plan  maintenance
“optimally”.  Our  approach  focuses  on  building  a  roadmap  for  decision-making
procedures.  In  our  roadmap,  maintenance  scheduling  or  planning  evolves  from
condition-based  (repeatable),  reliability-centered  (standard,  managed)  to  risk-based
(optimal).  In  the  “non-mature”  stages,  the  condition-based  and  reliability-centered
procedures  face  the  fact  that  reliability  data  is  not  ready  when  asset  management  is
firstly introduced. From the roadmap, the below fact can be learned:

138

Although  the  risk-based  method  represents  the  ultimate  goal  of  decision-making,
the  risk-based  AM  strategy  is,  unfortunately,  not  self-standing.  It  requires  additional
AM strategies to deal with the two following situations.

•  The  maintenance  scheduling  and  planning  with  immature  methods,  when
the data and knowledge are not ready to support risk-based methods;

•  The accumulation of data and knowledge themselves.

In  the  first  additional  AM  strategy,  the  immature  decision-making  methods  are
performed  with  “precautions”.  Precautions  are  taken  when  the  risks,  especially  their
probability,  are  unclear.  In  this  situation,  a  typical  risk  analysis  procedure  becomes
unfeasible  to  support  those  decisions-making  procedures  of  high  maturity  levels.
Understandably,  this  situation  is  common  when  the  risk  management  regime  is  firstly
established, or when new engineering designs are introduced.

The  pessimistic  versions  of  knowledge  in  Section  4.4.1  are  typical  examples  of
“precautions”.  From  the  perspective  of  the  data  processing,  the  asset  manager  usually
stays  at  the  safe  side  of  confidence  intervals  of  statistical  estimations,  rather  than
guessing  the  unknown  “optimal”  positions.  According  to  this  reasoning,  the  lower
confidence  bound  is  more  widely  used  for  lifetime  estimations  than  their  maximum
likelihood estimations.

The  second  additional  AM  strategy  is  “information  strategy”.  The  “information”
typically  includes  the  reliability  data  itself,  as  well  as  the  knowledge  to  guide
maintenance  scheduling  and  planning.  Regarding  maintenance  planning,  Section  4.4.2
has  concluded  that  FMECA,  as  the  risk  analysis  on  failure  modes,  requires  a  large
failure  database.  Regarding  maintenance  scheduling,  software  technology  is  necessary
to  couple  engineering  reliability  data  with  economic  risk  profiles.  Otherwise,  the
knowledge  on  failure  modes  and  economic  failure  effects  are  far  beyond  knowledge
domains of field inspectors and maintainers, who are responsible to provide CI’s.

Both  additional  AM  strategies  will  be  further  introduced  in  the  second  half  of

Chapter 5.

139

4.6 Conclusions

Reliability  of  assets  and  asset  systems  are  reflected  in  four  data  categories:
accelerated  life  test  data,  service  failure  statistics,  operation  stress  data  and  diagnostic
data.  The  state-of-the-art  method  of  maintenance  scheduling  can  use  service  failure
statistics,  operation  stress  data  and  diagnostic  data.  However,  lacking  knowledge  on
failure  mechanisms,  failure  modes  and  failure  effects  is  the  bottleneck  of  employing
advanced maintenance scheduling techniques. Therefore, Section 4.4.1 has developed a
method  to  evaluate  the  maturity  of  maintenance  scheduling  at  the  operational  level  of
AM, through checking the accumulated knowledge on failures.

 In addition, the knowledge is supplied by maintenance planning at the tactical level
of  AM  through  analysis  on  accelerated  life  test  data  and  service  failure  statistics.  The
availability  of  data  and  analysis  methods  determine  how  much  knowledge  can  be
created.  Therefore,  Section  4.4.2  has  developed  a  method  to  evaluate  maturity  of
maintenance planning, through checking the readiness of failure data sources.

Finally,  Section  4.5  emphasized  that  the  maturity  models  for  maintenance
scheduling and planning  are  reflecting  the  level  of  risk-based  asset  management.
However,  the  existence  of  an  immature  state  implies  that  when  insufficient  data  is
available  to  support  risk  management,  other  AM  strategies  should  be  implemented  to
support AM decisions. These strategies, categorized as precaution-based and discourse-
based, will be further introduced in Chapter 5 and 6.

140

Chapter 5   Asset Management Strategies

Tackling  long-term  stakeholder-oriented  risks  is  one  of  the  main  challenges  to
today’s asset management. Quantitative risk analysis is the mainstream method to model
all  risks  since  the  appearance  of  asset  management.  Accordingly,  risk-based  AM
strategy is believed to be the main AM strategy, as summarized in Chapter 1.

Chapter 4 provides an overview on risk-based AM strategy, especially regarding its
implementation  in  the  form  of  maintenance  scheduling  and  planning.  Specifically,  the
knowledge maturity model we developed can evaluate the level or risk management in
maintenance  scheduling  and  planning.  Our  knowledge  maturity  model  reveals  two
insufficiencies of the risk-based AM strategy.

•  Firstly, when the maturity levels are low, the risks are actually tackled by

an AM strategy other than the risk-based one.

•  Secondly,  when  asset  managers  want  to  improve  the  maturity  level,  asset

information needs to be managed additionally.

Inspired  by  these  two  facts,  in  this  chapter,  we  investigate  the  applicability  of
quantitative risk analysis in long-term, stakeholder-oriented risk modelling for electrical
infrastructure assets. These risks, named “strategic risks” in this thesis, will be compared
to the short to medium-term, technically-oriented “normal risks” in Section 5.1. Based
on  this  comparison,  we  identify  the  characteristics  of  strategic  risks  which  prevent
quantitative  risk  analysis  from  being  performed  in  Section  5.1.3.  According  to  these
characteristics, we develop a decision tree to classify strategic risks in Section 5.2.

In  Section  5.3,  5.4  and  5.5,  the  “precaution-based  AM  strategy”,  “risk-based  AM
strategy”  and  “discourse-based  management”  are  defined  for  asset  management,  as  a
main contribution of this thesis. Each term represents the solution to two categories of
strategic risks classified in Section 5.2. Among them, the precaution-based AM strategy
and discourse-based management are tackling the two insufficiencies of risk-based AM
strategy concluded in Chapter 4 and mentioned above. To illustrate the precaution-based,
risk-based  and  discourse-based  solutions  to  strategic  risks,  cases  of  medium  voltage
cable assets are introduced in Section 5.3.4, 5.4.2 and 5.5.3.

141

In this chapter, the AM strategies are shown separately to solve individual strategic
risks.  In  Chapter  6,  a  method  will  be  developed  to  solve  multiple  strategic  risks  with
coordinated AM strategies.

This chapter will use large number of abbreviations to keep the text compact. All

abbreviations are listed after appendices for the reader’s convenience.

5.1 Risks of Owning Physical Assets

5.1.1 Conditions to Apply Quantitative Risk Assessment at the Operational

and Tactical Level of AM
In Section 2.2.4, a risk has been defined as a possibility that a stimulus (e.g. human
actions,  events)  leads  to  consequences  that  harm  aspect  of  things  that  human  beings
value. As emphasized in Section 2.2.4, at the operational and tactical level of AM, only
the “normal risks” are analyzed.

A  normal  risk  in  AM  is  composed  of  a  sequence  of  events.  We  name  them  as
listed  below.  Table  5.1  compares  our  names  with  those  in  Morgan’s  model  for
environmental pollution (not in AM) [98] and Wijnia’s model for AM [30].

•  Firstly,  a  technical  stimulus  (TS)  occurs  in  a  way  which  has  been

scientifically understood.

•  Then, a number of technical effects on assets (TEoA’s) are triggered nearly

instantaneously by the TS.

•  Finally,  the  consequence  (of  TEoA’s)  on  the  performance  (CoP)  can  be
interpreted  quantitatively  as  Financially  Measured  Losses  (FML).  The
interpretation can be done with the risk matrix mentioned in Section 2.2.

As  shown  in  the  last  column  of  Table  5.1,  when  asset  failures  due  to  aging  are
considered as a normal risk, aging mechanism of a failure mode, technical effects of a
failure mode and customer minute losses are respectively its TS, TEoA and CoP.

To be assessed quantitatively, the TS’s, TEoA’s and CoP’s need to have a number
of  properties.  These  properties  can  be  found  in  Figure  5.1,  which  will  be  further
explained as below:

About the TS’s
1)  Asset  specific: Each risk initiates from a specific asset due to a certain TS. Its

TEoA’s are mostly isolated within the range of the asset system.

2)  Scientifically modelled: Each stimulus is driven by a scientifically understood
process  such  as  an  aging  mechanism.  It  is  considered  not  to  be  influenced  by
economic  or  political  factors  from  the  stakeholders.  The  experience  on  the
stimulus is sufficient to model its occurrence with deterministic or probabilistic
models.

3)  Empirically identified: The samples of TS’s can be obtained from a large asset
population.  Based  on  the  samples,  technical  risks  can  be  identified  through
empirical  methods  such  as  FMEA.  Meanwhile,  the  historical  record  of  their
occurrence can support statistical inference on their probability distributions.

About the TEoA’s:
4)  Controllable: Service providers are capable to

clear the TEoA’s reactively, after the responsible TS occurs.

•
•  prevent the occurrence of a TS through LC Activities.

5)  Predictable: The frequency of the TEoA’s can be predicted, when

•

asset workers perform failure and life analysis on TS’s;

143

•

service providers perform condition diagnosis on individual assets.

6)  Instantaneous: The period between the occurrence of TS and TEoA is too short
for a decision to be made at the tactical level of AM. Hence, an asset manager is
unlikely to create or modify investment plans during this period.

About the CoP’s:
7)  Measurable: The loss of performance must be real and measurable. Commonly,

KPI’s provide the measurement standards.

8)  Convertible: All KPI’s can be converted to financial losses. Therefore, CoP is

only composed of FML.

Counterexamples of measurability and convertibility will be show in Section 5.1.3.

Technical Stimuli (TS)

Asset (system) specific

Scientifically modelled

Empirically identified

Technical Effects on Assets (TEoA)

Controllable

Predictable

Instantaneous effects

Consequences on Performance (CoP) or Financially Measured Losses (FML)

Measurable

Convertible

Figure 5.1: Typical components of a normal risk handled by the tactical level of AM, as well as

their characteristics.

The  normal  risks  are  handled  by  two  groups  of  people.  On  one  hand,  service
providers and asset workers assess normal risks quantitatively with technical variables
such as stress, aging, condition and failure frequency. This assessment process has been
introduced in Chapter 4. As experts on the technical stimuli and consequences, service
providers should know how to observe, to predict and to prevent the technical stimuli.
Their capability ensures the controllability and predictability of TEoA’s.

On  the  other  hand,  the  asset  manager  should  constantly  check  whether  the
characteristics of normal risks listed in Figure 5.1 are valid. These characteristics ensure
service providers to concentrate on the technical features of assets and asset systems.

144

When a risk does not have all characteristics listed in Figure 5.1, quantitative risk
assessment  cannot  be  applied  on  it.  This  does  not  mean  that  these  “irregular”  risks
should be ignored. Instead, they should be managed using different methods, which will
further  lead  to  developments  of  different  AM  strategies.  Such  non-classical  risk
management is a main task of the strategic level of AM.

5.1.2 Strategic Problems Concerned by Asset Owner

The strategic level of AM tackles the problems which occur throughout the entire
asset portfolio. These “strategic problems” need to be analyzed from the perspective of
the asset owner and solved with the long-term in  mind. Typically, a strategic problem
does  not  treat  technical  hazards  as  initial  stimuli  like  the  tactical  level  does.  Instead,
technical  hazards  are  considered  as  intermediate  consequences  caused  by  stakeholder
requirements.  Through  triggering  technical  hazards  throughout  the  asset  portfolio,  a
strategic  problem  will  ultimately  threaten  the  interest  of  other  stakeholders.  Below
examples  of “strategic  problems”  are listed  which are  a  concern for today’s  European
utility companies:

•  Poor  Data:  Historical  changes  of  geographical  information,  organization
and personnel undermine the quality of operation, maintenance and failure
data.  From  a  poorly  maintained  database,  it  is  difficult  to  identify  failure
modes and to perform risk assessment.

o  Example:  Different  utility  companies  measure  assets

in  different

standards and store O&M data in different formats. When they merge, the

standardization of data is a significant challenge.

•  Evolvement  of  Technologies:  The  technology  to  construct  an  asset
population  is  not  in  its  mature  phase.  The  experience  to  operate  and
maintain  it  is  insufficient,  which  can  limit  the  effect  of  Life  Cycle
Activities  (LC  Activities)  to  fully  use  the  lifetime  or  capacity  of  such
assets. Specifically,

o  For  old  technologies:  The  number  of  suppliers  and  service  providers
decreases. The skills and knowledge to operate/maintain them have been

lost due to the retirement of experienced personnel.

o  For  new  technologies:  Very  few  numbers  of  suppliers  monopolize  the
knowledge  of  how  to  operate/maintain  them.  Many  failure  modes  are

unknown  to  the  asset  manager.  E.g.  high  temperature  superconducting

connections

145

•  Different  Operating  Conditions:  Certain  “external”  factors,  which  are
unlikely  to  be  controlled  by  the  asset  owner,  are  changing  the  aging
mechanisms or aging rates of the assets.

o  Example  1:  The  energy  market  can  change  the  load  profiles  and,

consequently, add new operating stress factors/requirements.

o  Example 2: Changes on the operating environment, such as urbanization,
accelerate certain aging mechanisms over an entire geographical area.

•  Different  Constraints  on  LC  Activities:  External  factors  prevent  the  asset
manager  from  performing  certain  LC  Activities.  Typically,  such  factors
originate from safety and environmental concerns, but influence the utility
company through politics.

o  Example  1,  NIMBY  phenomena  and green  movements:  The  communities
resist  to  additional  substations,  or  even  civil  works,  within  their

neighborhood.  Meanwhile,  the  interests  and  concerns  of  environmental

groups  do  not  align  with  those  of  utilities.  Both  lead  to  introduction  of

underground lines and compact substations. Furthermore, materials such

as greenhouse gases can be prohibited from usage, which forces certain

technologies to be abandoned.

o  Example 2, shared infrastructure: In populated cities, power cables share
route  with  communication  cables,  pipes,  etc.  Permissions  to  modify  the

distribution  network  are  strictly  limited  by  the  municipality.  In  addition,

failures  of  these  cables  will  harm  other  infrastructure,  which  make  the

consequences more severe.

From  the  perspective  of the  asset  owner, the  strategic  problems  bring  risks to the
asset  portfolio. These  risks,  noted as “strategic  risks”,  are  constructed  in  a  way  that is
different from normal risks, as will be explained in the next section.

5.1.3 Components and Characteristics of Strategic Risks

Strategic risks in asset management preserve the sequence from TS, TEoA to CoP.
Such  preservation  is  necessary  because  the  role  of  AM  is  to  convert  engineering
uncertainties (modeled as TS and TEoA) into financial risks (modeled as CoP). In other
words, a risk without TS and TEoA can be better handled outside AM, for instance by
finance specialists.

146

Meanwhile,  Strategic  Problems  (SP’s),  such  as  those  listed  in  Section  5.1.2,  take
the place of TS as the initial stimuli of strategic risks. The sequences initiated with a SP
can appear in four different forms:

Strategic Problems

 (SP), Type 3

Performance
indicators (PI's)

Strategic Problems

Technical  Stimuli

 (SP), Type 1

 (TS)

Strategic Problems

Life Cycle Activities

 (SP), Type 2

 (LC Activities)

Technical  Effects on
Assets

(TEoA)

Figure 5.2: Components of strategic risks in different types of sequences.

Financially
Measured Loss

(FML)

Consequences on
Scarce Resources

(CoSR), Type 4

Unmeasurable
Consequences

(UC), Type 4

•  Sequence Type 1: SP  TS  TEoA  CoP (FML)

o  The SP is only associated with certain asset population and certain
TS’s,  rather  than  the  entire  asset  portfolio.  Therefore,  SP,  TS,
TEoA and CoP compose the sequence of risk.

o  “Evolvement  of  Technologies”  and  “Different  Operation

Conditions” are likely to initiate Sequence Type 1.

•  Sequence Type 2: SP  LC Activities  TEoA  CoP (FML)

o

In  classic  risks,  LC  Activities  are  treated  as  countermeasures  to
prevent  risks.  If  necessary,  LC  Activities  are  ready  to  be
performed. In contrast, some SP’s weaken the capability of service
providers to perform LC Activities.

o  “Poor data” and “Different Constraints on LC Activities” are likely

to initiate Sequence Type 2.

•  Sequence Type 3: SP  KPI  CoP (FML)

o  TEoA’s  are  interpreted  as  CoP’s  through  applying  risk  matrices.
When  analyzing  normal  risks,  the  KPI’s  in  risk  matrices  are
decided by asset owner. These KPI’s are also likely to be changed
in the long term by SP’s.

147

o  When  “Evolvement  of  Technologies”  or  “Constraints  on  LC
Activities”  affects  the  asset  owner  in  an  economic  way  (e.g.
increase  the  cost  of  incidents),  KPI’s  and  risk  matrices  should  be
amended. This presents itself as Sequence Type 3.

•  Sequence Type 4: SP  CoP (especially CoSR) or UC

This  CoP  include  Consequence  on  Scarce  Resources  (CoSR)  in
addition to FML. In addition, Unmeasurable Consequence (UC) is also
attached as another component other than CoP.
o

In  normal  risks,  FML  is  considered  as  the  only  type  of
consequences.  In  strategic  risks,  two  new  types  of  consequences
are frequently handled:

  Consequence on Scarce Resources (CoSR): It is the part of
CoP  which  is  composed  of  KPI’s  which  cannot  be
converted to FML.

  Each KPI deals with a resource which is “scarce”, i.e. not
(easily)  purchasable  in  a  free  market.  The  amount  of  a
scarce  resource  can  still  be  measured,  but  it  cannot  be
converted  financially.  KPI’s  in  CoSR  can  be  called  a
strategic KPI.

  Unmeasurable Consequence (UC): An UC is not a part of
CoP,  since  it  is  very  difficult  be  described  quantitatively
with  KPI.  An  UC  is  composed  of  qualities  rather  than
quantities,  because  the  formation  of  an  UC  is  frequently
psychological, subjective and case-sensitive.

o

If “Evolvement of Technologies” or “Constraints on LC Activities”
add  a  political  consequences  (modelled  as  CoSR  and  UC)  to  the
TEoA’s.

The additional components and different sequences of a strategic risk influence the
characteristics of it. The characteristics of normal risks defined in Section 5.1.1 cannot
be fulfilled, from which the new characteristics of strategic risks can be defined. These
new characteristics are listed in Table 5.2.

The nine characteristics for strategic risks are explained in detail as below.

1)  Asset population or portfolio
A  strategic  risk  normally  influences  a  large  asset  population  or  the  entire  asset
portfolio.  TS’s  are  identified  and  LC  Activities  are  designed  in  certain  technical  and
commercial  conditions.  When  these  conditions  are  broken  by  a  SP,  the  corresponding
TEoA’s are unlikely to be limited within a small group of assets or asset systems.

2)  Unknown possibilities
A  strategic  risk  can  be  a  possibility  which  has  never  been  experienced,  and
therefore  unknown  to  risk  specialists  in  two  situations.  Firstly,  changes  of  business
environment can bring new TS to the asset portfolio. If the LC Activities cannot adapt to
newly appeared TS’s, they can trigger one or more TEoA’s on large number of assets
throughout the asset portfolio.

Secondly,  multiple  TS’s  can  occur  which  jointly  trigger  a  catastrophic  incident
(TEoA).  In  normal  risks,  these  TS’s  are  identified  separately  and  assumed  to  be

149

independent from each other. Accordingly, the chance of such catastrophic incidents is
considered  to  be  small.  However,  a  strategic  risk  should  investigate  the  abnormal
conditions  which  concentrate  these  TS’s  on  one  site.  To  identify  these  conditions,  the
asset manager should examine pessimistically all possible scenarios. For example, these
conditions can not only be created by technical factors and personnel, but also by human
factors or external parties.

3)  Deductive learning
Strategic risks cannot always be learned inductively or empirically. Sometimes they
have to be learned deductively, especially in cases of unknown possibilities. As stated
in the last point, the TEoA’s of small probability should not be learned from historical
data  or  experiences,  but  deduced  from  combining  different  scenarios  of  TS’s  and  LC
Activities.

4)  External risks on LC Activities
In risk management, external risks have stimuli which are not controlled by the first
party  (utility  company  in  our  case).  The  probability  of  their  occurrence  cannot  be
predicted by the risk manager, because the information supporting probability prediction
is  either  inaccessible  or  too  complex  to  be  analyzed.  In  AM,  outsourcing  of
maintenance[38] is a typical situation when external risks are introduced. Moreover, the
infrastructure  sectors  tend  to  have  more  external  strategic  risks  than  other  industries,
because the assets are found in almost all civilian constructions.

Many strategic risks are caused by external SP’s. When the external SP is affecting
the  TS,  LC  Activities  or  KPI,  it  leads  to  poor  predictability,  controllability  or
measurability  respectively.  Among  them,  the  effects  on  TS  and  KPI  can  be  covered
through  developing  knowledge  on  technology  in  point  5  and  knowledge  on
performance  in  point  8  respectively.  Therefore,  in  this  chapter,  the  effect  of  external
SP’s will be limited in LC Activities.

5)  Insufficient knowledge on (specific) technology
Knowledge  on  Technology  (KoT)  describes TS’s,  LC  Activities  and TEoA’s, as
well as the relationship between them. Chapter 4 has shown that knowledge is necessary
to  assess  normal  technical  AM  risks  at  the  tactical  and  operational  level  of  AM.  In
maintenance scheduling and planning, knowledge on technology is required to improve
the maturity level from “ad-hoc” to “managed”. In Section 4.4, we have emphasized that

150

accumulating  knowledge  is  the  responsibility  of  strategic  AM,  since  it  should  be
coordinated with solutions to other SP’s (such as upgrade of technology).

6)  Delay effect
The  delay  effect  describes  the  situation  that  TEoA’s  are  triggered  a  long  period
(typically  decades)  after  the  corresponding  TS  or  LC  Activities.  The  delay  effect  is
common  when  the  TEoA  is  related  to  capital  investment  on  infrastructure  assets,
because infrastructure assets have very high reliability and long life cycle.

7)  Consequence on scarce resources
Strategic  risks  can have  Consequences  on  Scarce  Resources  (CoSR).  CoSR can
be  measured  but  not  converted  to  financial  losses.  The  situations  in  which  CoSR  can
appear have been studied extensively by risk management theory. Some examples are:

•

Irreversibility:  Damages  such  as  environmental  pollution,  fatal  hazards,
interruption of other infrastructures, cannot be fully reversed.

•  Appearance of unequal treatment: KPI’s, such as customer minute loss, are
supposed  to  be  similar  in  different  locations.  When  blackouts  or  injuries
occur repetitively in a neighborhood within a short period, the credibility of
the utility company will be damaged.

•  Ubiquity: TEoA’s and LC Activities (such as maintenance on medium- and
low-voltage cables) can cause minor inconveniences (such as noises, dust,
and  road  closure)  over  a
treated
individually,  the  “damage”  is  very  small.  However,  when  disturbing  too
many  stakeholders,  the  effect  will  be  significantly  enlarged  to  a  scale
sufficient to incur municipal limits or political opposition.

large  geographical  area.  When

•  Persistency:  Some  TEoA’s,  such  as  visual  impacts  and  noises,  can  occur
repetitively  or  exist  constantly  throughout  the  life  cycle  of  assets.  Their
negative effect can start at an insignificant level, but grow gradually until
they  are  no  longer  accepted  by  political  entities  such  as  regulators,
municipalities, etc.

8)  Insufficient knowledge on performance
Strategic risks can have technical consequences which are temporarily difficult to
be  interpreted  as  existing  KPI’s,  due  to  lack  of  Knowledge  on  Performance  (KoP).
Knowledge on performance typically consists of cases which describe the magnitude of

151

KPI’s lost due to a TEoA. From these cases, a quantitative relationship between TEoA
and  KPI’s  is  expected  to  be  established.  Unfortunately,  many  cases  only  mention  that
one  particular KPI  has  been  affected,  but  not  record how  much  performance  has  been
lost. As a result, the effect of TEoA on KPI’s can only be described qualitatively.

This situation commonly happens when the loss of KPI depends on non-technical
factors. For example, as a KPI, safety depends on the circumstance and the behaviors of
external  individuals/parties.  In  maintenance  planning,  how  probable  failure  modes  (as
the TEoA’s) will threaten safety is usually missing at the maturity level “managed”.

9)  Constructivism
Strategic  risks  can  have  unmeasurable  consequences  (UC’s),  which  is  typically
caused  by  mental  constructions  of  stakeholders.  Constructivism  is  a  theory  which
believes that the idea of a person on an object can be inferred, or “constructed”, from his
personal  experience.  In  other  words, the attitudes  of people  with  different  background
(e.g. ethnic group, education, profession, etc.) towards the same UC do not align with
each  other.  The  personal  experiences  which  lead  to  such  differences  are  referred  as
“mental constructions”. Constructivists use such a reasoning to estimate the UC on each
group of stakeholders with similar mental constructions.

If possible, KoP should quantify the linkage between TEoA and KPI’s. The KPI’s
themselves can be measured. In contrast, UC’s cannot be measured, and thus cannot be
converted  to  FML.  The  mostly  discussed  part  of  UC  is  the  psychological  effects  on
stakeholders,  such  as  all  forms  of  NIMBY  attitudes.  Unfortunately,  this  knowledge  is
out of the range of asset management. Section 5.5 will discuss the relationship between
asset manager and constructivists in detail.

Summary

If  a  risk  has  any  of  the  nine  characteristics  above,  quantitative  analysis  at  the
operational  or  tactical  level  of  AM  is  insufficient.  In  other  words,  the  strategic  level
should  identify  the risk  as  a  strategic  risk.  Based  on the  identification,  a  strategic  risk
can  further  be  classified  into  different  categories.  Section  5.2  will  develop  a  decision
tree for the classification.

152

5.2 Classification of Strategic Risks

This section develops a decision tree for asset managers to classify strategic risks
into six types. The types are named using terms and characters originating from ancient
Greek mythologies. Through the classification, Section 5.3 to 5.5 will further show how
AM strategies can tackle risks in corresponding types.

The Greek name, the classification tree, the types and their solutions (considered as
strategies  in  this  thesis)  were  originally  developed  by  [50]  for  risk  management  in
general.  In  the  original  study,  risks  are  categorized  according  to  three  characteristics,
namely their complexity, uncertainty and ambiguity. Based on the categorization, three
methodologies  namely  the  precaution-based,  the  risk-based  and  the  discourse-based
identified  for  managing  complexity,  uncertainty  and  ambiguity
methods,  are
respectively. In our opinion, these three characteristics also exist in risks associated with
electrical  infrastructure  assets.  Moreover,  they  can,  to  a  large  extent,  explain  the
difficulty  to  quantify  them  in  probability  and  consequence.  Thus,  in  this  section,  we
adapt them to the usage of strategic asset management.

Figure  5.3  shows  the  adapted  decision  tree.  It  begins  with  one  or  more  types  of
“acceptance tests” in Q1, Q2 and Q3. Among them, Q1 ensures that the risk should be
handled by the asset manager. Q2 checks whether the technical knowledge essential for
risk analysis is ready. Q3 confirms that the risk should be handled at the strategic level.
After  ascertaining  that  the risk  is  strategic,  Q4  to  Q9  investigate  which  characteristics
the strategic risk has and classify the strategic risk accordingly.

The questions are introduced in detail as below:

Q1: Which asset population and LC Activities are involved in the risk?

This question aims to clarify whether the risk can be studied by the asset manager,
or whether it belongs to other fields. The minimum requirement of being an AM risk is
that  it  can  be  associated  with  a  certain  asset  population  and/or  LC  Activities. We  call
those risks not matching this minimum requirement “Agnostos Theos”, “Unknown God”
in Greek.

Agnostos  Theos  were  worshiped  in  ancient  Greece  for  the  affairs  of  which  the
responsibility  was  not  associated  with  any  known  god.  Similarly,  a  risk  considered  as
“Agnostos  Theos”  needs  to  be  further  associated  with  assets  or  LC  Activities  before
being studied in AM.

153

Q1:

Figure 5.3: Decision tree to classify strategic risks in asset management into six risk types.

154

Q2:  Has  the  causality  from  TS  to  TEoA  been  identified?  Regarding  maintenance
management, have the maintenance scheduling reached the “Repeatable” level?

After identifying the relevant asset population and/or LC Activities, analysts of AM
risks  need  to  understand  the  technical  part  of  the  risk.  Within  the  sequence  of  risks
shown in Figure 5.1 and 5.2, it is the linkage from TS to TEoA. It should be obvious
that involvement of technology specialists is necessary to answer this question.

In many cases, the technical knowledge is insufficient to support a clear causality
map between TS’s and TEoA’s. We call such a poorly understood risk a “Python”. In
Greek  mythology,  Python  is  a  dragon  who  originally  occupied  Delphi  (home  of  the
Oracle). After Apollo killed Python, prophecies from Delphic oracles (Pythia) became
available  to  human  beings.  The  linkage  between  Python  and  Pythia  implies  that
technical knowledge needs to be ready before further analysis on strategic risks.

Q3: Is the risk applicable to a significant population of assets? If yes, does it have any
of the other eight characteristics of strategic risks listed in Table 5.2?

The third question aims to distinguish strategic risks from normal risks. Firstly, the
scale  of  relevant  asset  population  is  checked,  as  a  preliminary  clue  of  how  large
influence  of  the  risk  is.  Then,  the  other  eight  characteristics  of  strategic  risks  are
checked.  If  the  risk  has  any  of  these  characteristics,  it  cannot  be  analyzed  with
quantitative methods, and thus cannot be assigned to the tactical level. In other words, it
is a strategic risk.

Q4: Does the asset manager know the expected CoP of the risk?

To estimate the CoP, the asset manager needs to have knowledge in three aspects:

•  All  TEoA’s  relevant  to  the  strategic  risk.  It  is  included  in  KoT.  Q2  has

checked its readiness.

•  A  rough,  pessimistic  estimation  on  the  frequency  of  the  TEoA’s.  Data
supporting the estimation should be provided by KoT. When the TEoA’s
are  failure  modes,  the  maturity  level  should  reach  “repeatable”  for
maintenance planning. This ensures the basic predictability on TEoA’s.
•  Knowledge  on  performance  (KoP) for interpreting TEoA’s as KPI’s. It

guarantees the measurability of TEoA’s.

The answer to Q4 is “no” if any of the above knowledge is not ready.

155

Q5: Do some TEoA’s have CoSR?

The  fifth  question  aims  to  divide  the  CoP  into  FML  and  CoSR  according  their
convertibility. When a risk leads to FML and the FML cannot be precisely estimated, it
belongs to the risk category “Pythia”. “Pythia” is a Delphic oracle who gives prophecies.
The  prophecies  from  “Pythia”  are  difficult  to  interpret  as  real  affairs  and  feasible
countermeasures, as TEoA’s such as failure modes do not clearly explain their influence
on business.

On the other hand, when a risk leads to both FML and CoSR, it belongs to the risk
category  “Pandora’s  Box”.  The  damage  coming  out  of  Pandora’s  Box  is  irreversible,
persistent or ubiquitous, as CoSR is defined.

“Pythia”  and  “Pandora”  are  handled  with  precautions-based  strategies.  The
handling  method,  however,  has  slight  differences.  Since  a  pessimistic,  maximum
damage  from  a  risk  of  type  “Pythia”  can  be  estimated,  enhancing  LC  Activities  to
control  the  TEoA’s  can  be  applied.  In  contrast,  the  maximum  damage  from  a  risk  of
type  “Pandora”  is  very  difficult  to  be  estimated.  Accordingly,  the  countermeasures  to
risk of type “Pandora” are to eliminate its original TEoA or TS, which frequently means
substituting the asset population with another technology.

Q6: Does the CoP exceed the catastrophic level at least in one scenario?

The sixth question aims to filter out the risks with the characteristic of unknown
possibilities. These risks occur in such a low frequency that the tactical level of AM can
hardly  notice  their  existence.  Then,  in  Q7  and  Q8,  the  reason  of  not  noticing  will  be
checked.

Q7: Can the probability of TEoA be estimated? Regarding maintenance management,
has its scheduling and planning reached the “Standard” level?

The  seventh  question  examines  how  much  knowledge  on  technology  an  asset
manager has at his/her disposal to estimate the probability of TEoA’s under influence of
an SP. The strategic risk falls into the “Cyclops” category when an asset manager cannot
estimate the probability of all TEoA’s.

“Cyclops” is a group of ancient Greek giants who have only one eye. A risk of type
“Cyclops” reflects the fact that only a particular part of the TEoA’s is clearly “seen”. In
maintenance  management,  “Cyclops”  can  be  used  to  describe  the  “Repeatable”,
“Standard” and “Managed” maturity level.

156

The  solution to “Cyclops” is to  divide the  risk  into  two  parts.  For the  predictable
part,  quantitative  risk  analysis  can  be  applied.  For  the  unpredictable  part,  pessimistic,
precaution-based  measures  should  be  applied,  while  KoT  should  be  accumulated.  See
Section 5.4 for details.

Q8: Does the risk have both a high probability and a delay effect?

When  both  the  probability  and  CoP  are  available,  the  tactical  level  of  AM  can
effectively manage the TEoA’s which have been experienced. However, there are two
exceptions, as this question will find.

The first exception is “Sword of Damocles”. As its name suggests, the risk is vital
when it occurs. Moreover, the vital risk tends to have a low probability, since those of
high  probability  have  been  prevented  as  normal  risks.  Due to  this  low  probability,  the
tactical level of AM is barely able to quantify the risks of type “Damocles” empirically.
Therefore, a risk of type “Damocles” is an extreme situation of unknown possibilities.

Strategic  risk  analysts  should  identify  risks  of  type  “Damocles”  deductively.  For
example, a cascading failure scenario can be found out through assuming high-voltage
components and protection systems can fail simultaneously. Section 5.4 will discuss the
management of big risks.

The  second  exception  is  “Prophecy  of  Cassandra”.  The  Trojan  prophetess
Cassandra has made a series of precise predictions, including the fall of Troy. However,
none  of  them  had  been  taken  seriously  by  her  contemporaries,  because  the  disasters
mostly occurred long after the triggering event. Similarly, risks with a high delay effect
are likely to be dismissed by decision makers. The solution to risk of type “Cassandra”,
however, does not focus on knowledge, but organization. See Section 5.5 for details.

Q9: Does the risk have significant UC’s?

The last questions deal with unmeasurable, psychological effects which depend on
the  mental  constructions of stakeholders. Medusa, the Gorgons representing fear and
horror, is used to name this type of risks.

AM  is  an  area  which  mainly  deals  with  realistic  and  objective  problems.  Asset
managers  will  not  invest  in  asset  portfolio  just  to  reduce  UC’s,  since  UC’s  cannot  be
objectively  indicated.  However,  reduction  of  UC’s  can  be  achieved  while  investments
are made whose primary goal is to reduce CoP’s. Therefore, when dealing with an UC,

157

it is critical to know exactly which feature of an asset population needs to be modified.
Such preparations can be included in discourse-based management in Section 5.5.

Summary

Based on the nine characteristics of strategic risks in Section 5.1, we developed the

nine questions to
•

identify  the  two  forms  of  risks  which  require  further  technical  studies:
“Agnostos Theos” and “Python”,

•  distinguish strategic risks from normal risks, and
•

classify strategic risks into six types:
“Pythia”, “Pandora”, “Cyclops”, “Damocles”, “Cassandra” and “Medusa”.

Each risk type has its main characters which have been confirmed in the decision
tree. Table 5.3 shows these main characters in the shaded cells, the numbers of questions
checking  these  characteristics  are  written  in  the  cells.  It  is  noteworthy  that  the  blank
cells do not mean that the risk type cannot have the corresponding characteristic. They
only mean that the characteristic is not investigated when AM strategies are developed.

TABLE 5.3:  MAIN CHARACTERS OF THE SIX TYPES OF STRATEGIC RISKS, DERIVED FROM THE

CLASSIFICATION TREE
