The following three sections will demonstrate how AM strategies are developed for
cases  of  managing  medium  and  high-voltage  assets.  As  the  first  and  essential  step  of
each  case,  the  type  of  strategic  risk  will  be  classified  through  answering  the  nine
questions.  Afterwards,  the  type  of  AM  strategy  will  be  determined  accordingly.  The
examples are mainly based on cable assets in electricity distribution companies.

158

5.3 Precaution-based AM Strategy

5.3.1 Two Forms of Precautions: Enhancements and Substitutions

Precaution-based AM strategies aim to provide a “safety zone” in which the TS’s
and  TEoA’s  are  reduced  to  an  acceptable  level.  If  the  linkage  between  TEoA’s  and
KPI’s  can  be  proven,  but  the  CoP  cannot  be  quantified  as  Q4  in  Figure  5.2  stated,
precaution-based  AM  strategies  need  to  be  implemented.  In  other  words,  precaution-
based AM strategies are the solutions to risks of type “Pythia” or “Pandora”.

At  the  tactical  level  of  AM,  precautions  can  be  realized  in  many  different
technologies.    From  the  perspective  of  strategic  AM, precautions can  be  performed  in
two forms:

1.  Enhancements: Enhance the capabilities of assets or introduce preventive
LC Activities, in order to reduce the chance that TS’s will occur, or to limit
the range that TEoA’s will affect.

2.  Substitutions:  Substitutions  of  assets  or  LC  Activities.  The  substitutions

are free of the hazardous TS’s or TEoA’s.

Generally speaking, enhancements are cheaper to apply than substitutions, because
enhancements  are  based  on  existing  assets  and  LC  Activities.  Substitute  technologies
bring  not  only  strategic  risks  of  “infant  mortality”,  but  also  “costs  of  changes”  due  to
reorganization of personnel or low efficiency of new processes.

In  the  long  term,  enhancements  bring  less  improvement  than  substitutions,  since
existing  technologies  will  reach  their  maximum  capabilities  even  after  many
enhancements.  When  enhancement-based  solutions  are  applied,  risk  specialists  should
ensure that the expected performance does not exceed the maximum capabilities.

Risk  of  type  “Pandora”  should  be  solved  with  substitution-based  strategies.
According to the definition of type “Pandora”, risk specialists cannot convert its CoSR
into FML, even with pessimistic estimations. Therefore, an asset manager cannot decide
on  an  acceptable  level  of  TEoA.  Consequently,  whether  enhancement-based  solutions
are able to reach this “acceptable level” is unknown.

159

5.3.2 Costs of Changes and Flexibility

It  is  critical  to  balance  performance  gain  and  “costs  of  changes”  in  precaution-
based strategies, as the difference between enhancements and substitutions has shown.
Generally speaking, the solution lies in a concept called “flexibility”.

Flexibility  aims  to  solve  a  typical  situation  in  risk  management:  how  much
performance is demanded is likely to become clearer during the period of usage. In this
period, asset managers can firstly implement precautions with low “costs of changes” to
meet short-term demands. Precautions with high “costs of changes” tend to be delayed
until  the  long-term  demands  are  clearer,  in  order  to  avoid  excessive  investments  on
ample capabilities. In risk management, the feature that precaution-based solutions have
low “costs of changes” is termed as “flexibility”.

How  flexibility  is  considered  in  AM  strategies  will  be  illustrated  with  examples.
Generally  speaking,  flexible  precautions  are  mainly  based  on  enhancements,  such  as
dynamic  rating  technology  to  be  introduced  in  Section  5.3.4.  However,  when  more
functions rather than capacities are demanded, the modular and programmable devices
can  be  regarded  as  “flexible  substitutions”.  See  an  example  of  secondary  systems  in
Section 5.3.5.

5.3.3 Knowledge Development

KoT  and  KoP  are  examined  in  Q1,  Q2,  Q4  and  Q7  from  the  classification  tree.
From these questions, the types “Python”, “Pythia/Pandora”, “Cyclops” and “Damocles”
are classified. It implies that, through developing KoT and KoP, the type of a strategic
risk  can  be  changed.  Through  changing  the  risk  type,  the  AM  strategy  can  gradually
evolve from precaution-based towards risk-based, as we have illustrated in Chapter 4.

The  focus  of  knowledge  development  differs  for  each  risk  type.  For  the  type
“Python”,  the  TS,  LC  Activities  and  TEoA  remains  unknown  to  the  asset  manager.
Before starting any risk analysis, some essential knowledge should be acquired through,
for example, studying aging mechanisms and accumulating of failure data.

For  the  types  “Pythia”  and  “Pandora”,  the  development  should  focus  on  KoP

typically in the following three directions:

(1)  Complete  the  KPI’s.  Take  the  missing  TEoA’s,  such  as  “outages  in  low

voltage networks”, into the performance indicator system.

160

(2)  Ensure  that  KPI’s  are  quantified  correctly  as  FML.  E.g.  justify  the  value

safety in risk consequence matrix.

(3)  Study  the  difference  of  CoP  of  TEoA’s  in  different  circumstances.
Establish  additional  criteria  accordingly  to  distinguish  TEoA’s  which  can
have  higher  CoP  than  the  others.  For  example,  distinguish  “outage  of  a
hospital” from “outage of a home” in SAIFI.

Meanwhile,  with  a  minimum  required  amount  of  KoT,  the  strategic  risk  can  be
moved  from  type  “Pythia”  to  “Cyclops”.  Typically,  the  KoT  is  required  to  record  the
TEoA’s  in  a  standard  way,  which  enables  asset  workers  to  estimate  the  frequency  of
TEoA’s statistically.

For the type “Cyclops”, KoT can be further improved towards a full risk-based AM

strategy. Some typical improvements are listed below.

(1)  Describe TS’s of TEoA’s, preferably with their root causes. For example,
record “electrical breakdown of cable joints directly caused by oil leakage
and originally caused by load variations” instead of “joint failures”.

(2)  In  type  “Cyclops”,  TEoA’s  only  include  major  incidents  which  harm
stakeholders directly, such as outages. Hazards, such as “wear of insulating
oil” should also be noticed until they develop as major incidents.

(3)  LC Activities should include activities to detect hazards.
(4)  Develop  quantitative  and  probabilistic  relationship  between  TS’s,  LC

Activities and TEoA’s.

Section 5.4.2 will illustrate the above developments in a particular case. The final
goal is to be capable of predicting all TEoA’s which have been experienced. As a result,
the risk of type “Cyclops” can evolve towards the type “Damocles”, i.e. where only the
TEoA’s are not controlled.

5.3.4 Case 1: Precaution-based AM for Future Load Profiles

This  section  discusses  how  precaution-based  AM  strategies  are  applied  to  tackle
future load profiles. The discussion is based on our experience with a major Dutch DSO
in projects such as [90].

Generally  speaking,  the  capacity  of  electricity  network  should  be  enhanced  to

satisfy the demand in two aspects:

161

•

In most countries, electricity consumption is growing with population and
economy. This growth is enormous in emerging economies such as China
and India.

•  Recently,  European  countries  such  as  Germany  are  making  efforts  to
reduce  the  total  energy  consumption  [99].  However,  renewable  energies
generate electricity with variable power and at long distances from the load
centers. This requires existing electricity connections to carry higher loads.

In this section, we study future load profiles as a strategic problem (SP) on medium
voltage  (MV) cable asset  systems.  We  consider it  at  the  strategic  level rather  than  the
tactical level, mainly for three reasons:

•  Large population: It is a concern for the majority of replacements.
•  External risks: As mentioned above, the future load profile depends heavily
on  economy  growth,  energy  policies,  development  of  renewable  energy,
etc.
Insufficient  knowledge:  The  relationship  between  higher  loads  and
reliability  are insufficiently  studied  for  cable  assets  operating  in  the field.
See the two subsections for details.

•

In addition, we will show that, because of “insufficient knowledge” and “external
risks”, the strategic risk of “future load profiles” falls into the risk category “Pythia” and
“Pandora”. Accordingly, some technologies such as thermal rating and voltage upgrades
will be discussed as examples of precaution-based strategies.

Issue 1: Demand on Ampacity and Thermal Rating on MV Cables, Modelled with
Enhancement- Based Strategy with Concerns on Knowledge

When  demand  on  electricity  grows  in  a  steady  way  over  decades  in  a  MV  cable
network, the common choice is to lay more cables of the same voltage level. In this case,
how  much  current  a  cable  can  carry  (termed  as  “ampacity”)  determines  the  overall
investment through answering two questions:

•  How  many  cables  are  sufficient  for  a  connection  to  carry  a  certain

maximum load with N-1 reliability?

•  What are the remaining lifetimes of existing cables? Will this lifetime be

shortened by higher loads?

162

The answers are based on the thermal rating of cables. Thermal rating is the upper
limit  of  the  current  on  a  cable.  During  certain  cooling  conditions,  the  electrical
insulation can withstand the heat generated by current below thermal rating for a period
longer than its design life. When a cable is thermally deteriorated, electrical breakdowns
are expected to occur very frequently on various positions on the cable, because defects
are likely to spread throughout the cable. Therefore, the cable needs to be replaced.

According to the above description, the components of this strategic risk are:

•  TS: Thermal deterioration on the insulation.
•  LC  Activities:  Limit  the  current  below  thermal  rating  in  operation;

Replacement after thermal deterioration.

•  TEoA: Thermal deterioration of cable and consequent frequent failures.
•  KPI: Reliability and Financial Costs

Knowledge  on  the  strategic  risk  “demand  on  ampacity”  is  immature,  because  the
connection between SP, TS and TEoA is missing. Asset specialists cannot calculate the
temperature  of  the  insulation  of  a  cable  based  on  the  current  measured  at  HV/MV
feeders, because of two reasons:

•  The  current  through  a  MV  cable  ring  is  not  the  same  everywhere.  Some
current  will  flow  to  the  LV  network.  However,  this  current  is  seldom
measured.  As  a  result,  the  current  through  MV  cables  in  the  segments
between MV/LV transformers is often not measured [100].

•  Cooling  conditions  remain  unknown  for  most  MV  cables.  Currently,
thermal rating on cables is based on the worst possible cooling conditions.
In many cases, the cooling conditions are actually better, which allows for
more current through the cable without significant reduction on its lifetime.

Consequently, when the load grows annually, risk managers cannot determine how
much remaining life will be consumed on the cable population (TEoA). Therefore, the
answer to Q4 is “no”. Meanwhile, since the reliability is a convertible KPI, the answer
to Q5 is “no”. Therefore, the risk will fall into the category “Pythia”.

According  to  the  risk  type  “Pythia”,  the  AM  strategy  should  be  based  on
enhancements.  This  means,  at  the  tactical  level,  the  ampacity  and  remaining  life  of
cables should still be determined pessimistically by using the worst thermal conductivity
conditions.

163

However, at the strategic level, the main concern should be developing knowledge,
especially  regarding  the  distribution  of  current  through  cables  and  the  actual  cooling
conditions  along  cables.  After  developing  knowledge  in  these  two  aspects,  thermal
ratings  can  be  specified  for  individual  cables,  or  even  real-time.  The  real-time
technology, called dynamic rating, provides a quantitative relationship between the load
and  the  remaining  life  against  thermal  aging.  Consequently,  the  answer  to  Q4  will
become “Yes”. In other words, the risk type will then have changed to “Cyclops”.

As  a  result,  a  risk-based  strategy  can  be  applied  on  load  dispatch,  as  the  [96]

developed in Predictive Health Model.

Issue  2:  Voltage  Upgrades  on  MV  Cables,  Modelled  with  Substitution-Based
Strategy with Concerns on Flexibility

Renewable energy and international electricity markets can lead to rapid growth of
load  on  electricity  networks.  When  a  much  higher  load  is  expected  in  the  medium  to
long  term,  it  is  economical  to  introduce  a  higher  voltage  level  to  the  network,  so  that
transmission  loss  can  be  reduced.  Normally,  such  upgrades  are  not  only  applied  to  a
single  overhead  line  or  cable  connection.  Several  connections  tend  to  form  rings  to
ensure N-1 reliability, while substations also expand to connect to other voltage levels.

For the medium-voltage network in the Netherlands, the cable rings frequently use
the route of old connections. In this case, old cable systems will normally be replaced
with those of the higher voltage. Such replacements frequently occurred relatively new
cables.  In  [26],  this  situation  is  summarized  as  “strategic  criteria”  which  can  end  the
service life of assets regardless of their technical remaining life.

For  asset  managers,  implementing  a  strategic  criterion  on  replacements  is  a
substitution- based strategy. It is the precaution against an extreme scenario that the load
exceeds  the  ampacity  of  existing  networks.  This  scenario  belongs  to  the  risk  type
“Pandora”, because of the following reasons.

Firstly, future load profiles usually cannot be predicted in a precise way. Thus the

answer to Q4 is “No”.

Secondly,  the  utility  company  has  to  bear  irreversible,  ubiquitous  and  persistent

consequences when the load exceeds the ampacity of existing networks.

164

•

Irreversible  consequences:  when  a  load  needs  to  be  shed  repetitively,
relationships with clients and regulators will be destroyed.

•  Ubiquitous  consequences:  when  the  existing  cables  are  overloaded  in  the
peak period, their reliability will be reduced significantly. As a result, the
overloaded cables need to be replaced in a decade. This is a challenge for
the human resource, outage minutes, municipal permission, etc.

•  Persistent  consequences:  when  the  basic  load  is  reaching  the  ampacity  of
existing  cables,  the  consequences  of  large  amount  of  electricity  loss  in
power  delivery  will  be  borne  by  the  utility  company  until  the  upgrade  is
finished.
Thus the answer to Q5 is “Yes”.

The  benefit  of  higher  voltages  depends  on  one  assumption:  the  load  through  the
replaced connection will reach the expected level. However, as mentioned earlier, this
depends on the installation progress of distributed generators. In addition, demand-side
management,  as  another  emerging  technology,  is  capable  to  reduce  the  load  at  peak
hours. Therefore, the benefit is subjected to high external risks.

Flexible solutions defined in Section 5.3.2 can reduce the impact of external risks.
For 10kV MV cable assets, a flexible solution can be found in applying 20 kV directly
on  10  kV  cables.  Our  test  [90]  has  shown  that  this  is  technically  feasible  when  the
system is refurbished with 20 kV joints. Such refurbishments will typically cost about 1%
of the total cost of replacing a complete cable.

The refurbished cable system does carry extra technical risks. When the voltage is
increased, the cables are likely to deteriorate faster. To control the deterioration rate, the
ampacity rating should be slightly reduced, e.g., 75% current on 20 kV still carries 1.5
times power of 50% current on 10 kV.

Therefore,  the  above  solution  provides  10  kV  cables  with  possibilities  to  comply
with the 20 kV network with costs much smaller than a complete replacement. Though
the capacity will be lower than an entire 20 kV system and more failures will occur on it,
the refurbished cable system provide utility companies with an extra period of several
decades to ensure the load profiles brought by distributed generators. During this period,
the 10 kV cable can be replaced with a real 20 kV cable, when the higher efficiency of
20 kV is definite.

165

5.4 Risk-based AM Strategy

5.4.1 Perform Risk-based AM Strategies at the Strategic Level

The  content  of  risk-based  AM  strategies  at  the  tactical  level  has  been  discussed
extensively  in  Chapter  4.  At  the  tactical  level,  risk-based  AM  means  assessing  risks
quantitatively,  i.e.  estimate  the  expected  performance  statistically,  and  convert  the
expected performance to financial gains/losses.

At the strategic level, the asset manager can use the decision tree in Figure 5.2 to
examine  the  applicability  of  quantitative  risk  assessment.  Only  strategic  risks  of  type
“Cyclops”  and  “Damocles”  can  be  effectively  tackled  by  risk-based  AM  strategies,  in
the ways discussed below:

Mixture of risk-based and precaution-based strategies for type “Cyclops”

The  risk  type  “Cyclops”  is  distinguished  in  Q7  according  to  the  development  of
knowledge,  such  as  those  in  Section  4.4.1.  In  a  strategic  risk  of  type  “Cyclops”,
knowledge can be missing due to two situations:

Situation 1: Ignorance to emerging TS’s and TEoA’s

In  this  situation, the TEoA’s  have  not  been investigated  in  the  past, because  they
had a small effect on KPI’s. However, SP’s such as different operation conditions make
these TEoA’s much more critical.

If theses TEoA’s are considered separately as a strategic risk, they constitute a risk
of  type  “Pythia”.  Therefore,  precaution-based  AM  strategies  should  be  applied.  For
example,  the  minor  aging  mechanisms  are  limited  by  the  redundancy  in  engineering
designs.  When  a  minor  aging  mechanism  is  accelerated  by  future  load  profiles,
knowledge  on its TEoA  should  be  developed.  In  general,  with  more  knowledge,  these
TEoA’s can be managed risk-based.

Situation 2: Insufficient investigation on existing TEoA’s

In  this  situation,  the TEoA’s  have  occurred in history  and  were  identified  by  risk
specialists. From the historical records, inductive reasoning methods such as statistical
analysis provide the probability of TEoA’s without ascertaining their TS’s.

Meanwhile, risk specialists only know a certain number of TS’s that can cause the
TEoA’s.  The  contribution  of  each  TS  remains  unknown,  so  the  correspondent  LC

166

Activities  cannot  be  performed
just-in-time  and
management, this means the maturity level “Standard”.

just-on-site.  For  maintenance

The  solution  is  to  ascertain  the  probability  of  TEoA’s  caused  by  each  TS.
According to these probabilities, decisions can be made at the tactical level to invest on
LC  Activities  to  detect  these  TS’s.  In  maintenance  management,  this  normally  means
introducing condition diagnosis and monitoring technologies.

Risk-based strategy for type “Damocles”

A  strategic  risk  of  type  “Damocles”  has  such  a  low  probability  that  historical
records are not able to support any precise statistical estimation. Such a rare incident is
normally triggered by a chain of TS’s on different assets. Thus, risk-based AM strategy
can be implemented to avoid simultaneous occurrence of the TS’s as follows:

•  Decompose the trigger of the investigated strategic risk into a chain of TS’s

which are known to risk experts.
•  Determine the critical TS’s which are

o  essential to trigger the strategic risk, and
o

likely  to  be  prevented  by  certain  LC  Activities  effectively  and
efficiently.

•  Strengthen LC Activities to reduce the potential of each critical TS.
•  Coordinate LC Activities to reduce the probability that critical TS’s occur

simultaneously.

In summary, risks of type “Cyclops” should be handled through mixing precaution-
based  and  risk-based  measures,  while  risks  of  type  “Damocles”  can  be  analyzed  risk-
based  through  deductive  approaches.  These  principles  will  be  illustrated  with  cases  in
the next section.

5.4.2 Case 2: Risk-based Maintenance Strategy on MV Cable Systems

This section will discuss maintenance management on MV cables. Similar to Case
1  in  Section  5.3.4,  the  case  is  extracted  from  the  experience  with  two  major  Dutch
DSO’s and have been separately introduced in  [101-105].

Different  from  Chapter  4,  the  three  subsections  (entitled  with  “Situations”)
illustrate  the  concern  of  the  strategic  level  on  maintenance  management.  When
connected  in  series,  the  three  subsections  describe  a  storyline  in  which  the  risk  type
evolves from “Pythia” to “Cyclops” and “Damocles” as Section 5.3.3 describes. In the

167

storyline,  the  way  that  precaution-based  and  risk-based  AM  strategies  are  applied  to
handle these strategic risks, can be found.

Normally, maintenance costs are much lower than replacement investments. They
prevent dominant failure mechanisms which appear very frequently on assets. Therefore,
the  TEoA  tackled  by  maintenance  can  be  learned  empirically  at  the  tactical  level.
However, there are still several situations where maintenance management is brought to
the strategic level.

Situation 1: Life analysis to foresee the upcoming replacement wave

As discussed in Chapter 2, the aging of a large population of high-voltage assets is
a  major  SP  to  today’s  electricity  networks.  In  this  case,  the  failure  mechanisms  and
failure modes are the TS’s and TEoA’s.

For  MV  cable  asset  systems,  the  electrical  breakdowns,  as  the  main  TEoA,  are
normally  repairable  with  reasonable  costs.  However,  when  breakdowns  are  occurring
very frequently, the costs of a breakdown will not be the same due to several reasons.

•  The reliability of the system operation is decreased. During the repair, an
N-1 circuit can be operated with reliability of N. This avoids direct loss due
to outages of clients. However, possibilities exist that another failure occurs
on a circuit with its reliability reduced due to maintenance. In addition, the
disconnection of a cable leads to higher load on the others. When another
cable  is  loaded  nearly  100%  of  its  thermal  rating,  its  lifetime  will  be
reduced significantly.

•  The manpower is insufficient. In this situation, field mechanics will likely
finish the repairs more quickly, which will reduce the maintenance quality
and increase the hazard rate in long-term.

•  Municipal  permissions  on  digging  works  could  be  unavailable.  In  urban
area,  especially  where  the  route  is  shared  with  other  infrastructures,
municipal  permissions  limit  the  frequency  and  duration  of  civil  works.
These  civil  works  are  necessary  for  maintenance  and  replacement  on
cables.

168

Situation 1.1: Without life data analysis

If failure and life data are not available for MV cables, the failure frequency in the
upcoming years cannot be predicted. Accordingly, the answer to Q4 is “No”. Meanwhile,
outage  minutes,  manpower  and  municipal  permissions  are  the  CoSR’s  when  an  aged
MV cable population is maintained as today. Therefore, the answer to Q5 is “Yes” and
the SP of aged cable population belongs to type “Pandora”.

The solution is a substitution-based strategy: replace the aged cable systems. When
the  life  data  is  not  available,  the  cables  need  to  be  replaced  according  to  the
recommendation of manufacturers, typically after 50 years of usage.

However,  such  a  replacement  plan  will  trigger  new  SP’s  for  Dutch  DSO’s  and
probably those in other industrialized countries. This is mainly due to the “installation
wave”  from  1960’s  to  1980’s.  In  Netherlands,  one-third  of  existing  MV  cables  were
installed in these two decades, and another 10% were older than that. If they should be
replaced from 2010 to 2030, the CapEx (capital expenditure) of the entire electricity and
gas  distribution  network  would  increase  at  least  by  15%  (compared  to  the  CapEx  at
2012).  In  addition,  the  replacement  wave  will  also  lead  to  shortage  of  reliability,
manpower  and  municipal  permissions  in  the  two  decades.  In  conclusion,  time-based
replacement on MV cable population is a strategic risk of type “Pythia” or “Pandora”,
since  it  leads  to  the  shortage  of  CapEx  (as  KPI  “finance”)  and  other  resources  (as  a
CoSR’s).

Situation 1.2: Life data of poor quality is available

If life data is available, the time frame of replacement wave should be determined
from  the  lifetime  estimated  from  statistics,  instead  of  the  lifetime  recommended  by
manufacturers. Manufacturers set a warranty life from type tests, as we did in Section
3.5. Compared with type tests, failure statistics are closer to the real cumulative stress,
because the failures reflect the operation condition and maintenance quality applied by a
specific utility company.

When life analysis is applied at the strategic level, its purpose is to provide a rough
guideline on how replacement can be performed. For example, in [103], we studied a 10
kV  Paper-Insulated-Lead-Covered  (PILC)  cable  population  of  total  length  25,000  km
through analyzing its failure data. The failure data is only available from 2003 to 2013,
so  the  statistical  method  developed  in  Section  3.4  is  used.  The  statistical  analysis  has

169

shown  that  for  each  km  of  cable,  the  mean  time  to  its  first  failure  will  be  165  years,
whilst  the  standard  deviation  of  this  lifetime  is  75  years.  The  predicted  annual  failure
frequency  due  to  aging  before  the  year  2050  is  lower  than  the  average  annual  failure
frequency  due to  digging  from  2003  to  2013.  From  the  above  result, four  conclusions
have been formed.

1)  The  cable  population  will  be  sufficiently  reliable  until  2050,  given  the
operation  condition  and  maintenance  quality  of  today.  The  replacement
strategy should be performed with life extensions.

2)  The mean lifetime of 165 years provides the asset manager with sufficient
time  to  distribute the  demands  on  CapEx,  outage  minutes,  manpower  and
municipal  permissions  reasonably.  The  strategic  risks  of  type  “Pythia”  or
“Pandora”  caused  by  time-based  replacement  described  Situation  1.1  can
be shifted.

3)  The  standard  deviation  of  75  years  implies  that  the  conditions  of  MV
cables  differ  significantly.  It  indicates  that  life  analysis  cannot  quantify
risks  with  satisfactory  precision.  This  is  Situation  2  of  type  “Cyclops”  in
this  section.  Therefore,  the  solution  is  to  determine  the  remaining  life  of
individual cables through introducing condition diagnosis technologies.
4)  If  the  asset  manager  takes  no  action  to  study  the  condition  of  cables,  the
replacement wave will eventually come. In other words, the consequences
of  “no  action”  have  a  strong  delay  effect.  Thus,  “taking  no  action  after
knowing the mean life is 165 years” is a strategic risk of type “Cassandra”.
See Section 5.5.1 for details.

In  summary,  statistical  life  data  analysis  defines  the  rough  time  frame  of
replacements, which reveal whether the strategic risk of “replacement wave” can occur.
For the studied MV cable population, the analysis moves the strategic risk from the type
“Pandora” to type “Cyclops”.

Additionally,  when  a  large  standard  deviation  is  estimated  for  the  lifetime,
condition diagnosis should be introduced to distribute the replacement reasonably across
several decades. If the asset manager chooses not to investigate the condition of assets,
the  replacement  wave  coming  after  decades  is  a  strategic  risk  of  type  “Cassandra”  to
today’s asset manager.

170

Situation  2:  Improving  failure  data,  as  a  critical  step  towards  risk-based
maintenance

MV  cables  were  maintained  correctively  in  the  Netherlands.  However,  the

necessity to diagnose them is increasing nowadays due to four reasons.

1)  As  mentioned  in  Situation  1,  condition  diagnosis  on  cables  should  be

applied to determine the moment of replacements.

2)  Condition diagnosis can alert the asset manager to impending failures. On
critical assets such as those in shared infrastructure in metropolis, reduction
of frequency and/or outage minutes will be more valuable. See Situation 3
for details.

3)  As  mentioned  in  Issue  2  of  Section  5.3.4  (about  voltage  upgrades),  load
profiles  are  changed  in  the  future.  Compared  with  failure  statistics,
condition diagnosis provides an earlier warning to the effects of changing
load profiles.

4)  As introduced in Section 4.4.1, condition indicators standardize reliability
assessment  on  assets.  When  standardized,  knowledge  is  easier  to  be
transferred to new personnel, so that impact of the retirement of experts on
maintenance quality is less severe.

Nowadays,  a  large  number  of  condition  diagnosis  products  are  available  on  the
market. However, the asset manager needs to answer three questions listed below from
top-down analysis on the asset population.

•  Which diagnostic method is effective and profitable, and therefore should

be introduced?

•  When should a diagnostic method be introduced?
•  Which subpopulation of assets should be diagnosed first?

Regarding MV cables, these three questions cannot be answered by today’s Dutch
DSO’s, because the information sources supporting top-down analysis are insufficient in
three aspects:

1)  Failures  records  contain  little  information  about  their  root  causes,  even
though  root  cause
investigations  have  been  performed  frequently.
Consequently, an asset manager cannot determine which diagnostic method
can be the most effective.

2)  Cumulative  stresses  usually  cannot  be  estimated  for  cable  segments.
Consequently,  the  asset  manager  cannot  determine  which  cable  segments

171

are  more  vulnerable.  For  the  studied  PILC  cable  population,  the  missing
information includes:

o

thermal  stresses:  Load  profiles  and  cooling  conditions  on  MV
cables  are  not  available,  as  mentioned  in  Issue  1  of  Section  5.3.4
(about thermal rating).

o  mechanical  stresses:  Movement  of  ground  (caused  by  traffic,  for
example)  is  another  dominant  stress  factor.  It  is  not  measured  in
today’s MV network.

3)  How  TEoA  affects  CoP’s  is  unknown  to  risk  specialists.  This  situation
happens  more  with  KPI’s  new  to  risk  specialists  (such  as  safety  and
environment)  than  with  the  traditional  KPI’s  (such  as  reliability  and
finance).

o  For  example,  insulation  defects  on  MV  and  LV  cables  can

electrocute people through stray voltage.

Therefore, at the strategic level, the concern of risk-based maintenance strategy is
to  make  the  above  information  available  to  the  tactical  level.  When  the  above
information is ready, at the tactical level, the profit of applying a specific diagnostic tool
to a certain asset can be determined.

Situation  3:  Preventing  unregistered
maintenance

failure  scenarios

in  risk-based

Through  investing  in  condition  diagnosis  technologies,  risk-based  maintenance
strategy controls failures which (1) have been recorded in history, and (2) have affected
the KPI “reliability”. Therefore, these failures are familiar to maintenance personnel and
risk specialists.

Meanwhile,  some  failure  scenarios  have  not  been  registered  by  risk  specialists  as
failure modes because they rarely occur. As a typical example, large-scale blackouts can
be linked to local failures as a registered TEoA (e.g. breakdown on a cable). The range
of failure increases because the protection or redundancy mechanism fails to work, for
example, in the below situations:

•  The protection system fails to respond, since it is incorrectly configured or

temporarily disabled.

•  The connection is temporarily of N reliability, due to extreme power flows

or maintenance on parallel cables.

172

An unregistered failure scenario with large consequence is studied at the strategic
level, as a risk  of type  “Damocles”.  In  order  to  solve  an  unregistered failure scenario,
asset  managers  should  reduce  the  chance  that  the  registered  TEoA’s  escalate.  For
example,  for  a  cable  system  with  poor  health  index,  reinforcements  can  be  applied  as
below.

•  The  protection  system  over  the  deteriorated  cable  system  should  be  kept
online. Moreover, self-healing configurations should firstly be installed on
these aged connections.

•  Load dispatch plans, such as congestion management or dynamic rating on
young cables, should be ready when an aged cable is heavily loaded.

These  reinforcements  are  based  on  health  indices  as  the  output  from  risk-based
maintenance  at  the  tactical  level.  They  constitute  supplements  to  the  risk-based
maintenance in order to reduce catastrophic risk potentials.

5.5 Discourse-based Management

5.5.1 Discourse-based Management for Risk of Type “Cassandra” and

“Medusa”
Discourse-based management is applied to the people who cause the stimuli, who
suffer the consequences or who handle the countermeasures. When facing strategic risks
of AM, discourse-based management is expected to reach some of the goals below:

(1)  Through reorganization and discourses, those who cause the SP or handle
the  LC  Activities  are  motivated  to  take  action to reduce  risks,  or  at  least,
conscious of the strategic risks.

(2)  Through  communication  with  those  who  suffer  the  mental  consequences

(as the main UC), their negative psychology can be relieved.

(3)  Through communication with those who suffer the UC, asset manager can
identify the LC Activities or technology which can effectively reduce UC.

Specifically, Goal (1) aims to solve risks of type “Cassandra”. For the case of risk-
maintenance  introduced  in  Situation  2  of  Section  5.4.2,  the  first  goal  means  that  the
knowledge development should be handled by the same legal entity as maintenance &
replacement  management.  Otherwise,  the  knowledge  developer  tends  to  plan  the

173

replacement with less caution, because the economic output from such a plan will not be
revealed within a short term.

Goal  (2)  and  Goal  (3)  of  discourse-based  management  aim  to  tackle  UC’s  in  the
risks  of  type  “Medusa”.  UC’s,  such  as  NIMBY  phenomena,  are  difficult  to  be
objectively quantified as KPI’s. For the asset manager, an UC can only be understood
when  it  is  interpreted  as  “an  additional  LC  Activity”  or  “one  LC  Activity  as  the
alternative of the other”, as Goal (3) described. The utility company is normally willing
to  perform  the  alternative  LC  Activity  if  it  is  cheaper.  Nevertheless,  the  additional  or
more  expensive  LC  Activity  will  not  be  performed  until  (1)  an  AM  policy  offers  a
special budget to do so, or (2) another AM strategy select the LC Activity to gain other
KPI’s. If these two situations are not applicable, the only measure to reduce the UC is
the communication itself as Goal (2) described.

5.5.2 Relationship between Discourse-based Management and Asset

Management
Section  5.5.1  has  shown  that  when  discourse-based  management  is  performed,
controlling LC Activities or budgets is not the main focus. In contrast, the operational
level and the tactical level of AM are constantly controlling the LC Activities and the
budgets. Hence, the operational level and the tactical level of AM are barely involved in
discourse-based  management.  Accordingly,  we  do  not  consider  discourse-based
management as a type of AM strategy.

Meanwhile,  the  strategic  level  of  AM  supports  discourse-based  management,
mainly  through  the  AM  information  system.  AM  information  is  the  historical  log  of
TS’s,  LC  Activities  and  TEoA’s.  Normally  speaking,  the  original  purpose  of
establishing  AM  information  system  is  to  support  knowledge  development  within  the
asset  management  department.  However,  today’s  data  management  technology  makes
AM  information  system  accessible  to  employees  and  stakeholders  outside  the  asset
management department. Therefore, discourse-based management will benefit from AM
information system in the two following ways.

Firstly,  in  Goal  (3),  risk  specialists  should  determine  the  assets,  TEoA’s  or  LC
Activities responsible for the UC’s. However, in the feedbacks from stakeholders, UC’s
are ambiguously described with time, location and visual/audio disturbances. Coupling
asset  profiles,  O&M  logs  with  feedback  from  stakeholders  is  a  high  demand  on  AM

174

information system. Section 5.5.3 will use the failure information system as an example
to explain the demands.

Secondly,  in  Goal  (2),  the  asset  owner  should  convince  stakeholders  that  he  is
making efforts to reduce UC’s. The AM information system can facilitate the purpose in
two  ways.  Firstly,  the  total  investment  on  extra  LC  Activities  and  more  expensive
alternative LC Activities can be reported as a proof of such efforts. Secondly, predictive
or real-time information on condition, maintenance, outage, load shedding and EM field
can be provided stakeholders to avoid the unwanted UC’s.

Summary

The  AM  information  system  is  the  bridge  between  asset  management  and
discourse-based  management.  AM  information  system  is  initially  constructed  for
knowledge  development  rather  than  discourse-based  management.  To  be  utilized  in
discourse-based  management,  an  AM  information  system  should  be  restructured  in
special  ways.  The  next  section  will  illustrate  this  reconstruction  in  a  case  about  the
failure information system in the Netherlands.

5.5.3 Case 3: Vision on Future Failure Information Systems – How It Can

Facilitate Communication with Stakeholders
Based  on  the  Situation  2  of  Case  2  in  Section  5.4.2,  this  section  will  further
introduce  failure  recording  procedures  in  today’s  Dutch  DSO  based  on  our  own  work
experience (e.g. in [105]), and discuss the long-term evolvement of failure information
systems.

Traditionally, failures are recorded technically and internally by network operators
and service providers. For example, when a failure is repaired correctively, the network
operator  will  notice  the  breakdowns,  determine  the  failed  components,  identify  the
rough location of failure and reconfigure the circuit before/after the repair. The service
provider will send mechanics to the field, search for the exact location, transport spare
parts, set up safety barriers and repair the component. After the repair, experts from the
service providers are likely to investigate on the root cause of the failure. When these
activities  are  registered  in  a  standard  way,  they  form  the  primary  failure  information
system.

The failure information can be used and supplemented by many stakeholders. For
example,  client  services  often  establish  failure  information  system  earlier  than  asset

175

managers, since they use failure information to respond to clients about the progress of
outages. The feedback from clients may also be recorded to measure the consequences
of these failures. Moreover, the public relationship department of utility companies may
also  use  the  system  to  communicate  with  municipalities  and  media,  especially  when
failures such as an explosion are interfering in local lives. These usages constitute the
early form of “discourse-based management” on failures.

According  to  our  work  experience,  in  today’s  Dutch  DSO’s,  these  usages  are

supported by information system based on spread-sheets. It means that:

•  All failure information is included in a single data table in a database. This
data table has typically over 100 columns. These columns cover all inputs
from  the  three  main  users  of  failure  information  system,  namely  the
network operator, service provider and client service.

•  All failures are recorded in the same format. The format prevents a specific
failure mode from being described with more details. For example, Dutch
DSO’s  record  time-to-failure  in  the  format  of  intervals,  as  Section  3.4
mentioned. For cable joints, the missing deployment information makes the
interval  life  data  reasonable.  However,  for  cables  and  transformers,  the
deployment data is available and the interval life data should be avoided.
•  All  users  can  access  the  same  amount  of  information,  regardless  whether
to
is  relevant.  Technical  knowledge
the
understand  the  information.  This  prevents  client  services  or  public
relationship  department  from  offering  properly  interpreted  technical
explanation on failures.

is  necessary

information

We  expect  that  failure  information  systems  will  expand  significantly  in  the  next
decades. Firstly, the asset manager will become the regular user of failure information
system.  As  emphasized  in  Chapter  4,  Section  5.3.3  and  Section  5.4.2,  failure  records
perform  a  critical  role  in  supporting  AM  decisions  on  maintenance  and  replacements.
Secondly, when assets are maintained risk-based, knowledge providers such as the asset
specialists who interpret condition data for the asset manager, will become main users of
the software system. After interpreting condition data, knowledge providers will predict
failures  probabilistically  with  failure  modes,  and  add  these  “future  failures”  to  the
system.  Thirdly,  in  a  smart  grid,  the  clients  (especially  those  that  operate  distributed
generators),  the  regulator,  the  municipality  and  other  public  sectors  (such  as

176

environmentalists)  will  not  only  ask  for  failure  causes  after  failures,  but  also  keep
receiving reliability information at real time.

In  order  to  include  the  additional  information  stated  above,  we  recommend
structuring  future  failure  information  systems  in  new  ways  described  below.  The
description  uses  terms  from  object-oriented  software  engineering.  Ideas  such  as
standardization, encapsulation, polymorphisms and extendibility are commonly applied
in information systems in other sectors, but not yet in DSO’s.

•  Standard  tags:  Identification  tags  of  asset  groups,  asset  systems,  assets,
failures,  failure  modes,  outages  and  locations  should  be  unique  in  all
information systems of the asset owner. This enables failure information to
be  coupled  with  operation/maintenance
records  or  geographical
information, so that the users can investigate root causes or non-technical
consequences of failures.

•  Polymorphisms  for  different  users  of  the  failure  information  system  to
describe failures. How a user describes a failure depends on (1) how and to
what extent he is involved in the failure and (2) how much knowledge he
has  about  failure.  Future  failure  information  systems  should  provide
different users with different methods to describe a failure from the aspect
of asset specialists, network operators, field mechanics, clients, etc.

•  Encapsulation  for  users  of  different  knowledge  backgrounds.  Similarly,
which  characteristics  users  need  to  learn  about  a  failure  depends  on  how
they  are  affected  by  the  failure  as  a  stakeholder.  Failure  information
systems  should  hide  technical  details  to  a  user  according  to  (1)  his
knowledge  capabilities  and  (2)  his  relationship  with  DSO  (e.g.  internal
worker, contractor, shareholder, regulator, or other external stakeholders).
•  Compatibility  for  continuous  improvements.  Failure  records  can  be
preserved  for  decades.  In  this  period,  new  sensors  and  knowledge  can
describe failures with more details. Failure records and failure information
systems of different age should be compatible with each other. This means
that new systems should deal with failure records with a mixture of old and
new  formats,  while  new  failure  records  can  still  be  processed  in  old
methods. For example, failure mode “aging of cable” can be further divided
into “thermal aging” or “mechanical aging”. When a search on failures due

177

to  “thermal  aging”  is  performed,  the  result  should  also  include  the  old
failures due to “aging”.

When  the  above  four  principles  are  established  for  future  failures  information
systems, the asset manager can understand the attitude of different stakeholders towards
a specific failure mode. From the perspective of AM, this helps to identify new KPI’s
through  analyzing  the  feedbacks  from  stakeholders.  From  the  perspective  of  client
services  and  public  relationship  management,  the  information  system  should  provide
more  predictability  and  transparency  on  failures.  Based  on  the  information,  proper
communication with stakeholders and vice versa can be arranged to prevent or improve
their negative attitude towards electrical infrastructures.

5.6 Summary

This chapter has developed a theory to manage risks at the strategic level of AM.

The  theory  firstly  distinguishes  “strategic  risks”  from  normal  risks  which  are
managed at the tactical or operational level. The strategic risks have more components
than normal risks, so they deal with more complex causalities than normal risks do. This
complexity provides “strategic  risks” with  nine  new  characteristics. They are “asset
population”,  “unknown  possibilities”,  “deductive
risks”,
“insufficient  knowledge  on  technology”,  “delay  effect”,  “consequence  on  scarce
resources”, “insufficient knowledge on performance” and “constructivism”.

learning”,  “external

Based on these nine characteristics, Section 5.2 develops a decision tree in Figure
5.3 to classify strategic risk into six types: “Pythia”, “Pandora”, “Cyclops”, “Damocles”,
“Cassandra”  and  “Medusa”.  The  classification  tree  decides  that  each  type  focuses  on
one or several characteristics as its main feature.

The  main  features  decide  that  the  strategic  risks  should  be  managed  in  three
different  methodologies:  precaution-based  AM  strategy,  risk-based  AM  strategy  and
discourse-based management.

The precaution-based AM strategies implement enhancements or substitutions to
tackle  risks  of  type  “Pythia”  and  “Pandora”.  For  the  asset  manager,  the  difference
between  enhancements  and  substitutions  are  the  costs  of  changes.  Solutions  with  low

178

costs  of  changes  provide  the  asset  manager  with  extra  flexibility,  when  the
consequences  of  risks  will  be  clarified  as  time  passes.  Meanwhile,  developing
knowledge  on  performance,  i.e. the  data  and  rules  interpreting  technical  consequences
as  KPI’s,  is  also  an  important  concern  of  precaution-based  AM  strategies.  In  Section
5.3.4, we use this theory to model the load management in MV cable systems.

The risk-based AM strategies balance investment in quantitative risk management
at the tactical level. Its concern at the strategic level can be summarized in two aspects.

Firstly,  when  faced  with  a  risk  of  type  “Cyclops”,  developing  knowledge  on
technology helps to improve the quality of risk analysis. As a result, the scheduling and
planning  of  LC  Activities  can  be  optimized,  as  the  knowledge  maturity  model  has
shown in Section 4.5.

Secondly, a risk of type “Damocles” concerns the rare but catastrophic scenarios.
These  scenarios  should  not  be  identified  empirically,  but  deductively.  The  triggers  of
these scenarios can be decomposed as simultaneous occurrence of technical stimuli of
multiple normal risks. Through controlling some of the normal risks or preventing their
simultaneous  occurrence,  the  catastrophic  consequences  can  be  effectively  reduced  to
normal level.

The case in Section 5.4.2 applies the above theory in maintenance management of
MV cables. The case has also shown that through developing knowledge, the strategic
risk of “large population of aged cables” evolves from “Pythia”, firstly to “Cyclops” and
finally to “Damocles”. This is a typical roadmap when risk management is introduced in
asset management.

The  discourse-based  management  reorganizes  personnel  or  communicates  with
stakeholders to deal with risks of type “Cassandra” and “Medusa”. These activities are
not the responsibility of asset manager or service provider. However, asset information
system, which is originally built to support knowledge development, can be restructured
to  facilitate  discourse-based  management.  A  number  of  recent  documents  about  AM
such  as  [106]  tend  to  acknowledge  the  management  of  asset  information  system  as
another  AM  strategy  called  “information  strategy”.  Section  5.5.3  narrates  future
development of failure information systems in distribution networks.

179

5.7 Conclusions and Discussions

The  strategic  level  of  AM  investigates  risks  on  large  asset  population  or  asset
portfolio  from  the  perspective  of  the  asset  owner.  The  risk  analyst  should  take  the
requirements  of  all  major  stakeholders  on  the  assets  and  their  LC  Activities  into
consideration.  Through  modelling  these  wider  considerations  as  strategic  risks,  we
conclude that the responsibility of the strategic level of AM is to shield LC Activities
and routine risk management at the operational and tactical level from the non-technical
requirements from stakeholders. To accomplish this responsibility, several goals need to
be achieved.

•  Limit the influence of some stakeholders with precautions.
•  Control  costs  of  changes  and  introduce  flexibility  within  investments  on

precautions.

•  Develop  knowledge  on  performance  and  technology,  so  that  risk-based

strategy can be performed at the tactical level

•  Generate  knowledge  on  rare  but  catastrophic  scenarios  through  deductive

methods

•  Develop  and  maintain  asset  information  system  to  support  knowledge

developments, as well as discourse-based management

The above goals are derived from AM strategies. In this chapter, the AM strategies
are described separately in different cases. However, in reality, multiple AM strategies
will  apply  on  the  same  asset  population,  while  one  technology  or  LC  Activity  can
implement  multiple  AM  strategies  at  the  same  time.  These  two  issues  will  be  further
discussed in the next chapter.

180

Chapter 6 Integrate Asset Management

Strategies with Stakeholder

Requirements

In  Chapter  5,  it  was  studied  the  ways  in  which  a  large  asset  population  can  be
affected by stakeholder’s requirements, as well as how AM strategies can be developed
to  fulfill  these  requirements.  The  results  revealed  two  important  features  of  strategic
asset management (AM):

•  Strategic  AM  is  multi-objective.  Some  of  the  performance  indicators
cannot be translated into a financial gain or loss, and therefore they need to
be treated separately. In contrast, all performance indicators at the tactical
level can in fact be translated into a financial gain or loss.

•  AM  strategies  should  be  implemented  for  long-term.  During  this  period,
knowledge and information can be gained (or lost), so that the maturity of
risk management at the tactical level can be improved (or reduced).

The  above  two  features  can  be  observed  when  a  single  strategic  risk  is  handled.
Meanwhile,  the  strategic  level  of  AM  has  another  important  feature  when  multiple
strategic risks are handled at the same time.

•  When  multiple  strategic  risks  are  influencing  the  same  asset  population,
their technical stimuli and effects interact with each other. As a result, the
causalities  in  multiple  strategic  risks  have  a  shape  which  is  NOT  a
straightforward “sequence”, but as a mesh.

In this situation, there is not one specific AM strategy that can be chosen as a solution to
deal  with  one  specific  strategic  risk.  Instead,  AM  strategies  should  be  combined,  in
order  to  counteract  all  strategic  risks  which  occur  with  respect  to  the  same  asset
population.

In  management  theories,  the  interacting  causalities  can  be  modelled  as  complex
systems,  while  the  insufficient  knowledge  or  information  on  a  certain  factor  (e.g.  a

181

failure mode or a performance indicator) is a form of uncertainty. Therefore, analysis on
multiple  strategic  risks  in  electrical  infrastructure  assets  requires  a  method  capable  of
deal  with  both  complexities  and  uncertainties.  Meanwhile,  the  method  should  be
qualitative,  so  that  information  strategies  or  data  acquisition  systems  are  not  a
prerequisite but the objects of decision in strategic AM.

The method to be developed in this chapter is a modified version of system diagram
method. System diagram method is originally a tool for qualitative analysis on complex
systems.

In this chapter, we use system diagram method in two ways different from earlier
researches. Firstly, our applications are deeply embedded into the technical complexity,
which  is  one  of  the  main  difficulties  to  analyze  electrical  infrastructure  assets  as
summarized in Section 1.2.3. As a result, the output can be directly related to technical
solutions. Secondly, we integrate system diagram method with our knowledge maturity
model  which  was  developed  in  Chapter  4.  From  the  aspect  of  management
methodologies, the complexities and the uncertainties will be managed at the same time.
From  the  aspect  of  practice,  our  method  produces  a  roadmap  of  developing  data
acquisition  systems  (modelled  as  information  strategies).  This  is  a  part  frequently
missing from today’s strategic infrastructure asset management.

The  sections  are  organized  as  follows.  In  Section  6.1,  we  identify  the  generic
patterns  where  strategic  risks  depend  on  each  other.  These  patterns  are  not  only
described in text,  but  also shown in the  form  of  system  diagrams  as  a tool for  system
analysis. Those who are familiar with complex systems can skip this section.

Then,  in  Section  6.2,  a  complete  procedure  of  our  system  diagram  method  is
developed. We give the symbols in the system diagram new  meanings with respect to
asset management. Accordingly, the interdependent strategic risks are integrated in the
same  system  diagram,  from  which  risk  analysts  can  check  whether  the  proposed  AM
strategies can solve all of these risks.

Moreover,  the  knowledge  maturity  model  developed  in  Chapter  4  is  generalized
from maintenance management to all components of strategic risks. We use the colors to
indicate the readiness of knowledge on strategic risks in our system diagram. Based on
that, risk analysts can check whether existing knowledge can support a risk-based AM
strategy. If no, a roadmap towards the risk-based AM strategy can be developed.

182

In Section 6.3, a case study about medium voltage (MV) cables is performed, based
on the background information introduced in Section 5.3.4 and 5.4.2. Through applying
system diagram, the coordination between AM strategies in Section 5.3.4 and 5.4.2 will
be clearly shown.

6.1 Interdependencies at the Strategic Level of Asset

Management

6.1.1 Complex Causalities in Strategic Risks

In Chapter 5, we discussed how strategic risks can be managed with AM strategies.
These strategic risks are modelled through extending the causal chains of normal risks.
The causal chain of a normal risk is typically a sequence from “technical stimuli (TS)”,
“life  cycle  activities  (LC  Activities)”,  “technical  consequences  on  assets  (TEoA)”  to
“consequence on performance (CoP). Through changing or influencing TS, TEoA or LC
Activities,  the  asset  owner  can  manage  the  strategic  risks.  From  the  methodology  of
such  changes  or  influences,  two  types  of  AM  strategies,  namely  precaution-based  and
risk-based, can be defined.

In Chapter 5, each strategic risk is composed of a sequence of causalities. This is a
simplified situation. In the real case, a main causal chain is normally extracted from a
complex “mesh” of causalities. In such extraction, risk analysts assume that a number of
conditions  prevent the  disturbance  from  other  causalities. These  disturbances  typically
affect  the  resources  necessary  to  handle  the  main  causal  chain,  such  as  supply  chain,
manpower, political permissions, etc. For example, the cost of a spare part is normally
decided by its wholesale price. However, if the wholesale market is shrinking, the price
is  subjected  to  risks  of  increases.  Specifically,  when  the  mechanical  relay  control
systems are being replaced by digital ones, the number of suppliers is likely to decrease.
Since fewer competitors will remain at the supply side, the price of these apparatus is
likely to go up.

Therefore, the scale of a strategic risk depends not only on the main causal chain,
but also on the disturbances to the chain. For the case of mechanical relay control, its
popularity  in  African  markets  ensures  a  stable  price  on  the  international  market.
Accordingly,  when  life  extension  strategy  is  applied  to  mechanical  relay  protection
controls, attention should be paid to its international supply chain.

183

This chapter aims to model the interdepending causalities in strategic AM. Before
the modelling, sections 6.1.3 to 6.1.6 summarize four patterns in which ways causalities
interdepend on each other. These patterns will be used in system diagrams in section 6.2
to identify the conditions as to when an AM strategy is needed.

6.1.2 Causality in System Diagrams

In  Figure  5.2  of  the  previous  chapter,  we  connected  components  of  one  strategic
risk with arrow lines. These arrow lines symbolize causalities. In a system diagram, the
components of multiple strategic risks are connected in the same way – each arrow line
begins  and  ends  at  a  component.  Such  a  system  diagram  is  a  type  of  directed  graph,
while the lines and components are respectively termed as “edges” and “nodes” [107].

A risk component is best studied by risk analysts when it is measurable and internal
(see  Section  5.1.1,  the  properties  of  normal  risk  for  details).  In  our  system  diagrams,
such a risk component is called an internal  business  factor. It is one type of node in
our  system  diagram.  The  other  types  of  nodes  will  be  introduced  in  Section  6.2.1.  An
internal business factor is symbolized as a circle with solid border as Figure 6.1 shows.

Additionally,  a  plus  or  minus  symbol  (in  a  square  bracket  for  visibility)  will  be
added above an edge, to indicate whether the correlation is positive or negative. When
the correlation is positive, Factor A and B tend to change in the same direction. On the
contrary,  negative  correlation  means  changing  in  the  opposite  direction.  As  a  type  of
edge,  the  arrow  line  further  indicates  that  Factor  A  is  stimulating  such  changes.  The
other types of edges will be introduced in Section 6.2.3.

A

[＋]

B

A

[－]

B

Figure 6.1: Positive (left) or negative (right) causality relationship from international business

factor A to B.10

10 From Figure 6.1 to 6.15, the gray background aims to draw the boundary of the (subparts of)
system diagrams.

184

The  relationship  between  two  business  factors  (symbolized  as  an  arrow  line  in
Figure  6.1)  can  exist  in  different  situations  in  asset  management.  They  are  illustrated
briefly below.

•  Situation (1): The relationship can be determined by scientific rules and/or
engineering  designs,  especially  when  two  connected  business  factors  are
technical parameters. Due to their engineering nature, they are unlikely to
be changed by stakeholder requirements.
Example:  The  causality  from  stress  factors  to  aging  mechanisms  and
failure rate is a technical causality, as was shown in Chapter 4.

•  Situation  (2): The relationship means that mandatory consequences, such
as  penalties,  are  applied  to  technical  events,  such  as  an  outage.  Such  a
relationship  is  typically  decided  by  the  authority  and  regulator,  thus
unlikely to change in short term. But in long term, political decisions, under
the requirement of stakeholders, are still possible to change the rule.
Example: Many performance indicators are converted as financial losses.
The converting rules, such as the risk consequence matrix in Section 2.2.4,
depend on the regulation or the law.

•  Situation (3): The relationship represents a causality which is insufficiently
understood. Human knowledge can only confirm the existence of causality,
but not quantify the relationship. Section 6.2.5 uses the knowledge maturity
model in Chapter 4 to label these relationships in a system diagram.
Example:  In  technology,  an  aging  mechanism  unfamiliar  to  service
providers;
the  environmental
consequences of engineering constructions

in  management,  public  concerns  on

•  Situation (4): The relationship is weak. It can be a causality which is valid
only  in  certain  conditions,  or  an  empirically  identified  correlation.  Such
relationships  can  be  easily  affected  by  business  environments  and
stakeholders.  In  Table  6.2,  we  use  a  special  symbol  for  the  conditional
causality.
An  example  will  be  shown  in  Section  6.1.5  with  “Loop  with  negative
feedback”.

6.1.3 Multiple Consequences and Joint Effects

The network of nodes and edges will contain branches, typically in two patterns as

studied below.

185

Multiple consequences

The  first  pattern appears  when  a  stimulus  has  multiple  different  consequences,  as
Figure 6.2 shows. Typically, technical effects on assets (TEoA) tend to have effects on
multiple performance indicators.

A

B

C

Figure 6.2: Factor A has two consequences, Factors B and C, in a system diagram.

It  is  common  for  risk  analysts  to take  one  branch, such  as “A  to  B”,  as  the  main
causal chain. However, there are three strategies which help to take the effect of C into
consideration, which makes the risk analysis more comprehensive.

•  Accumulate knowledge on Factor C:

When  the  relationship  between  Factor  C  and  performance  indicators  is
unclear, it is inappropriate to ignore Factor C. If Factor C is significant but
ignored,  Factor  A  will  present  as  a  strategic  risk  of  type  “Pythia”.
Accordingly, the build knowledge on Factor C is a natural precaution.

•  Convert Factor C into Factor B:

When Factor B and C are financially convertible, they can be merged into
one performance indicator, the financial gains/losses. The risk consequence
matrix is the typical example of such mergence.
If both Factor B and C affect another Factor D, a feed-forward pattern will
be formed. Section 6.1.6 will discuss feed-forward pattern in detail.

•  Reduce Factor C to a negligible level:

Precaution-based  strategy  can  be  directly  implemented  to  reduce  the
influence of Factor C to a very low level. In this case, Factor C will not be
managed risk-based. An advantage of this strategy is to decouple “A to B”
from other causal chains in the same system diagram, so that they can be
analyzed separately. See the next subsection “joint effects” for details.

Example: The functional part and the side-effects are the two technical consequence of
a failure mode on the assets, as Section 4.1.2 has introduced. Each part will further lead

186

to the losses of performance indicators, which will finally be converted into financially
measured losses.

Joint effects

The  second  pattern  “joint  effect”  appears  when  multiple  stimuli  affect  the  same
factor. Figure 6.3 and 6.4 show the two roles that Factor B can play when “A to C” is a
main causal chain.

In  Figure  6.3,  Factor  B  is  controlled  by  an  AM  strategy  to  reduce  the  effect  of
Factor  A  on  C.  A  typical  example  could  be  understood  as:  Factor  A  is  an  aging
mechanism, Factor B is the maintenance activity and Factor C is the failure frequency.
In this example, the correlation between B and C is negative and that between A and C
is  positive.  Such  control  can  be  realized  in  precaution-based  or  risk-based  ways,
depending on the knowledge on A, B and C.

A

B

AM
strategy

[＋]

[－]

C

Figure 6.3: Factor A and B have opposite effects on Factor C, while Factor B is controlled by an

AM strategy.

When  Factor  B  is  NOT  controlled  by  an  AM  strategy,  the  effect  of  Factor  B  on
Factor  C  will  disturb  the  main  causal  chain  from  A  to  C.  This  typically  occurs  when
multiple failure modes are competing with each other (See Figure 4.1 for more details
about failure modes). Figure 6.4 (a) shows an example: the causal chain from A to C is
coupled with another causal chain from D via B to E. In these cases, when the causality
from  B  to  C  is  ignored,  both  causal  chains  will  be  insufficiently  studied.  Thus,  the
causal chains are likely to form a strategic risk of the type “Cyclops”. In other words,
the risk is not completely managed.

To solve this problem, the asset manager can take two actions.

•  Record the contribution of Factor A or B to Factor C for individual assets,

events or incidents:
It  aims  to  build  risk-based  management  on  both  causal  chains.  When  the
contribution of each causal link is clear, Factor C can be split according to

187

its stimuli, as Figure 6.4 (b) shows. As a result, the causal chains decouple
from each other, and risk-based management can be performed separately
on either side.

•  Reduce Factor B to the minimum possible level:

This  is  the  precaution-based  strategy  on  the  causal  chain  from  D  to  E.
However, its main purpose is to minimize the effect of B on C, so that the
causal chain from A to C can be managed separately.

D

B

E

A

Figure 6.4: Two causal chains A-C and D-B-E are coupled in (a). In (b), they are decoupled in a

risk-based way, while in (c) the coupling effect of Factor B on Factor C is limited by

the AM strategy.

In summary, the presence of “multiple consequences” and “joint effects” lead to the
coupling of one causal chain with another, and therefore complexity is introduced into
strategic  AM.  We  have  suggested  solutions  to  reduce  the  complexity,  or  the  coupling
effect of these two patterns. For either pattern, the causal chain can be decoupled in a
risk-based way. Alternatively, precaution-based AM strategy can be applied to minimize
the effect one causal chain on the other.

6.1.4 Changeability

Interchangeable consequences

A  special  situation  of  “multiple  consequences”  is  the  situation  of  changeable
consequences. The asset manager can enlarge one consequence and reducing the other
through  choosing  specific  LC  Activities.  In  other  words,  the  asset  manager  can
manually balance different consequences of the same stimulus.

188

Figure 6.5(a) uses the dashed arrow line to indicate the changeability. When both
ends  of  the  line  have  arrows,  Factors  B  and  C  are  interchangeable.  However,  such
changes do have costs. When one substitution, e.g. Factor B in Figure 6.5(a), is closer to
the current state of the investigated asset population, the arrow at its end is smaller. This
means this substitution requires less change costs, or can be considered more “flexible”
than the other substitution, e.g. Factor C in Figure 6.5(a), as Section 5.3.2 has defined.

Example: In Section 5.3.4, two interchangeable consequences of “demand on capacity”
were  introduced.  They  are  “thermal  rating  on  ampacity”  and  “voltage  upgrades”.  In
strategic AM, (inter)changeability relies heavily on the technical design of asset systems.
According  to  the  description  in  Section  5.3.4,  the  smaller  arrow  points  to  “thermal
rating on ampacity” rather than “voltage upgrades”.

Interchangeable stimuli

Similarly,  the  stimuli  in  “joint  effects”  can  also  be  changeable,  a  situation  that  is
schematically depicted in Figure 6.5(b) . Typically, these stimuli are LC Activities. The
costs to introduce and perform these LC Activities determine their flexibility, and hence
the size of arrows at the end of dashed line in Figure 6.5(b).

Example: In Section 5.4.2, the case of maintenance on medium voltage cables has been
introduced.  The  two  maintenance  methods,  namely  the  reactive  method  and  the
proactive  method,  are  interchangeable.  The  larger  arrow  points  to  the  proactive
maintenance, since it represents the future state which requires long-term investments.

A

(a)

A

[＋]

[＋]

[＋]

[＋]

B

C

C

B

(b)

Figure 6.5: Interchangeability between (a) two consequences of the same stimulus in, or (b) two

stimuli with the same consequence.

6.1.5 Loops and Feedbacks

A loop refers to a path which begins and ends at the same node in a system diagram,
as for example the route from A  via B, C, D and then back to A in Figure 6.6. In the
field of control theory [108], a loop is a pattern of special interest, because it determines
whether the system under investigation is stable.

189

In a system diagram, the causal links are qualitatively simplified as “positive” and
“negative”. Quantitative relationships are not included due to lack of knowledge or data.
Therefore, the stability of the system can only be studied with two simple criteria.

Loop with positive feedback

If  there  are  an  even  number  of  “negative”  links  in  a  loop,  a  positive  feedback  is
formed. The loop in Figure 6.6 is a positive feedback. According to the control theory
[108], a positive feedback will magnify any disturbance occurred on the loop, therefore
it tends to cause system instability. If a positive feedback loop is identified within the
system, it needs to be controlled. One typical technique of such control is to introduce
an  extra  link  to  offset  the  gain  generated  by  the  positive  feedback.  In  the  example  in
Figure 6.6, an AM strategy exerts such control on Factor B.

The control of a positive feedback loop can be precaution-based or risk-based. On
one  hand,  the  precaution-based  strategy  restricts  the  target  node  (see  definitions  in
Section 6.2.3) to the least effective level (reduce Factor B to minimum level in Figure
6.6).  Such  a  restriction  only  needs  to  qualitatively  measure  the  target  node,  without
knowledge on the other nodes.

On  the  other  hand,  the  risk-based  strategy  controls  the  nodes  according  to  their
influence  on  performance  indicators  (such  as  Factor  E  in  Figure  6.6).  The  causality
chain  between  the  target  node  and  the  performance  indicators  (Factor  B,  C  and  E  in
Figure 6.6) needs to be quantitatively understood.

A

[＋]

B

[－]

AM
strategy

[＋]

[＋]

D

[＋]

C

E

Figure 6.6: A positive feedback loop A-B-C-D is formed, while Factor B is controlled to avoid

instable output of the loop.

Example:  In  Figure  6.10,  we  will  explain  a  very  common  positive  feedback  loop  in
electricity network. It can be simply described as heavier load on components – thermal
aging of components – global wear out of components – higher failure frequency in the
component  population  –  longer  duration  of  N-1  reliability  –  heavier  load  on  the
(redundant) components

190

Loop with negative feedback

If there are an odd number of “negative” links in a loop, a negative feedback, such
as  the  loop  A-B-C-A  in  Figure  6.7(a),  is  formed.  The  values  of  the  nodes  in  the  loop
stabilize, i.e. converge to a certain “steady state” without extra influence.

For  example,  when  an  investment  requires  a  scarce  resource  from  the  market,  a
negative feedback loop will be formed. In this case, Factor A, B and C in Figure 6.7 can,
for  example,  respectively  be  “number  of  investments  (e.g.  new  substations)”,
“consumed scarce resource (e.g. land)” and “price or scarcity of the resource (land)”.

C

D

[－]

[＋]

A

[＋]

B

[－]

AM
strategy

A

[＋]

B

(a)

(b)

Figure 6.7: A negative feedback loop in (a) can be simplified as conditional causal link (b), given

that AM strategy is limiting the effect of the feedback.

The  stability  of  a  negative  feedback  loop  can  be  influenced  by  other  business
factors. For example, in Figure 6.7(a), Factor D can be the market. The scarcity of the
resource  is  low,  when  the  consumed  resource  can  be  quickly  supplied  by  the  market
without significant increase of price (shown as the link from D to C). In this situation,
the effect of feedback is so insignificant such that the loop can be simplified as a one-
direction causal link in Figure 6.7(b).

However, in Figure 6.7(b), a double line is visible and this implies that the direction
is  conditional.  When  Factor  D  is increasing  rather than  decreasing  Factor  C,  Factor  B
will become the stimulus which reduces Factor A. In the above case, when the land is
not purchasable on the market, it will become the bottleneck of new substations. As a
result, a new strategic risk is stimulated. To avoid this situation, AM strategies should be
applied to reduce Factor B through, for example, applying compact substation designs.

6.1.6 Feed-forwards

“Multiple  consequences”  and  “joint  effects”  can  coexist  in  one  graph  and  form
multiple routes from one node to another. Each route like this is called a “feed-forward”,

191

as  Figure  6.8  shows  between  Factor  A  and  D.  The  feed-forward  pattern  makes  the
relationship between Factor A and D more complicated, in two patterns explained below.

Homogenous feed-forward pattern

In  a  relatively  simple  situation,  all  feed-forwards  have  the  same  correlation
direction.  Thus,  we  name  these  situations  “homogenous  feed-forwards”.  Homogenous
feed-forwards can be merged into one single causal link. For example, route A-B-D and
A-C-D  in  Figure  6.8(a) are  both  positive.  Hence,  they  can  be  replaced  with a  positive
causal link from A to D.

Figure 6.8: Homogenous feed-forward (a) and heterogeneous feed-forward (b).

When the asset manager wants to control Factor D by using A, the AM strategy can
be  implemented  as  Table  6.1  shows.  The  criteria  to  select  strategies  are  based  on
knowledge of the causalities. Typically, the knowledge reveals the situations in which
the feed-forwards will have significant undesirable effects. Therefore, the focus of the
risk-based strategy is to find a roadmap which avoids falling into any of these situations.
