192

Example:  When  several  aging  mechanisms  can  lead  to  failures  of  the  same  asset
population,  they  form  homogenous  feed-forwards.  The  feed-forwards  begins  at  the
business  factor  (e.g.  distributed  generation)  triggering  the  stress  factors  (e.g.  heavier
load, overvoltage etc.) and ends at the business factor “failure frequency”.  Figure 6.10
will explain this example in detail.

Heterogeneous feed-forward pattern

As  its  name  suggests,  the  heterogeneous  feed-forward  pattern  contains  multiple
routes  of  different  correlation  directions.  Heterogeneous  feed-forwards  cannot  be
merged  into  one  in  system  diagram,  since the  correlation  direction of  the  merged  link
cannot  be  determined.  In  Figure  6.8(b),  routes  A-B-D  and  A-C-D  together  form  a
heterogeneous feed-forward pattern.

When the feed-forwards are not quantified, heterogeneous feed-forwards are more
difficult to  manage  than  homogeneous  ones. The  precaution-based strategy  in  the  first
row of Table 6.1 is not suitable, since extreme values of Factor A in either direction will
lead to large effect of one feed-forward.

Therefore, when the knowledge is insufficient to quantify the heterogeneous feed-

forwards, asset manager should adopt two principles to manage them.

At the operational level, asset manager should apply “doctrine of the mean” to most
operation  and  maintenance  (O&M)  activities.  The  “doctrine  of  the  mean”  emphasizes
the reasonability  of  current  practice rather  than  the ideology  of  long-term  visions.  For
AM, it means no significant changes to the asset portfolio and corresponding life cycle
activities,  etc.  In  other  words,  substitutions  or  significant  enhancements  should  not  be
applied.

However, the conservatism of “doctrine of the mean” should not be applied to resist
the  information  strategy,  since  it  is  a  critical  step  towards  the  real  solution  of  the
strategic risk.

Therefore,  at  the  tactical  and  strategic  level,  asset  manager  should  develop
knowledge  on  the  insufficiently  studied  causal  chains.  Typically,  such  developments
require investments on sensors, communication systems, data systems and intelligence.
The  additional  devices  will  bring  change  costs,  through  challenging  the  reliability  of
existing technical systems or by modifying existing processes.

193

Example: The effect of preventive maintenance on duration of N-1 is a heterogeneous
failure  frequency  and,
feed-forward.  On  one  hand,
consequently,  duration  of  N-1  due  to  failures.  On  the  other  hand,  the  maintenance
activities  are  often  performed  in  the  planned  outages,  during  which  the  network  is
operating at N-1 situation.

the  maintenance  reduces

6.1.7 Summary: Critical Patterns of Interdependency

Sections  6.1.3  to  6.1.6  investigated  different  patterns  of  interdependencies  which
introduce complexity into the management of strategic risks. These patterns describe the
features  of  a  system  diagram,  and  AM  strategies  can  be  developed  accordingly  in
Section 6.2.6.

Three patterns have the most significant influence on the whole AM system from

the system engineering point of view. They are
•  Positive feedback loops
•  Negative feedback loops, when its steady state cannot be reached, because
an  extra  link  (a  stakeholder  requirement)  restricts  the  quantity  of  a  node
(the availability of an internal business factor). In this situation, the internal
business factor becomes a scarce resource.

•  Heterogeneous feed-forwards

In  addition,  the  branches  in  Section  6.1.3  are  combined  with  the  knowledge
maturity model in Section 6.2.5 to determine the coordination between precaution-based
and  risk-based  AM  strategies.  The  interchangeability  in  Section  6.1.4  is  an  important
concern when a new precaution-based strategy is implemented.

6.2 System Diagram for Strategic Asset Management

A  system  diagram  is  used  to  show  complexity,  e.g.  formed  by  interdependent
causal relationships. In this section, we adapt the general system diagram method to our
purpose,  namely  managing  electrical  infrastructure  assets.  For  those  who  have
experience with the original system diagram method, our method has several differences.
Firstly,  the  central  area  of  our  system  diagram  aims  to  technical  complexity,  as
mentioned in the  beginning  of  this chapter.  Secondly,  for  applying  it  in  strategic  AM,
we  modify  the  procedure  of  using  system  diagrams  in  Section  6.2.1  and  introduce
different  types  of  edges in  6.2.3. Thirdly,  our system  diagram  visualizes uncertainties,
through symbolizes the knowledge maturities with different colors in Section 6.2.5.

194

6.2.1 General Description of System Diagrams for Asset Management

Figure 6.9 demonstrates a system diagram. It consists of four types of nodes which
are symbolized as different shapes. Their names and common locations are explained as
follows.

1)  The  internal  business  factors  are  in  the  solid  line  circles  in  the  middle:
They  compose  the  main  body  of  the  AM  system.  The  readiness  of
knowledge on an internal business factor can be further represented by the
color in the circle. See Section 6.2.5 for details.

2)  The external business factors are in the dashed line circles: They mostly
are  located  at  the  top  and  bottom  side.  They  are  stated  as  OSP’s  and
stakeholder requirements in PAS55. When modelled as strategic risks, they
are strategic problems which initiate the causal chains.

3)  The potential AM strategies are in the rectangles: They are located at the
left or right side. They are the investment plans which can be applied to the
entire asset population or asset portfolio for at least a life cycle. They are
always at the begin side of an edge. The shapes of this edge symbolize the
type of strategy. See Section 6.2.3 for details.

Figure 6.9: Four types of nodes in a system diagram.

195

4)  The  performance  indicators  in the rounded  rectangles: They  are located
mostly  at  the  right  side.  They  are  the  part  of  stakeholder  requirements
which  are  measured  quantitatively  and  used  in  decision  making  at  the
tactical level. Section 6.2.2 and 6.2.4 will discuss stakeholder requirements,
including external business factors and performance indicators in detail.

A system diagram consists of four types of nodes and only one type of edges [13].
In this section, we adapt the general system diagram for strategic AM in several aspects
by

•  Keeping the shapes of four types of nodes in the generic system diagram,
as introduced above. They have been renamed according to terms in AM.
•  Enriching  the  types  of  edges.  Use  the  shape  of  edges  to  symbolize  the
properties of causalities. Specifically, an edge starting from a node of type
“AM  strategy”  indicates  if  it  is  a  precaution  -based,  risk-based  or
information strategy defined in Chapter 5. (See Section 6.2.3)

•  Adding colors to the nodes. The colors are used to indicate the knowledge

maturity introduced in Chapter 4. (See Section 6.2.5)

•  Developing rules to check whether the AM strategies can completely cover
the  strategic  risks,  through  checking  the  combination  of  nodes and edges.
(See Section 6.2.6)

In  this  thesis,  a  system  diagram  is  used  for  a  conceptual  analysis  on  complexity
(composed of strategic risks). To complete an analysis based on our system diagram, we
recommend a risk analyst to perform the following procedure.

(1)  Identify stakeholder requirements
(2)  Construct a system diagram
(3)  Search critical patterns formed only by internal business factors
(4)  Study the causalities between stakeholder requirements, and search critical
patterns  which  are  formed  by  internal  business  factors,  external  business
factors and performance indicators.
(5)  Introduce (potential) AM strategies

Step (6) and (7) can be skipped, if none of the AM strategies in Step (5) is risk-based.

(6)  (Optional) Evaluate knowledge maturity of internal business factors
(7)  (Optional) Identify insufficiency of knowledge, establish roadmap towards
risk-based AM strategies, and develop information strategy accordingly.

196

Sections 6.2.2 to 6.2.6 will explain the above procedure in detail. Section 6.3 will

apply the above procedure on the case of MV cables.

6.2.2 Interpreting Stakeholder Requirements as Performance Indicators and

External Business Factors
Before constructing a system diagram, the goals of the asset owner to satisfy certain
stakeholder(s) should be defined in Step (1) of our system diagram method. These goals
are named as stakeholder requirements, and typically appear as OSP defined in PAS 55.
The stakeholder requirements appear in two different forms in system diagrams.

On  one  hand,  when  the  amount  of  stakeholder  requirement  satisfied  by  a  certain
technical  events/activities  can  be  measured,  a  performance  indicator  can  be  created
accordingly.  These  performance  indicators  can  be  applied  to  benchmark  the  output  of
the entire asset portfolio.

In  the  electricity  sector,  the  regulator  normally  defines  a  number  of  performance
indicators,  called  “business  values”,  as  well  as the  standards  to  convert  corresponding
performance indicators into  financial  gains  or losses.  For instance,  in  the  Netherlands,
the  business  values  include  finance,  reliability,  safety,  environment,  compliance  and,
occasionally,  image,  as  mentioned  in  Section  2.2.4.  In  system  diagrams,  these
performance  indicators  are  symbolized  by  rounded  rectangles.  In  addition  to  these
business  values,  the  asset  owner  can  develop  its  own  performance  indicators  for
Consequences on Scarce Resources (defined in Section 5.1.3).

On  the  other  hand,  stakeholders  can  impose  political  requirements  on  the  asset
owner  through  the  regulator  or  other  authorities.  These  requirements  force  the  asset
portfolio to change in certain way, while these changes  mean extra investments to the
asset  owner.  A  typical  example  of  such  requirements  is  the  environmental  regulations
which oblige a TSO/DSO to install indoor substations and eco-friendly constructions.

In strategic risks, these mandatory requirements play the role of strategic problems
rather  than  performance  indicators.  In  other  words,  they  initiate  the  causal  chain  of
strategic  risks.  In  system  diagrams,  they  are  modelled  as  external  business  factors,
because the stakeholders are mostly external parties.

197

6.2.3 Edges in System Diagrams

In  the  generic  system  diagrams,  only  one  type  of  arrow  line  is  used,  as  drawn  in
Section 6.1.2. They symbolize the causal chain from an AM strategy/external business
factor via internal business factors to performance indicators. The 1st, 2nd, 3rd and 5th row
of Table 6.2 list the four possible combinations of their beginnings and ends.

The generic system diagram assumes that risk specialist has a complete knowledge
on the internal business factors. Therefore, as the 5th row of Table 6.2 has figured out,
the risk-based AM strategy is applicable. Unfortunately, this is only the ideal situation in
AM.  Hence,  as  the  4th  row  shows,  a  hollow  arrow  is  used  to  indicate  that  the  AM
strategy is precaution-based, while its boldness indicates the strength of its influence.

labelled with a plus or minus symbol.

In the procedure we proposed at the end of Section 6.2.1,

Edges in 5th and 6th row mostly introduced in Step (7), since the establishment of risk-
based strategy need to comply with the knowledge maturity of internal business factors
(see 6.2.5 and 6.2.6 for details).

6.2.4 Linkage between Stakeholder Requirements

In  strategic  risks  studied  by  the  asset  manager,  the  external  business  factors
(strategic problems) influence the performance indicators through affecting the internal
business factors. The internal business factors can be understood and controlled by the
asset manager, since they describe technical events or actions within the utility company.

Meanwhile,  causalities  exist  between  stakeholder  requirements  without  affecting
internal  business  factors.  For  example,
is
psychologically deciding the willingness of labors to work in the electricity sector, and
consequently, economically affecting the availability of work force.

the  safety  performance

indicator

The  asset  manager  cannot  really  study  such  linkages,  because  they  are  non-
technical and external. However, the asset manager should be aware of their existence,
since loops can be formed through combining internal and external causalities. Step (4)
of our system diagram method aims to reveal these loops.

199

These loops are unlikely to be managed risk-based, because they contain external
causalities. If the asset manager is responsible for tackling them, the solution is normally
precaution-based.  If  discourse-based  management  is  applied  by  the  asset  owner,
information strategies should be implemented on the internal part of these loops.

6.2.5 Embed Knowledge Maturity Model into the Nodes of System Diagrams

In  Chapter  4,  we  use  readiness  of  knowledge  to  indicate  the  maturity  of  risk
management.  In  Section  5.2,  the  maturity  level  is  used  to  answer  Q4  and  Q7  in  the
classification  of  strategy  risks  in  Section  5.2.  These  two  questions  decide  whether  a
precaution-based or risk-based AM strategy is needed.

A similar score can be given to the knowledge on an internal business factor (as a

node). A generic scoring standard is described in Table 6.3. The higher the score is,

•

•
•

the more information and data is available, regarding the studied node itself
as well as its correlation with other nodes;
the more causal links from or towards the studied node have been identified;
the more quantitative models have been established for the causal links.

When the failures or maintenance activities are studied as an internal business factor, the
scoring  standard  will  adapt  to  the  knowledge  maturity  model  for  maintenance
management in Chapter 4. Therefore we also name this score as “knowledge maturity”.

In a system diagram, the colors are filled in the circle of the studied node, so that
the score can be seen together with the edges/causal links. Such arrangements allow risk
analysts  to  check  whether  the  AM  strategies  (shown  in  edges)  comply  with  the
knowledge maturity of nodes.

Usually, the differences between the knowledge maturities of two connected nodes
are  no  larger  than  one  level.  It  is  because  the  knowledge  on  one  node  can  be  derived
from  its  neighboring  nodes.  For  example,  if  Node  A  has  a  maturity  level
,  its  own
value and the causal links from and to it can be quantitatively measured. In this situation,
if causality exists between Node A and B, the value of Node B can be calculated from
the value of Node A and the quantitative relationship between A and B. Such calculation
is  likely  to  increase  the  estimation  error  of  Node  A  (which  can  be  modeled  as
confidence intervals as shown in Chapter 3). As a result, Node B can only be estimated
qualitatively and accordingly, the knowledge maturity of Node B is

3

External  business  factors  are  always  filled  with  white  colors  in  system  diagrams.
The  externality  means  that  these  business  factors  can  only  be  studied  in  methods  of
humanities  instead  of  science,  or  acquired  through  discourses.  Thus  the  knowledge
cannot really reach the knowledge maturity level

.

2

The concept of knowledge maturity is used to check whether a risk-based strategy
is ready to implement. If  yes, then precaution-bases strategies should be introduced to
control  the  branches of the  causal  chain in  which  the  knowledge  is insufficient.  If  no,
then information strategies should be introduced to acquire knowledge on those nodes of
low maturity levels. Section 6.2.6 will describe this issue in detail.

⟨

⟩

6.2.6 AM Strategies in System Diagrams

An  AM  strategy  can  be  described  from  two  aspects.  The  first  aspect  is  its  type
which  reflects  how  an  internal  business  factor  is  controlled.  Chapter  5  divides  AM

201

strategies  into  three  types:  precaution-based,  risk-based  and  information  strategies.  In
system diagrams, the types of strategies are shown in the edges, as Table 6.3 lists.

The second aspect is the activity which realizes the control. Such activity normally
appears as a standard investment plan on certain assets and/or LC Activities. In practice,
AM  strategies  are  commonly  named  after  (the  sum  of)  their  activities,  such  as
“maintenance strategies”, “20kV upgrade strategies”, etc. Similarly, the rectangles at the
left side of system diagrams are also named after these activities.

In  Step  (5)  of our system  diagram  method,  the  rectangles  are  introduced  by  asset
specialists  (who  are  mainly  engineers).  These  rectangles  represent  potential  AM
strategies. Meanwhile, risk analysts should draw edges between the rectangles and other
nodes.  Drawing  an  edge  means  making  a  decision  on  (1)  the  target  node  of  the  AM
strategy and (2) the type of the AM strategy.

Below introduces how risk analyst can complete all edges between rectangles

Three situations to introduce precaution-based AM strategies

Precaution-based strategies need to be implemented in three situations:

•  To reduce a positive feedback:

o  When a positive feedback loop is formed internally (i.e. only with
internal  business  factors),  at  least  one  node  in  the  loop  must  be
controlled. The  control  can  be  risk-based,  if  all  nodes  in  the  loop
reach  knowledge  maturity  level
.  Otherwise,  the  control  is
precaution -based.

3

o  When  a  positive  feedback  loop  contains  external  causalities
between stakeholder requirements, at least one node in the loop is
highly recommended to be controlled. Since knowledge on external
causalities  is  difficult  to  acquire,  the  control  is  likely  to  be
precaution-based.

⟨

⟩

•  To reduce disturbance on a negative feedback:

o  When a negative feedback loop is formed internally but its stability
is disturbed, the causal link/chain introducing the disturbance need
to  be  minimized  through  implementing  precaution-based  strategy
on  it.  Similarly,  when  a  causal  link  has  a  conditional  direction,

202

precaution- based strategies should be applied to hold the direction
of causality.

•  To reduce disturbance on the risk-based management:
o  See the next subsection for details.

Precaution-based  strategies  require  less  knowledge  than  risk-based  strategies.  In

general, the target node should reach knowledge maturity level

2

.

Two rules to introduce risk-based AM strategies in a system diagram

⟨

⟩

The  heterogeneous feed-forward  pattern  introduced  in  Section  6.1.6  is  difficult  to
be dealt with precaution-based AM strategies. Therefore, risk-based AM strategies have
to be implemented to control the consequences of heterogeneous feed-forwards.

In  practice,  most  precaution-based  AM  strategies  will  introduce  heterogeneous
feed-forwards  to  a  certain  degree.  Principally,  a  precaution  is  introduced  to  improve
performance, while applying precautions leads to costs and the consummation of other
resources  (e.g.  the  reliability  of  system).  This  fact  implies  that:  upgrading  precaution-
based AM strategies with risk-based strategies will bring some savings on costs.

However,  Chapter  4  and  5  have  concluded  that  risk-based  strategy  is  based  on
knowledge.  In  Section  6.2.5,  the  readiness  of  knowledge  has  been  embedded  into  the
system diagram as the colors of internal business factors. Therefore, in a system diagram,
introduction of a risk-based AM strategy follows the main rule below.

All routes from the target node to the performance indicators are in a blue color.

This  means that  the  target node,  as  well  as  the  internal  business factors  from  it to  the
performance indicators, has to reach the knowledge maturity level
. These routes are
the main causal chains. In risk-based AM strategies, the status of each node in the main
causal chains is firstly interpreted as, for example, ordinal categorical variables such as
condition  indicators.  Then,  the  interpreted  data  are  reported  to  the  asset  manager  to
supervise the output of the strategy.

3

⟩

⟨

Unfortunately, quantitative measurement on all branches of the main causal chain(s)
is  a  demanding  job.  In  addition,  the  interdependencies  between  causal  chains
(introduced in Section 6.1) prevent the asset manager from finding the optimal balance
between multiple performance indicators. According to Section 6.1.3, 6.1.5 and 6.1.6, a

203

number of causal links need to be “cut” from the main causal chain, through applying
precaution-based  strategy  to  minimize  their  effects.  This  brings  the  below  exception
rule to the introduction of risk-based AM strategy.

The  routes  joining  the  main  causal  chain(s)  or  those  splitting  from  the  main  causal
chain(s) need not to be in blue color, if their effects are completely limited by another
precaution-based strategy.

Through  applying  the  main  rule  and  the  exception  rule  in  Step  (6)  of  our  system
diagram  method,  the  risk  analyst  can  confirm  whether  the  knowledge  is  sufficient  to
introduce  risk-based  management  on  a  specific  strategic  risk  (treated  as  main  causal
chain).

Knowledge development and information strategies

Through applying the main rule and the exception, risk analysts can decide whether
the risk-based AM strategy can be introduced. Meanwhile, when lack of knowledge of
some  nodes  presents  itself  as  the  obstacle  to  introduce  the  risk-based  AM  strategies,
additional actions should be taken to acquire and develop knowledge. Technically, this
means investments in sensor, communication and database systems.

In Step (7) of our system diagram method, risk analysts are required to draw two
system diagrams. The first one shows the knowledge maturity levels of today, as well as
the risk-based AM strategy which needs to be implemented. The second one shows the
knowledge  maturity  levels  in  the  future  when  the  risk-based  AM  strategy  can  be
implemented.  Through  comparing  the  difference  between  two  diagrams,  the  target
nodes of information strategies can be identified.

6.3 Case Study: Integrate AM Strategies with Stakeholder

Requirements in System Diagram for Medium Voltage Cables
In  Section  5.3.4  and  5.4.2,  individual  AM  strategies  related  to  replacement  and
maintenance  of  medium  voltage  (MV)  cables  have  been  identified,  according  to
strategic  risks  on  cable  systems.  In  this  section,  we  will  apply  our  system  diagram
approach to model these strategic risks, so that the AM strategies identified in Chapter 5
can be coordinated in a roadmap. Similar to the cases in Chapter 5, the description of
strategic  risks  and  identification  of  AM  strategies  are  based  on  our  experience  with
Dutch DSO’s [101, 103-105, 109].

204

The modelling follows the procedure we designed at the end of Section 6.2.1.

Step (1): Stakeholder Requirements

The stakeholder requirements are clear. The performance indicators include:

Finance, reliability, safety, compliance and environment

Section 5.3.4 has described the strategic problems on the capacity. They are:

The growth of sustainable energy and the effect of demand side management

In  addition,  we  introduce  two  extra  strategic  problems  to  demonstrate  the

interdependencies at the strategic level of AM. They are:
The civil activities on the route of cables and the availability of workforce

6.3.1 System Diagram for Operation and Maintenance of MV Cables

Step  (2):  construct  the  system  diagram,  and  Step  (3):  search  critical  patterns
formed only by internal business factors

The failure modes

Unlike the tactical level, the strategic level of AM does not scan through all failure
modes. Instead, a failure mode can catch attention of strategic AM for either of the two
reasons:  (1)  the  failure  mode  is  currently  the  dominant,  or  (2)  the  failure  mode  will
become dominant due to a strategic problem.

In Figure 6.10, four failure modes form four homogenous feed-forwards. This feed-

forward pattern composes the upper part of our system diagram.

The  two  feed-forwards  at  the  right  side  are  the  local  failures.  Local  failures  are
caused  by  stresses/damages  concentrating  on  a  segment  of  limited  lengths  of  cables.
Such defects are typically repaired with cable joints and seldom lead to replacement of
complete cables.

For MV cables in the Netherlands, digging damages are the dominant failure cause
of today. Civil activities are responsible for most of digging damages. Meanwhile, the
mechanical  aging  is  directly  triggered  by  thermal  expansion  and  contraction,  and
originally caused by daily fluctuation of the load. It is an emerging failure mode, since
sustainable  energy  will  increase  the  variation  and  accelerate  the  changing  of  the  load.

205

Demand side management aims to reduce such variations, but its effectiveness remains
difficult to understand and control [110].

Growth of
sustainable
energy

Effect of
demand side
management

[＋]

[＋]

[－]

Figure 6.10: Part of system diagram for O&M of MV cables, in which strategic problems lead to

different failure modes.

The  two feed-forwards at  the  left  side are  the  global wear-outs.  Global  wear-outs
are caused by stresses spreading on the entire cable. Individual failures caused by global
wear-outs can still be repaired. But replacement will be a more economical solution to
global wear-outs, if large numbers of defects have been created by the stress. Because of

206

the high investments of such replacements, external business factors accelerating global
wear-out should be considered as strategic problems.

Thermal  aging  and  electrical  aging  are  two  primary  concerns  of  cable  designers.
They are associated with the two essential options of capacity upgrade: more current or
higher voltage. Therefore, they are included in the system diagram.

Technical effects and consequences of failures

Both local failures and global wear-out finally increase the failure frequency. In our
system diagram, failure frequency is the node which connects the upper part of failure
modes and the lower part of maintenance activities.

In  MV  networks,  cable  failures  have  three  effects.  Firstly,  before  a  failure  is
isolated, the clients will have outages. This is the functional part of failure consequences
which will further lead to losses of performance indicators “reliability”, “finance” and
“compliance”. Reducing this functional consequence of failures is the primary concern
of maintenance activities in Figure 6.11.

Secondly, the failures could threaten the “safety” due to voltage shocks and fires or
and “environment” due to fires and leakage of dielectric liquids, etc. These are the side-
effects of failure consequences. See Chapter 4 for detailed definitions.

Finally,  frequent  failures  will  accelerate  thermal  aging  in  long-term.  Within  the
period of repair, a sub-network (typically a cable ring) has to operate in a less redundant
configuration. The node “duration of N-1” represents this situation. In an N-1 situation,
other cables in the sub-network have to carry extra load. This adds to the chance that the
current  will  temporarily  reach  or  exceed the  ampacity,  which  will  significantly  add  to
the  thermal  aging.  Figure  6.10  uses  bold  arrow  lines  to  highlight  this  critical  positive
feedback loop of thermal aging, as the primary concern of the asset manager.

The maintenance activities

Figure 6.11 shows how maintenance activities deal with failures and their technical

effects.

Figure 6.12: A causal link from performance indicators to a strategic problem builds an

additional feedback loop.

208

From the perspective of strategic AM, maintenance activities can be simply divided
into  reactive  repairs  and  preventive  repairs,  according  to  whether  they  are  performed
before or after failures. A reactive repair plan typically includes

•
•

a switching plan which reduce the duration of outages, and
a repair plan to reduce the duration of N-1.

In contrast, preventive repairs reduce potential failures without causing outages of
clients. However, most preventive repair plans need to be performed at N-1 situations.
As  a  result,  the  contribution  of  preventive  repairs  to  duration  of  N-1  forms  a
heterogeneous feed-forward pattern.

Step (4): search critical patterns formed not only by internal business factors

Both  reactive  and  preventive  repairs  are  implemented  by  field  workers.  When  a
sufficient  number  of  maintenance  mechanics  are  ready,  the  asset  manager  can  simply
regard repair tasks as costs. However, workforces are not always available. One scenario
is  that  the  workforce  refuses  to  perform  maintenance  when  the  environment  or  safety
record  of  the  asset  owner  is  poor.  In  this  scenario,  the  loss  of  performance  on
“environment”  and  “safety”  will  lead  to  shortage  of  available  field  workers,  and  the
shortage of field workers will further delay the repair tasks, as Figure 6.12 shows. As a
result, a positive feedback loop is formed due to poor field working conditions, which is
definitely an undesired situation for the asset owner.

Step (5): introduce AM strategies

According  to  Section  5.3.4  and  5.4.2, three precaution-based  AM  strategies  listed

below are currently applied on MV cables.

•  Static  ampacity  rating:  Pessimistic  cooling  parameters  are  applied  to  rate
the ampacity of cables. It is an enhancement-based strategy, since it ensures
that the rate of thermal aging is below a certain level.

•  Periodic replacement: Cables are replaced after a predetermined maximum
period. The replacements clear the cumulative effects of global wear-out. It
is  a  substitution-based  strategy  of  high  CapEx  (capital  expenditure).  As
Situation 1 in Section 5.4.2 mentioned, it is a solution of high investment,
but it is the only solution when the diagnostic data is not available.

209

•  Corrective  maintenance:  the  switching  plans  and  the  repair  plans  control
the outage duration and the N-1 duration within a certain acceptable range.
As Section 5.4.2 explained, it is an enhancement-based strategy. It can only
control the duration of each outage within a certain range acceptable to the
clients.  It  cannot  prevent  failures  from  occurring,  while  the  occurred
failures still have significant risks on the environment and the safety.

Their roles in strategic AM are summarized in Table 6.4. All of them contribute to
control the positive feedback loop of thermal aging. However, their exact effect differs.
Ampacity  rating  controls  rather  than clears  thermal  aging.  Periodic  replacement  clears
the defects created not only by thermal aging, but of course also defects caused by other
global aging mechanisms. Corrective maintenance deals with all failure modes, but the
durations of reactive repairs can be optimized to reduce the duration of N-1.

TABLE 6.4:  PRECAUTION-BASED STRATEGIES CURRENTLY APPLIED IN O&M OF MV CABLES TO

CONTROL THE POSITIVE FEEDBACK LOOP OF THERMAL AGING.

AM strategy

Related pattern(s) in the system diagram

Name

Type

Type

Target of control

ampacity
rating

(Precaution)

replacement

(Precaution)

maintenance

thermal aging

Unfortunately,  the  AM strategies  in Table  6.4  cannot  cover  all  critical  patterns  in

several aspects.

Firstly,  the  external  positive  feedback  loop  of  field  work  conditions  cannot  be
effectively  controlled.  On  one  hand,  reactive  repairs  cannot  prevent  the  occurrence  of
failures resulting from the stimuli of environmental and safety risks. On the other hand,
replacements cannot prevent a specific failure mode from occurring, therefore it is not
an  efficient  way  to  control  the  specific  failure  modes  which  pose  a  risk  to  the
environment and safety.

Figure 6.13: The complete system diagram about O&M of MV cables, on which today’s

211

Secondly, the heterogeneous feed-forward pattern of preventive repairs is the main
barrier towards preventive maintenance strategy. Preventive repair can control specific
failure  modes.  However,  when  performed  periodically,  preventive  maintenance  is  a
precaution-based  strategy  which  requires  N-1  much  more  frequently  than  corrective
maintenance. This reason together with its high cost and demand on field workers make
the asset manager hesitant to adopt periodic preventive maintenance in MV cables.

Therefore, the solution is to schedule preventive repairs in a risk-based way. This is
referred  as  the  proactive  maintenance  strategy.  The  barrier  towards  proactive
maintenance strategy is the knowledge. See the Step (6) and (7) for details.

As a result, preventive repairs have not been widely introduced to most MV cable

population, as the grey rectangles in Figure 6.13 shows.

Step (6): evaluate knowledge maturity of internal business factors

Figure  6.13  shows  the  knowledge  maturity  of  today  regarding  the  O&M  of  MV
cables  in  the  Netherlands.  The  colors  are  according  to  Section  5.3.4  and  5.4.2,  which
have described today’s knowledge on aging and failures of MV cables.

2

. The electrical aging and mechanical aging remain at level

In  Figure  6.13,  most  nodes  have  a  yellow  color  (knowledge  maturity  level
).
Specifically, the thermal aging and failures, as well as the digging failures, have reached
level
. Although most
major failures  have  been  recorded,  the  information  on  failure  mode  is  usually  of  poor
⟨
quality.  This  prevents  node  “failure  frequency”  from  achieving  level
.  At  the  same
time, since failure modes are poorly recorded, the contribution of a preventive repairing
method cannot be identified. Eventually, a proactive maintenance strategy is blocked by
lack of the knowledge, as we concluded in Chapter 4.

2

⟩

⟩

⟨

⟩

⟩

⟨

Figure 6.14: The complete system diagram about O&M of MV cables in the future, which

213

Table  6.5  uses  two  shades  to  divide  the  strategies  into  two  groups.  The  three
strategies  at  the  top  without  shade  are  the  risk-based  version  upgraded  from  the
strategies  in  Table  6.4.  Section  5.3.4  and  5.4.3  has  introduced  these  strategies  as  the
advanced  stage  of  O&M.  In  general,  they  bring  benefits  on  (capital  or  operational)
expenditures on replacement and repairs.

The  two  strategies  at  the  bottom  of  Table  6.5  with  the  grey  shade  are  the
precaution-based strategies. Risk-based strategies cannot cover some branches of causal
chains where the knowledge maturity level is low. Such branches include

•
•

the minor failures of limited effects, and
the major failures of unknown or complicated causes.

The  first  point  includes  electrical  aging  in  Figure  6.14  and  other  failure  mechanisms
insufficiently  studied. Typical  examples  of the second  point  include  the  failure modes
caused by the climate or the side-effects of other failures.

To  solve  these  failure  modes,  the  corrective  maintenance  is  still  necessary  as  a
precaution-based  strategy.  Moreover,  as  a  supplement  to  the  maintenance  strategies,
fault  location  and  self-healing  technologies  are  introduced  into  the  MV  network  to
shorten to period of switching and, therefore, to limit the duration of outages.

TABLE 6.5:  RISK-BASED AND PRECAUTION-BASED AM STRATEGIES APPLIED IN FUTURE O&M OF

MV CABLES TO CONTROL ALL IDENTIFIED CRITICAL PATTERNS IN OUR SYSTEM DIAGRAM.

AM strategy

Related pattern(s) in the system diagram

Name

Type

Type

Summary

Static  ampacity  rating,  periodic  replacement  and  corrective  maintenance  are  the
three precautions implemented on today’s MV cables. They control the global thermal
aging of cables and ensure the availability of them.

Preventive repairs should be introduced to control the side-effects of failures which
threaten  environment  and  safety.  However,  the  contribution  of  periodic  preventive
maintenance  to  the  availability  of  cable  assets  is  seldom  identified  in  today’s  MV
network.  Therefore,  preventive  repairs  should  be  managed  in  a  risk-based  way  in  the
future.

215

To  develop  towards  proactive  maintenance,  large  amounts  of  information  and
knowledge should be available, such as the load data, diagnostic condition data, failure
records and outage records. In addition, when proactive maintenance is implemented to
control  critical  failure  modes,  a  number  of  AM  strategies  should  be  implemented  as
supplements, such as corrective maintenance for minor failures, fault location for major
failures, and dynamic ampacity rating for overloading.

6.3.2 System Diagram for Replacement and Upgrade of MV Cables

Section  6.3.1  applied  the  complete  procedure  of  our  system  diagram  method  on
O&M of MV cables. Compared with O&M, replacement and upgrade (R&U) is an area
in which external parties are heavily involved and less knowledge is required. Therefore,
risk-based AM strategy is uncommon in R&U. Accordingly, we do not perform Step (6)
and (7) in this case. Instead, the role of interchangeability and flexibility in precaution-
based strategies is noteworthy in this case.

Step (2): construct the system diagram

The system diagram of R&U shares some nodes and edges with that O&M. They

differ in four aspects below.

•  Regarding  the  demand  on  capacity:  the  internal  business  factor  is
“additional line” is added. Additional cable connection of the same voltage
is  a  common  option  to  reinforce  the  network.  But  it  does  not  change  the
maintenance plans, so it is not included in O&M diagram.

•  Regarding  the  gain  and  loss  of  different  upgrade  options:  three  internal

business factors are added.

o  Line  losses:  The  fraction  of  electricity  lost  due  to  the  heat
generated  by  (electrical)  current  and  resistance.  Heavier  load  will
increase this fraction, while higher voltage will decrease it. In the
Dutch regulation, the network operator has to pay for this loss.
Investment on new assets: It includes the expenditure of the cable
systems, as well as the costs to install them. Normally speaking, a
cable  of  a  higher  voltage  rating  is  more  expensive.  However,  in
Netherlands, 20 kV cables are nearly the same price per kilometer
as 10 kV ones.

o

o  Early disposal of assets: An existing cable can be disposed before
reaching its technical end of life, if a connection of higher voltage

216

is using its route. In accounting, this reduces the item “fixed asset”
in the balance sheet, and the item “depreciation of fixed asset” as a
source of cash. Both are negative effects on the financial profiles of
asset owner.

o  Land usage: Land is treated as a scarce resource which is needed in
the  upgrades.  Cables  require  a  much  narrower  corridor  than
overhead lines. Yet, the communities have environmental concerns
on the existence of electrical power equipment.
Investment on new assets, early disposal of assets and land usage
are the change costs of a capacity upgrade. Therefore, the change
cost is the lowest for “heavier loads”, medium for “additional lines”
the  edges  of
and
changeability show in Figure 6.15.

the  highest  for  “voltage  upgrades”,  as

o

•  Regarding  the  aging  mechanisms  and  failure  modes:  The  local  failure
modes  are  not  included  in  the  R&U  diagram.  They  are  assumed  to  be
completely handled by the maintenance strategies.

•  Regarding  the  failure  consequences  and  maintenance  activities:  Section
5.4.2 has explained that maintenance costs on MV cables are much lower
than replacements. Therefore, they  are  simplified as one  internal  business
factor to indicate that the consequences of failures have been limited.

Step (3), (4) and (5): search critical patterns and introduce AM strategies

The  positive  feedback  loop  of  thermal  aging  is  the  core  of  R&U  of  MV  cables.
Naturally, the “additional lines” or “voltage upgrades” should be implemented to reduce
the loads on existing lines. The two AM strategies at the top right of Figure 6.15 shows
this conclusion.

Meanwhile, the causal link from “additional lines” or “voltage upgrades” to “land
usage” is conditional. The asset owner should prepare for the situation that the land for
new  installations  is  in  shortage  in  long-term,  since  it  will  block  the  investments  on
capacity.

In this situation, the two risk-based AM strategies analyzed in O&M will be helpful.
Dynamic  ampacity  rating  allows  existing  cables  to  carry  more  loads  in  good  cooling
conditions.  Since  the  load  reaches  its  annual  maximum  in  winter  in  the  Netherlands,

217

dynamic  ampacity  rating  can  delay  the  upgrade  plans  and  provide  sufficient  time  to
prepare for the high change costs.

218

Condition-based replacement should be applied in the contrary situation. When the
remaining  life  of  existing  assets  ends  earlier  than  the  expected  moment  of  voltage
upgrades, condition diagnoses can assist the asset manager in extending the lifetime of
existing  assets.  This  is  a  typical  example  in  which  replacements  and  upgrades  can  be
coordinated to make full use of value of assets.

Summary

The risk-based O&M strategies contribute to R&U of MV cables through providing
them  with  “flexibility”.  Through  knowing  and  managing  the  global  aging  of  existing
cables, the asset owner has the flexibility to choose the moment of upgrades according
to its economic and political interests.

6.3.3 Summary on Contribution of Condition Diagnosis and Monitoring

Technology to Asset Management
Condition  diagnosis  and  monitoring  (CMD)  technologies  play  important  roles  in
the  AM  strategies  identified  in  Section  6.3.1  and  6.3.2.  We  summarize  their
contributions as follows, for engineers and asset managers who are interested in future
developments of CMD. For MV cable assets:

On one hand, CMD is a necessary supplement to introduce preventive maintenance,
while the primary contribution of preventive maintenance is to reduce the side-effects of
failures.

•  When applied in a small scale, CMD is regarded as an information strategy
which  acquires  knowledge  on  different  aging  mechanisms  and  failure
modes.  This  is  a  critical  step  to  introduce  the  correct  preventive  repair
methods against critical failure modes.

•  When applied in a large scale, CMD supports the proactive maintenance, a
risk-based  AM  strategy  which  controls  the  preventive  repairs  without
sacrificing the availability of assets.

On the other hand, CMD provides capacity upgrades with flexibility on their timing

in two ways.

•  Condition data on global aging mechanisms provides proactive information
on the remaining life of assets. They can extend the lifetime of assets when
upgrades are expected but not upcoming in the very short term.

219

•  Condition  data  on  thermal  aging  can  be  applied  in  dynamic  ampacity
rating.  It  provides  extra  capacity  on  existing  connections.  When
investments on an additional asset are delayed by external parties, this extra
capacity can ensure the operation of network for an extra period.

6.4 Conclusions on Our System Diagram Method

A  strategic  risk  can  be  classified  into  a  category  according  to  the  decision  tree
developed  in  Section  5.2,  when  only  one  stakeholder  is  stimulating  it.  Unfortunately,
asset  managers  normally  face  multiple  stakeholder  requirements  within  the  long-term
planning  of  the  asset  portfolio.  Stimulated  by  multiple  stakeholder  requirements,  the
strategic risks forms a complex system in which their causal chains interact with each
other.  This  complexity  needs  to  be  considered  within  the  decision  making  at  the
strategic level of AM.

Our  system  diagram  method  developed  in  this  chapter  has  solved  the
complexity of strategic risks. Instead of selecting between precaution-based, risk-based
and information AM strategies in Chapter 5, our analysis process can identify multiple
AM  strategies  at  the  same  time  to  counteract  multiple  strategic  risks.  This  is  an
essential feature for decision making at the strategic level of asset management.

Specifically,  the  interdependent  causal  chains  are  divided  into  business  factors.
When the business factors forms the below patterns, an AM strategy is necessary to be
implemented:

•  A positive feedback loop requires an AM strategy to control its effect. The
stability  of  a  negative  feed-back  loop  needs  to  be  ensured  by  an  AM
strategy.  The  strategy  can  be  precaution-based  in  the  beginning,  but
would/should be further developed as risk-based.

•  A  heterogeneous  feed-forward  pattern  brings  uncertain  influences  on  an
internal business factor. These influences should be controlled in risk-based
way rather than precaution-based.

In  addition,  through  applying  our  system  diagram  method,  the  roadmap  of
introducing  risk-based  AM  strategies  can  be  recognized.  Specifically,  a  risk-based
AM  strategy  can  only  be  implemented  under  the  two  conditions  below.  The  two

220

conditions  are  created  respectively  by  information  strategies  and  precaution-based
strategies.

•  The causal chain managed by the risk-based AM strategy has to reach the
.  This  means  quantitative  measurement  and
knowledge  maturity  level
models are available to describe the stimuli, consequences and causalities.
Information  strategy  can  be  implemented  to  improve  the  knowledge
maturity level to level

3

3

.

⟨

⟩

•  When  a  risk-based  AM  strategy  is  managing  a  causal  chain,  the  causal
chain  can  be  disturbed  by  a  branch.  This  effect  of  this  branch  should  be
managed by a precaution-based strategy.

⟨

⟩

For  the  researchers  of  asset  management  in  general,  most  existing  researches
assume  that  risk-based  AM  strategies  can  be  well  supported  through  purchasing  data
acquisition  systems  from  the  market.  In  comparison,  the  significance  of  our  system
diagram method is that: it guides the development of data acquisition systems through
coordinating them  with  the  introduction  of  other  engineering  systems.  Specifically,  on
one  hand,  the  technical  complexity  of  high-voltage  assets  visualized  in  our  diagrams
reveals which physical phenomena should be observed. On the other hand, the maturity
levels  demanded  by  risk-based  AM  strategies  show  the  precision  of  data  needed  to
control specific strategic risks.

Finally,  in  the  case  study,  we  further  show  that  risk-based  AM  strategies  can

produce flexibility of implementing precaution-based AM strategies.

•

Interchangeability  can  exist  between  multiple  AM  strategies.  When  some
strategies  are  temporarily  disabled  or  limited  by  strategic  problems,  their
risk-based alternatives can support the operation of the system for an extra
period. This provides flexibility to the timing to introduce upgrades.

As a result, our system diagram method integrates AM strategies on a certain asset
population  with  stakeholder  requirements.  The  integrated  AM  strategies  not  only
provide a solution to the strategic risks, but also coordinate to each other according the
available knowledge. These features have proven that our system diagram method is a
powerful tool for making decisions at the strategic level of asset management.

221

222

Chapter 7 Conclusions and

Recommendations

7.1 Conclusions

Infrastructure  assets  should  be  managed  simultaneously  at  the  operational,  the
tactical and the strategic level which concern respectively the technical, the economical
and the societal performances of assets. Currently, TSO/DSO’s have started introducing
risk-based method into their AM decision making at the tactical level.

This  thesis  has  developed  two  statistical  methods  for  life  data  analysis,  the
reliability data model and the knowledge maturity model within the regime of risk-based
AM, as well as AM strategies, and our system diagram method as the essential parts of a
new  regime  called  strategic  AM.  From  these  methods  and  models,  the  below  main
conclusion can be formed.

The  strategic  level  of  asset  management  is  responsible  to  control  the  critical
characteristics  and  patterns  of  causalities  associated  with  long-term  risks.  The
means of such control, called AM strategy, can be precaution-based, risk-based or
information strategy.

Specifically,  our  investigation  within  the  risk-based  regime  has  established  a

roadmap towards risk-based decision making at the operational and tactical level.

According  to  our  reliability  data  model,  the  data  describing  the  reliability  of  an
asset to an asset manager can be divided into four categories according to the methods to
acquire  them.  They  are  service  failure  statistics,  accelerated  life  data,  operation  stress
data and diagnostic data.

Achieving Aim 1 of this thesis, described in Section 1.3:

1.  Statistical  analysis  on  service  failure  data  mainly  supports  risk-based  AM
strategy. It is applied at the tactical level to assess risks triggered by failures in
asset  population.  The  completeness  of  failure  and  installation  data  decides
whether  the  life  of  assets  can  be  statistically  estimated.  If  the  failure  records
and/or  installation  records  of  an  asset  population  is  partly  missing,  the  life  of

223

assets can still be modelled, through the Iterative Approximation Maximization
(I-AM) method we developed in Section 3.3.
Achieving Aim 2 of this thesis:

2.  Accelerated  life  data  can  only  support  precaution-based  AM  strategy  at  the
tactical  level.  Parametric  statistical  methods,  such  as  the  inverse  power  –
Weibull  method  we  applied  in  Section  3.4,  provide  interval  estimation  rather
than  point  estimation  of  the  lifetime.  However,  the  confidence  interval  on  an
estimated lifetime is normally wide. Thus, the confidence bounds can be applied
as  a  warranty,  while  the  maximum  likelihood  estimation  cannot  be  used  in
quantitative risk analysis.

3.  Operation stress data and diagnostic data supports respectively the precaution-
based and risk-based AM decisions on individual assets at the operational level
of AM.

Reliability  data  and  derived  knowledge  is  the  key  to  evolve  from  corrective
maintenance  to  risk-based  maintenance.  Correspondingly,  our  knowledge  maturity
levels (Aim  3  of  this  thesis) evaluate how much  knowledge and data are available to
support  the  quantitative  analysis  on  risks  triggered  by  unreliable  assets.  Therefore,
improving the knowledge maturity paves the way for risk-based decision making.

The knowledge maturity model reveals that long-term stakeholder-oriented risks of
owning  electrical  infrastructure  assets,  called  strategic  risks  in  this  thesis,  should  be
managed  at  the  strategic  level  of  AM.  Accordingly,  this  thesis  has  established  a  new
regime  called  strategic  AM  to  manage  strategic  risks.  The  strategic  AM  consists  of
several concepts and methodologies listed below.

Achieving Aim 4 of this thesis:

4.  The decision tree we developed in Section 5.2 classifies strategic risks into six
categories,  according  to  the  characteristics  of  strategic  risks  which  preventing
them  from  being  assessed  quantitatively.  Based  on  this  classification,  an  AM
strategy can be found as the countermeasure.
Achieving Aim 5 of this thesis:

5.  At  the  strategic  level,  risk-based  AM  strategy  concerns  the  knowledge  on  the

causal chain of strategic risks.

224

6.  Precaution-based  AM  strategy  reduces  a  certain  trigger/consequence  of  a
strategic risk to the minimum level. This requires less knowledge on the causal
chain of strategic risk than in case of risk-based AM strategy.

7.  Information  strategy  accumulates  data  and  knowledge,  which  is  critical  for

upgrading the management from precaution-based to risk-based.

8.  The  discourses  between  asset  specialists  and  stakeholders  reduce  the  strategic
risks  with  delay  effects  and  mental  consequences.  Such  discourses  can  be
enabled by information strategy.
Achieving Aim 6 of this thesis:

9.  The  causal  chains  of  multiple  strategic  risks  on  the  same  asset  population
interdepend on  each  other.  Our  system  diagram  models such interdependency.
Through using system diagrams in the procedure we developed in Section 6.2,
asset  management  can  identify  and  coordinate  multiple  AM  strategies  as  a
solution to multiple strategic risks.

10.  Through  combining  our  system  diagram  with  our  knowledge  maturity  model,
the roadmap of transiting from precaution-based AM strategy to risk-based AM
strategy  can  be  revealed.  From  the  roadmap,  an  asset  manager  can  also  plan
how  to  introduce  information  strategy  on  a  specific  asset  (sub)  population  in
long term.

In  our  investigation  on  strategic  AM,  we  defined  “flexibility”  as  an  important
feature  of  precaution-based  AM  strategy  in  response  to  the  high  uncertainty  of
predicting long-term demands. The role of “flexibility” in strategic AM is concluded as
below.

11.  In  AM,  a  flexible  precaution  should  have  meet  short-term  demands  on  asset
systems  and  asset  portfolio  with  low  change-costs.  Its  main  contribution  is:  to
delay decisions on alternative precautions, which can meet long-term demands
with high cost of changes, until the demand is ascertained.

12.  Currently,  the  flexibility  is  mostly  developed  for  demand  on  capacity.  But  in
future, the demands can be extended to other stakeholder requirements, such as
the environmental issues.

13.  For  investments  on  replacements  and  capacity  upgrades,  flexibility  can  be

introduced through controlling reliability according to condition data.

225

7.2 Recommendations for Further Research

This  thesis  has  developed  statistical  methods,  the  reliability  data  model,  the
knowledge  maturity  model,  AM  strategies  and  our  system  diagram  method  for
managing risks in electrical infrastructure assets. These concepts, methods and models
are still generic. We recommend extending this research in several aspects described in
the italic text.

1.  In AM practice, failure and life data from the field can be incomplete in many
different ways. Our I-AM method deals with the missing failure and installation
information before a certain calendar year. It is designed for DSO’s which have
a short history of failure data.
Statisticians should develop other methods dealing with other incomplete data
sets,  such  as  missing  failure  or  installation  data  in  a  certain  geographical
region or asset type.

2.  The four reliability data categories are identified for distinguishing precaution-
based  AM  strategy  from  risk-based  AM  strategy.  The  data  categories  can  be
further subdivided, especially according to the features of diagnosis and testing
methods.
Specialists  of  new  diagnostic  and  testing  methods  should  analyze  their  new
reliability  data  set  from  the  same  three  respects  as  in  Chapter  4,  namely  the
acquisition,  interpretation  and  decision  making.  The  analysis  will  reveal  the
proper moment to introduce the diagnostic/testing method.

3.  The knowledge maturity levels reveal the content of information which should
be  ready  for  supporting  risk-based  decision  making.  It  can  be  regarded  as  the
first step to build the information system for risk-based asset management. We
expect that two issues will be challenging from engineering perspective.
Asset  specialists  should  specify  the  content  of  generic  concepts  in  our
knowledge  maturity  model,  such  as  “the  functional  part  of  failure  mode”,  for
each asset group, type or population.
ICT specialists should design an extendable framework for the AM information
system. The extendibility means that: additional knowledge (e.g. a new failure
mode or condition indicator) can be added into the information system, without
reducing the value of existing data significantly.

226

4.  As  the  knowledge  maturity  indicates  the  applicability  of  a  risk-based  AM
strategy, the change cost indicates that of a precaution-based AM strategy. We
have  discussed  in  Chapter  6  that:  the  precautions  of  low  change  costs,  called
flexible solutions, can be tackle external risks stimulated by a political issue.
Therefore,  asset  managers,  especially  those  who  are  responsible  for  the  long-
term  planning  of  electrical  grids,  should  develop  “flexible”  technologies
according  to  the  external  risks  on  his  asset  portfolio  (e.g.  future  load  profile,
etc.).

5.  This  thesis  has  avoided  discussing  discourse-based  management,  since  we
believe  that  it  should  be  studied  in  humanity  rather  than  in  engineering  or
science.  However,  the  importance  of  communication  with  stakeholders  is
increasing in the TSO/DSO’s.
Asset  managers  should  cooperate  with  public  relationship  managers  in
TSO/DSO,  to  find  out  ways  to  identify  and  handle  the  risks  of  delay  effect  or
mental consequences.
Analysts  of  strategic  risks  should  also  use  discourses  to  identify  linkages
between  stakeholder  requirements.  Then,  our  system  diagram  method  can
reveal whether critical patterns can be formed due to these external linkages.

227

228

Appendix A Failure Rate Models and

Failure Data Analysis

The  time-constant  failure  rate  model  is  widely  applied,  primarily  because  of  one
reason: it estimates directly from failure data instead of from life data. Therefore, a low
requirement,  namely  completeness  degree
  defined  in  Table  3.1,  is  placed  on  raw
1
failure records.

⟨

⟩

For  utility  companies,  the  “failure  rate”  refers  to  “the  annual  number  of
failures/failure frequency”, i.e. the probability that a survived object fails in its operation
in the next year. Failure rate and annual failure frequency are different in mathematical
definition. However, they are equal in cases of utility companies, when ∆T =1 year and
TTF>>1  year,  λ  (T,  ∆T)  and  h(T)  are  approximately  equal.  When  the  failure  rate  is
available,  the  failure  frequency  can  be  estimated  with  the  models  introduced  in  this
section.

A.1 Time-Constant Failure Rate Model

Time-constant failure rate models can be applied when installation data is missing.
, and
. When data is more
,  the  failure  rate  can  be  calculated

In this section, the Binominal distribution is introduced for completeness degree
the Poisson distribution is introduced for completeness degree
complete  in  situations  such  as  degrees
more precisely using lifetime models that will be discussed in Appendix B.

1

4

⟨

⟩

⟨

⟩

⟨

⟩

⟨

⟩

As  Figure  3.1  shows  in  its  top  left  part,  the  failure  rate  models  are  based  on  one
assumption: the failure/hazard rate function is a constant, noted as λ. Consequently,
the annual failure probability is also λ, if the unit of time is in years. When N objects are
running in a certain year, the expected failure frequency in this year is Nλ.

Bionominal Distribution

The  Binominal  distribution  can  be  applied  to  estimate  λ  in  the  situation  below.

•  Ni objects were in operation, and
•  ni failures occurred.

Summing these two time series, the total amount of operation and the total number

of failure can be obtained respectively as

•  NΣ=∑Ni (i=1, …, k) (unit: object∙year)
•  nΣ=∑ni (i=1, …, k) (unit: times)

The  model  treats  “running/stressing  an  object  for  one  year”  as  a  Bernoulli  trial,
from which two mutually exclusive results can be observed – survival or failure. If the
probability  of  failure  in  each  Bernoulli  trial is  λ,  the total  number  of failures  nΣ  in  NΣ
Bernoulli trials follows a Binomial distribution B(NΣ , λ).

The procedure to apply Binominal distribution is as follows:

When  nΣ  and  NΣ  are  known  from  the  data,  λ  can  be  estimated  as  nΣ/NΣ.  And  the

annual number of failure in N objects can be estimated as N∙nΣ/NΣ.

Poisson Distribution

The Poisson distribution can be applied to failure data of a completeness degree
.
This means the exact population Ni or N is unknown. Instead, Ni is assumed to remain
⟩
equal to N within the period of investigation. For an asset population, it means that the
failed  objects  will  be  replaced  with  one  of  the  same  type,  which  keeps  the  total
population at a constant number N.

1

⟨

The Poisson distribution is an approximation of Binominal distribution, when N is
large (>20), but the expected number of failures (Nλ for one year) is small (typically <5).
It is usually common to assume that the annual number of failures Nλ follows a Poisson
distribution.

The  procedure  to  apply  Poisson  distribution  is  as  follows:  Treat  the  annual

number of failure as ni samples of Nλ. Then, Nλ can be estimated as nΣ /k.

230

A.2 Time-dependent Failure Rate and Bathtub Curve

In industrial applications, the failure rate is not a constant within the period of time
that spans the whole lifecycle. Instead, it changes according to the well-known bathtub
curve.

The  bathtub  curve  is  the  typical  shape  of  the  failure  rate  observed  from  asset
population operated in the field. Figure A.1 demonstrates that the bathtub curve at the
top is the total rate of failures of three categories. They are

•

Infant  mortality.  It  is  mainly  caused  by  design  problems,  installation
hazards, etc. It is normally high at the beginning of operation but decreases
quickly after several years of operation.

•  Random  failures.  When  subcomponents  are  aging  but  recovered/repair

regularly, the failure rate of the component is time-invariant.

•  Wear-out failures. Normally, the failures result from (a series of) complex
aging  mechanism(s).  Wear-out  failures  are  due  to  poor  maintainability.
Therefore, a wear-out failure normally indicates the end of useful life of a
component population. Thus, it increases the failure rate with service life.

Due to different failure rate vs. service life features, an asset life cycle can also be
divided into three phases named “infant mortality”, “random failures” and “wear-out”.
In  practice,  in  less  technical  terms,  the  three  phases  are  frequently  called  “early  life”,
“mid-life” and “late life”.

Figure A.1 shows the three phases with green, yellow and red color respectively on
the  bathtub  curve.  The  curve  is  sharply  decreasing,  relatively  constant  and  steadily
increasing in the three phases. The boundary between different phases does not need to
be defined precisely; therefore, the color changes are shown with a gradient.

Failure  rate  models  are  difficult  to  apply  to  the  entire  life  cycle  of  an  asset
population, because of two reasons. On one hand, the time-constant failure rate model is
only  for  the  mid-life  when  the  deterioration  is  caused  by  random  factors,  since  the
failure rate is not increase/decrease steadily in this phase of life cycle. It is not suitable
to be the decision support tool of AM strategy against asset aging, since the failure rate
tends to increase in an old asset population due to a certain dominant aging mechanism.

231

On the other hand, a time-dependent failure rate model is difficult to apply in utility
companies. Assets within a population are installed during a long time span. In a certain
calendar year, the number of failures is the sum of failure rates of each asset at its own
age (service year). Therefore, the form of the mathematical function of failure frequency
is  difficult  to  determine.  These  limits  motivate  the  failure  analysts  to  employ  lifetime
models to be introduced in the next section.

Figure A.1 An simulated bathtub curve demostrating three stages of failure rate, which are infant

mortality in green, random failures in yellow and wearout (aging) failures in red.

232

Appendix B Applying the Weibull

Distribution in Life Data Analysis

Failure  rate  models  fit  the  failure  frequency  into  probability  distributions.  In
contrast, life data analysis fits TTF or TBF data, rather than failure frequency data, into
probability  distributions.  Thus  these  probability  distributions  are  called  “lifetime
models”.  Through  utilizing  the  installation  data,  lifetime  models  can  break  the  two
limits of failure rate models. This section will introduce a basic method which has been
successfully  applied  to  analyze  life  data  of  high  voltage  assets  [61,  62].  This  method
uses the Weibull distribution as the lifetime model. It is systemically introduced in [85,
111] and our introduction is based on [65]. This section lays the foundation for Section
3.3 and 3.4, in which we develop new Weibull analysis method for incomplete life data
and accelerated life data respectively.

B.1 Weibull Distribution

Weibull  distribution  was  invented  to  model  the  TTF  of  steel  material  in  1951.
Afterwards,  it  has  been  accepted  as  standard  models  for  TTF  for  dielectrics,  high-
voltage insulation constructions and high-voltage components [112]. For simplicity, this
thesis will only discuss the 2-parameter Weibull distribution. Other lifetime models such
as Normal, Log-normal, Gumbel, Logistic distributions are left for future researches.

The CDF and hazard function of 2-parameter Weibull distribution are shown below:

B.2 Estimate Exponential Lifetime Distribution through Time-

Constant Failure Rate Model
As mentioned in the last section, an exponentially distributed lifetime is essentially
described by a constant failure-rate model. Therefore, in theory, the procedure to apply
exponential distribution can be performed by using a time-constant failure rate model.

In other words, when the data is of completeness degree

, it cannot be converted
into a TTF as was mentioned in Section 3.1.2. However, the hazard rate can actually be
calculated with the Binominal estimator λ = nΣ/NΣ. Moreover, the Mean-Time-Between-

2

⟨

⟩

234

Failure  (MTBF),  as  the  expectation  value  of  TBF,  is  1/λ.  Accordingly,  it  can  be
concluded that the TBF is exponentially distributed with parameter λ.

However, in practice, we recommend to use the failure rate λ only for a short-term
prediction of the failure frequency. Compared to a complete lifecycle, the period that the
failure data is of completeness degree
 is short. Therefore, the MTBF estimated from
it  is  not  convincing  enough  to  support  long-term  plans,  such  as  replacement  of  asset
population or other strategies in Chapter 5.

2

⟨

⟩

B.3 Maximum Likelihood Estimator of Weibull Distribution

Parameters
When failure data is above completeness degree

, a TTF is available and a “real”
life data analysis can be performed. In this section, we will only introduce the maximum
likelihood (ML) approach. Other estimation methods, such as rank regressions, can be
found  in  [Rogier  thesis,  Wbl  tech  book].  We  select  maximum  likelihood  estimation
(MLE) as our main estimator for several reasons:

3

⟨

⟩

•  MLE  is  asymptotically  unbiased,  i.e.  the  estimation  approaches  the  true
value  of  the  estimated  parameter  with  the  growth  of  sample  size.
Empirically, more than 5 TTF samples are required for MLE, which can be
satisfied for most life data sets.

•  MLE can be applied for the majority of probability distributions commonly
used  in  life  data  analysis,  such  as  Normal,  Lognormal,  Weibull,  Gamma,
Logisitic, Gumbel, etc. [65]

•  To  deal  with  accelerated  life  test  data,  the  lifetime  distributions  can  be
extended  with  life  vs.  stress  relationships.  MLE  can  be  applied  on  these
models. Section 3.4 applies these models.

•  MLE can be applied to (heavily) censored data, and the estimator remains

asymptotical unbiased. [65]

•  From  MLE,  the  confidence  intervals  can  be  easily  calculated  by  using  a
Fisher information matrix (see Appendix B.5) and likelihood ratio [111]
•  MLE can be extended with the Expectation-Maximization (EM) theory to
deal  with  the  partly  missing  installation/deployment  data  (between

235

B.4 Estimate Failure Frequency from Hazard Rate

Typically,  life  data  analysis  gives  the  annual  failure  frequency  within  an
investigated asset population. When the installation data is available, the lifetime model
(e.g. Weibull) can provide the analyst with a time-dependent hazard rate function. This
enables  the analyst to judge  whether the  failure  frequency  is  increasing,  which further
indicates whether the asset population is aging.

The hazard rate function for a Weibull distribution is:

B.5 Calculation of Confidence Intervals via Fisher Information

Matrix
Statistical  analysis  is  based  on  limited  number  of  observed  data.  It  goes  without
saying that the conclusions drawn from a larger number of observed data will be more
credible. One way to indicate this credibility is usage of confidence bounds/intervals as
defined below:

Given  a  set  of  observed  data  (TTF  in  this  chapter)  and  a  certain  probability  α%
(called confidence level), the statistical parameter (normally a continuous number) has a:

237

•  probability  1-  α%  to  be  larger  than  its  α%  (one-sided)  lower  confidence

bound (LCB)

•  probability 1-  α% to be smaller than its α% (one-sided) upper confidence

bound (UCB)

•  probability 1- α%/2 to be larger/smaller than the upper/smaller bound of its

α% confidence interval (CI)

Hence, CI is a type of interval estimate of a statistical parameter.

In order to estimate the exact CI, the conditional distribution of the parameters with
given  observed  data,  called  posterior  distribution,  needs  to  be  calculated.  This
calculation is usually difficult. Therefore, statisticians have developed different methods
to acquire approximate posterior distributions or confidence intervals. The selection of
one  of  these  methods  depends  on  the  probability  model  (e.g.  Normal,  Log-normal,
Gumbel,  Logistic  etc.  to  model  TTF),  the  estimation  method  of  parameter  (e.g.  rank
regression, maximum likelihood, etc.), the data volume and the data quality.

This  section  will  only  discuss  the  procedure  to  analytically  calculate  CI  of
Weibull-distributed  TTF  through  applying  the  Fisher  information  matrix.  This
approach is modified in Section 3.5 for accelerated life analysis.

Step 1: Given a large number of data points, the posterior distributions of the two

Weibull parameters are:

N(μ, σ) is the normal distribution with mean μ and standard deviation σ. LogN  is
the  lognormal  distribution.  If  x  follows  LogN(μ,  σ),  then  ln(x)  follows  N(μ,  σ).  To
emphasize that the two parameters of a log-normal distribution are not its (arithmetic)
mean and deviation, we mark the parameter μ and σ of a lognormal distribution with a
tilde hat.

likelihood function in equation (B.7):

B.6 Summary of Applications of Weibull Model

This section summarizes the standard life data analysis method with the commonly
accepted  Weibull  distribution.  Generally  speaking,  fitting  TTF  data  into  a  Weibull
distribution has two primary functions. The first function is to predict failure frequency,
as  Section  B.4  has  shown.  The  second  function  is  to  predict  TTF  with  a  certain
reliability and confidence levels, as Section B.5 has shown. The significances of these
two functions in AM strategies are described in Chapter 4.

Meanwhile,  the  two  functions  of  Weibull  modeling  support  the  contributions  of
thesis  to  life  analysis  in  the  next  two  sections.  In  Section  3.3,  the  function  of  failure
frequency  prediction  is  extended  in  a  real  situation,  namely  the  incomplete  life  data
analysis. In Section 3.4, the interval estimation on TTF will be applied to the laboratory
testing data observed at different stress levels.

240

Appendix C Using Condition Data to
Support Maintenance Scheduling

C.1 Definition of Maintenance Scheduling and Planning

Maintenance  scheduling  is  the  decision-making  process  performed  by  service
providers at the operational level of AM. As its name suggests, decisions are made on
when  to  perform  certain  maintenance  methods  specified  by  maintenance  plans.
Obviously, each decision is made for an individual asset. In order to clarify the above
definition  of  maintenance  scheduling,  its  difference  from  maintenance  planning,
replacement scheduling and maintenance strategies are described below.

Maintenance  scheduling  should  be  distinguished  from  maintenance  planning.
Maintenance plans are decisions made at the tactical level of AM. They formulate which
maintenance and diagnosis methods should be applied to a certain type of asset. From
the technical perspective, maintenance planning is frequently considered to be a part of
standardization of assets and corresponding LC Activities.

Maintenance  scheduling  and  maintenance  planning  are  the  two  types  of

decisions made in maintenance management.

Difference with Replacement Scheduling

Replacement  or  disposal  of  individual  assets  can  also  be  scheduled.  From  the
technical  perspective,  when  replacements  are  scheduled,  the  reliability  assessment  can
be performed with the same method as maintenance scheduling, or even within the same
organization as maintenance scheduling. For example, service providers can implement
decision support procedures to propose which asset should be replaced.

From  the  financial  and  management  perspective,  however,  maintenance  costs  and
replacement  investments  are  considered  separately  as OPEX  and  CAPEX in  corporate
finance. Therefore, the asset manager (for large capital investments, the asset owner) is
the decision maker who ultimately approves replacement plans and schedules.

241

In  addition,  replacements  provide  opportunities  to  modify  maintenance  plans.
Technically, assets can be reconstructed to integrate advanced sensors. Financially, the
investments on these sensors can be approved together with the replacement itself.

Difference with Maintenance Strategies

Maintenance  strategies  can  also  be  condition-based,  reliability-centered  or  risk-
based.  This  makes  them  frequently  to  be  confused  with  maintenance  scheduling.
However,  maintenance  strategies  are  a  set  of  principles  guiding  maintenance  planning
and  scheduling.  It  can  be  regarded  as  a  subset  of  AM  strategies,  as  maintenance  is  a
subset of lifecycle activities.

In  [40],  maintenance  strategies  are  narrowly  defined  for  a  specific  type  of  asset

system as the combination of.

•

•

a  set  of  predetermined  diagnostic/repair  methods  (included  maintenance
plan in our model), and
a recommended diagnosis, maintenance or replacement schedule

However,  in  Section  4.4.1  and  C.5,  we  show  that  this  only  reaches  the  maturity
level “standard”, while the risk-based AM strategy require its maintenance strategies to
be further “optimized”.

Risk-based Maintenance Scheduling – an Ultimate Goal

Reliability data can support maintenance scheduling at the operational level of AM.
In  [40],  a  complete  process  to  make  decisions  on  maintenance  and  replacement
schedules is introduced. It uses the advanced risk-based approach described in [113].

This appendix introduces a generic way to apply condition and diagnostic data in
risk-based maintenance scheduling. It shows that the risk-based approach requires large
amounts of knowledge to implement. Today’s maintenance management, as mentioned
in Chapter 1, is still time-based, since this decision-support knowledge is not available.
Therefore, our introduction aims to fill the gap between the current state of maintenance
management and the ultimate state of risk-based decision-making.

242

C.2 Indicating Failure Probability of Individual Assets with

Condition Indicator and Health Indices
A condition indicator (CI) typically uses an ordinal categorical variable to rate the
probability  that  a  component  can  fail  due  to  certain  failure  mechanism.  In  contrast,  a
health index (HI) also uses an ordinal categorical variable to rate the failure probability
of  a  component,  but  regardless  of  its  failure  mechanism.  In  practice,  a  CI  or  HI  is
typically  an  adjective,  such  as  “good”,  “fair”,  “moderate”,  “poor”,  or  “bad”.  These
adjectives  are  “ordinal”  because  they  can  be  ranked.  In  our  opinion,  the  failure
probabilities of a series of CI value should form a geometric sequence, so that it matches
the CI values with the probability indicators from “nearly possible” to “permanent” in
the risk matrix introduced in Section 2.2.4. As a result, the technical CI’s of components
become understandable to the risk analysts.

In an ideal condition assessment system, a HI is composed from several CI’s. This
implies that a component can fail due to multiple mechanisms. It also implies that each
CI  is  derived  from  multiple  reliability/condition  data  sources.  To  comply  with  the
indication  system,  the  reliability/condition  data  are  also  interpreted  as  ordinal
indicator  (DCI),  statistical
categorical  variables,  called  diagnostic  condition
remaining life indicator (SRLI) and operation stress indicator (OSI) respectively in
this chapter.

SRLI-1

 of Component A

CI-1

of Component A

CI-2

of Component A

DCI-1

of Component A

SRLI-2

of Component A

DCI-2

of Component A

DCI-3

of Component A

SRLI-3

of Component A

OSI-1

of Component A

HI of Component A
