Figure  C.1  shows  how  a  HI  is  assembled  from  DCI’s,  SRLI’s,  OSI’s  and  CI’s.
Table  C.1  firstly  explains  generically  the  data  source  of  indicators  listed  shown  in
Figure C.1, then provides examples of these data sources in the case of oil-filled cable.
The examples are extracted from [26].

Figure C.1 and Table C.1 also show how the HI can be stepwise established. When
visual  inspection  skills  are  available  and  root-cause  studies  are  performed,  a  primary
condition indicator CI-1 can be added to detect a critical failure mechanism. Compared
with CI-1, CI-2 is more complete because it contains all condition data sources.  These
additional  data  sources  reveal  more  details  about  the  aging,  but  also  require  more
equipment, personnel, knowledge and investments. Section C.3 to C.5 will focus on the
developing process of maintenance scheduling techniques.

244

C.3 Inferring Failure Probability of Asset Systems

CI and HI reflect the failure probability of individual assets. According to them, the
probability of asset system failures can be inferred, with the assistance from knowledge
on failure modes.

The Subsystem Level

When  the  failures  of  individual  high-voltage  asset  systems  are  discussed,  a  layer
between  assets  and  asset  systems,  called  subsystems,  is  frequently  used.  In  electricity
networks,  a  subsystem  is  typically  connected  to  other  subsystems  via  circuit  breakers.
Therefore,  a  subsystem  can  be  entirely  disconnected  from  the  network,  in  order  to
isolate  failures  or  to  perform  maintenance.  Subsystems  that  can  be  isolated  are  more
common at higher voltage levels, as a natural result of their multiple circuits and mesh
topology.  Maintenance  tasks  on  different  components  in  the  same  subsystem  can  be
coordinated to reduce outage minutes, see Section C.3 for details.

According to the above definition, at the subsystem level, a failure mode is mainly

studied in three aspects, regarding its effects:

(1)  Interruption: An asset failure has interrupted operation of the subsystem
(2)  Escalation: The failure “escalates”, i.e. spreads to other subsystems or the
entire asset system. The protection system, if existed, was unable to isolate
its  consequences  within  the  subsystem.  Sometimes,  the  escalations  are
caused by violent side-effects of certain failure mechanisms.

(3)  Insufficient  recovery:  Asset  workers  are  not  capable  to  restore  the
subsystem  completely  for  a  certain  period.  Within  this  period,  the
subsystem is operated with less capabilities, lower capacities and/or lower
redundancy.

Among  the  three  aspects,  interruption  and  insufficient  recovery  belong  to  the
functional part of a failure mode. Escalation can be caused by either the functional part
or the side- effect part of a failure mechanism. However, a properly designed protection
system  should  prevent  the  functional  part  of  escalation  from  occurring.  See  Section
4.1.2 for definitions of the functional and side-effect parts.

The  probability  that  interruption,  escalation  and  insufficient  recovery  occur  in  a
subsystem differs. Escalation depends on the side-effects, so it relies on specific failure
mechanisms  and  CI’s,  while  the  other  two  aspects  only  consider  the  HI.  Furthermore,

245

escalation and insufficient recovery, as indirect effect of failures, are more probable to
be  caused  by  some  critical  components  than  the  others  [57].  Therefore,  these  critical
components need to be identified before reliability assessments of asset systems. Table
C.2 summarizes the differences mentioned above as below.

TABLE C.2:  PROBABILITY OF INTERRUPTION, ESCALATION AND INSUFFICIENT RECOVERY CAUSED

BY A SUBSYSTEM FAILURE ARE INFERRED FROM CONDITION INDICATORS AND HEALTH INDICES IN

DIFFERENT WAYS

Failure
effect

Escalation

Reactions and effects on reliability

protection system, or manually by asset

other assets in or out of the subsystem.
These damages can further enlarge the

The restored subsystem cannot carry its
rated load. Temporary solutions, such

components

The Asset System Level

An asset system uses CI of components and reliability of subsystems to assess its
own  reliability.  Unlike  the  subsystem  level,  reliability  assessment  at  the  asset  system
level should provide a complete image on the failure mode, i.e. estimate the occurring
probability of both the functional part and the side-effect part.

The functional part of asset system failure is inferred from reliability of subsystems

in four steps:

1)  Calculate  the  unreliability  of  each  subsystem  through  summing  the

probability of its interruption and insufficient recovery.

2)  Find  out  all  scenarios  of  asset  system  failures  through  checking  how
subsystems  are  connected  with  each  other.  For  example,  in  a  double  bus
substation layout, “failure of both buses” and “failure of one bus and the
bus coupler” are two scenarios of asset system failures.

246

3)  Calculate the probability of all scenarios of asset system failures.

o  The  simplest  calculation  method  assumes  that  subsystem  failures
are  independent  from  each  other.  Therefore,  the  probability  that
subsystem A and B fail at the same time is simply the multiplying
product of their unreliability values.

o  Escalation  is  the  situation  that  subsystem  failures  are  correlated.
When the probability of escalation is added, the probability of asset
system failures is calculated more accurately.

4)  Sum the probabilities of all scenarios.

When  the  outage  of  an  asset  system  is  the  only  concern  in  maintenance
management,  the  above  calculation  should  be  sufficient.  However,  when  the  failure
effects on other KPI’s are considered, the side-effects of asset system failures need to be
estimated through summarizing

•

•

the CI’s of each component, which indicates the probability of hazards of
side-effect at asset level
the  probability  of  escalation  of  each  subsystem,  which  indicates  the
possibilities  that  side  effects  are  enlarged  at  subsystem  and  asset  system
level.

In  general,  the  side-effects  part  is  more  difficult  to  quantify  into  probabilities
compared to the functional part. Therefore, they are only used in advanced maintenance
scheduling methods. See Section C.5 for details.

C.4 Definition of Failure Scenarios and Technical Scenarios

Failure Scenarios

Aging mechanisms, failure mechanisms and failure modes occur in a sequence on
certain  assets  and  asset  systems.  We  term  a  particular  sequence  of  these  events  as
“failure scenario”. A failure scenario is a potential incident that a failure mode occurs in
a specific asset system. Failure scenarios are studied for individual asset systems, thus it
should be distinguished from failure modes. Table C.3 shows the differences in several
aspects. These differences show that in maintenance scheduling, failure scenarios, rather
than failure modes, are assessed as risks.

247

TABLE C.3:  DIFFERENCES BETWEEN FAILURE MODES AND FAILURE SCENARIOS

Maintenance Methods, Tasks, Programs and Technical Scenarios

Maintenance  methods  can  be  applied  on  assets  to  prevent  certain  failure
mechanisms and their consequent failure modes. In contrast, a maintenance tasks is an
instance of applying a maintenance method, with its target asset and schedule specified.
The  relationship  between  maintenance  methods  and  tasks  is  similar  to  that  between
failure modes and scenarios.

At  the  subsystem  level,  multiple  maintenance  tasks  can  be  performed  in  a
subsystem  at  the  same  time.  Such  a  composition  of  maintenance  tasks  can  reduce  the
costs and outage minutes. The composed groups are called maintenance programs.

A  technical  scenario  describes  how  maintenance  programs(s)  affect  the  failure
scenarios.  When  a  maintenance  method  can  prevent  a  failure  mechanism,  its
effectiveness can be quantified as the improvements of corresponding CI’s. Accordingly,
given that a certain maintenance program has been performed, its improvements on the
reliability of an asset system can predicted. Therefore, a technical scenario consists of
two parts:

•  The maintenance program part, including

248

o

the  maintenance  tasks  (methods,  target  assets  and  schedules),  as
well as how they are coordinated within an asset system
the technical resources it requires, especially the outage schedules,
spare parts, transportation services, and safety services.
•  The  failure  scenario  part,  describing  its  expected  improvements  on  asset

o

reliability.

The  numbers  of  technical  scenarios  considered  in  AM  decisions  are  different.  It

depends on the AM strategy and scheduling techniques to be introduced in Section C.5.

C.5 Decision Making Methods for Maintenance Scheduling

Condition-based Method for Maintenance Scheduling

The condition-based maintenance scheduling method prioritizes maintenance tasks
on  assets/components  of  the  same  type.  It  only  investigates  failures  at  the  component
level, frequently even without identifying failure mechanisms. The failure probabilities
are  compared  within  an  asset  population,  while  the  failure  modes  and  effects  are  not
considered. Its application is based on the three assumptions below.

A.  The consequences of failures are considered to be similar.
B.  The technical scenarios are simple, i.e. the choices are to maintain or not in

short term (typically 1 fiscal year as defined in Table 2.1).

C.  The  constraints  are  predetermined,  according  to  availability  of  budget  or

other resources.

These assumptions can be invalid in the “reliability centered method”.

As  an  optimization  process,  the  decision-making  process  can  be  described  with

three elements:

•  Goal:  to  minimize  the  failure  frequency  within  an  asset  population.  The

asset population consists of assets of the same type.

•  Constraint: the maximum number of assets which can be maintained.
•  Process:

(1)  Condition indexing: interpret the acquired raw diagnostic data, life
data and operation stress data as DCI, SRLI and OSI respectively.

249

(2)  Condition assessment: compose DCI, SRLI and OSI into CI and HI

for each asset.
(3)  Decision making:

i.  Rank the assets according to their HI’s.
ii.  Maintain assets with highest ranks in short term.
iii.  Delay maintenance on the others until the next scheduling

period.

Reliability-centered Method for Maintenance Scheduling

Reliability performance of TSO/DSO’s is measured with SAIFI and SAIDI, which
sum the frequencies and durations of asset system failures respectively. Therefore, the
reliability performance treats the major failures on different asset systems equally.

Based on this assumption, the goal, constraint and approach for reliability-centered

maintenance scheduling are:

•  Goal:  to  minimize  the  frequency  that  outages  occur  in  asset  systems.  The
outage can occur randomly due to failures or at a scheduled moment due to
maintenance.  In  systems  with  redundancy,  outage  due  to  maintenance  is
very rare, therefore frequently not taken into account11.

•  Constraint:  the  technical  resources  available  for  maintenance  activities,

including spare parts, total outage minutes, etc.

•  Process:

(1)  Condition  indexing  and  condition  assessment:  acquire  CI’s,  and
HI’s for all components, as the condition-based method does in its
step (1) and (2).

(2)  Reliability  assessment:  for  each  failure  scenario,  calculate  the
unreliability of subsystems and asset systems according to Section
4.3.3.

(3)  Search for a technical scenario:

i.  Find out feasible maintenance programs

11 Failure on the systems whose redundancy is reduced due to maintenance can lead to outages.
These outages are rare, but they normally have severe consequences. They are not analyzed by

the tactical level, but considered as strategic risks of type “Damocles” in Chapter 5.

250

ii.  Find  out  the  failure  scenarios  after  performing  these

maintenance programs

iii.  Perform reliability assessment on the failure scenarios after

maintenance

(4)  Benefit analysis: calculate the benefit of a technical scenario as its

improvement on asset system reliability
(5)  Decision-making:  Rank  benefits  of
prioritize the maintenance programs.

technical  scenarios  and

In the reliability-centered optimization process, assets of different types need not to

be considered separately. This breaks Assumption A of condition-based method.

However, the reliability-centered method only optimizes the maintenance services
in short term. Since financial costs are not considered, a balance between performance
and  cost  is  unlikely  to  be  achieved.  Therefore,  more  than  the  necessary  maintenance
tasks tend to be performed, and thus, maintenance strategies with financial advantages
but  little  contribution  to  reliability  tend  to  be  excluded  from  technical  scenarios.
Accordingly, Assumption B and C of the condition-based method can also be applied to
reliability-centered method.

Risk-based Method for Maintenance Scheduling

The risk-based method is the advanced stage of maintenance scheduling. Similar to
the  reliability-centered  method,  it  firstly  looks  for  technical  scenarios,  then  makes
decisions  according  to  their  benefits.  These  benefits,  however,  are  measured  by  the
KPI’s  in  the  risk  consequence  matrix  introduced  in  Section  2.2.4,  rather  than  merely
outage frequency/duration in the reliability-centered method.

Therefore, the goal, constraint and approach for risk-based maintenance scheduling

are:

•  Goal: to minimize the sum of expenditure and expected performance loss.
•  Constraint: the budget available for maintenance activities, as well as other

strategic limits, such as safety and environment regulations.

•  Process:

(1)  Perform  condition  indexing,  condition  assessment,  reliability
assessment  and  technical  scenario  searching,  as  the  reliability-
centered method does in its step (1), (2) and (3).

251

(2)  Failure criticality analysis:

i.  Failure modes are registered as risks at the tactical level. In
the risk register process, technical failure consequences of
failure  modes  are  assessed  economically  according  to
KPI’s.

ii.  For each failure scenario, use the risk register information

to specify its economic effects.

iii.  Meanwhile,  its  probability  of  occurrence  can  be  assessed

through reliability assessment.

iv.  The expected loss of performance of a technical scenario,
called  criticality
through
in  FMECA,
multiplying the probability and the consequence in the last
two steps.

is  calculated

(3)  Cost estimation:

i.  A maintenance program requires spare parts, transportation
service  and  manpower.  They  are  proposed  by  service
providers,  validated  by  financial  affairs  as  costs  and
controlled in service contracts.

ii.  Risks  are  involved  in  maintenance  delivery.  Personnel  at
field  are  more  exposed  to  safety  hazards.  Engineering
activities  can  damage  the  environment  or  the  nature.  The
public  usually  dislike  the  interruptions  and  the  nuisance
associated  with  maintenance.  Measures  to  prevent  these
risks should also be considered as costs.

(4)  Benefit analysis: calculate the benefit of a technical scenario as the

sum of its expected loss of performance and costs.

(5)  Decision-making:  rank  benefits  of
prioritize the maintenance programs.

technical  scenarios  and

Evolvement of Maintenance Scheduling Methods

The  above  three  subsections  have  shown  that  through  utilizing  condition  data,
especially  diagnostic  data,  maintenance  scheduling  can  be  performed  with  condition-
based method, reliability-centered method or risk-based method. Figure C.2 summarizes
the processes of these three methods.

252

Figure  C.2  shows  that  the  three  methods  represent  different  stages  within  the
evolvement  of  maintenance  scheduling.  The  reliability-centered  method (in  blue)  adds
reliability  assessment  to  the  condition-based  method  (in  yellow),  while  the  risk-based
method (in green) adds criticality analysis and cost estimation to the reliability-centered
method.

Figure C.2:  Information flows of different maintenance scheduling methods shows that they are

supported by different knowledge.

253

Such evolvement is not enabled by measurement or communication systems, but by
knowledge marked by purple cylinders in Figure C.2. The concept of these knowledge
domains are introduced in Section 4.4.1, as a part of our knowledge maturity model.

254

Appendix D Evolvement of Maintenance

Planning Procedures

The maintenance planning procedure can evolve from the ad-hoc level to the risk-
based level. Such evolvement is achieved through extending its three steps. This section
firstly  introduces  the  three  steps  at  different  maturity  levels,  and  then  summarizes  the
evolvement  process  in  Figure  D.1  and  D.2.  The  procedures  introduced  in  this  section
reflect the concerns of a Dutch DSO, but not yet ready for implementation, because they
still  needs  to  be  adapted  to  the  business  model  and  organization  of  the  implementing
company.

The ad-hoc level

Step 1: Deduct prototype failure modes from the design

The ad-hoc method is used when the asset manager has recorded very few cases of
the  asset  failures.  In  this  situation,  it  is  difficult  for  failure  analysts  to  perform  proper
FMEA to statistically identify risky failure mechanisms from operation history. Instead,
the  top-down  Fault  Tree  Analysis  (FTA)  is  more  feasible,  as  the  first  block  in  Figure
D.1 shows. From FTA, prototype failure modes can be deduced from the design. These
prototype  failure  modes  form  the  first  knowledge  on  failure  mechanisms  and  failure
modes.

Step 2: Search for preventive maintenance (PM) methods

In the early stage of an asset lifecycle, very few technical actions are available as
maintenance  methods.  As  a  maintenance  method,  a  technical  action  must  be  able  to
prevent,  and/or  correct  a  failure  mode.  Accordingly,  “knowledge  on  maintenance
methods” can be primarily established through the below activities:
(PM)  plans

•  Distinguish  preventive  maintenance

from  corrective
maintenance  (CM)  plans,  as  the  white  blocks  in  Figure  D.1  shows.
Standardize them.

•  Associate each PM/CM plan with the failure modes it can tackle.

255

Step 3: Scheduling method is empirically time-based

Condition  indexing  is  likely  to  be  absent  at  the  ad-hoc  level.  However,  some
minimum level of scheduling can be performed through setting a fixed interval for each
PM plan. This maintenance interval can be decided empirically by experts. Figure D.2
does not include such an ad-hoc process.

The repeatable level

Step 1: Identify failure modes from failure statistics

When  a  small  volume  of  failure  data  is  available  for  the  asset  manager,  some
clusters  of  similar  failures  emerge.  These  clusters  can  be  matched  with  the  prototype
failure modes deducted from theory and expert knowledge. Through the matching, some
failure modes with highest frequencies can be revealed.

Based on the existing failure records, FMEA can also be performed in a simplified

form as below:

•  The failures severity can be empirically investigated from occurred failure

cases.

•  The failure probability is estimated from the failure rate model introduced
in  Section  3.2.  The  data  source  can  be  externally  acquired  from  multiple
companies or knowledge-sharing organizations.

•  The chance of detection is normally not a concern due to shortage of data.

The severity and probability help to define the risk level of the failure mode.

Step 2: Update PM plans according to the FMEA result

In this step, the failure modes of the highest risks are firstly discovered, and then

PM plans are developed to tackle them.

Meanwhile,  some  failure  modes  have  relatively  small  local  effects  but  large  end-

effects on the system. For them, it is critical to check

•  whether  protection  systems  are  capable  to  isolate  failure  within  an

individual subsystem and to prevent cascading failures

•  whether automated solutions (e.g. geographical asset information systems)
are  capable  to  locate  the  faults,  and  thus  to  reduce  the  reaction  time  and
repair time.

256

It  is  necessary  to  adapt  PM  plans  to  the  protection  systems  and  fault  location
systems, according to their effectiveness to reduce the failure consequence. However, if
the failure consequences cannot be reduced to an acceptable level, reinvestment, or even
replacement plans need to be considered. The yellow diamonds at the middle of Figure
D.1 indicates this process.

Step 3: Introduce condition indicators

In case that the experience on failures and knowledge on aging is limited, the low-

cost approaches to assess and indicate asset conditions are introduced, such as:

•  Diagnostic indicators provided by inspections on components and/or built-

in gauges (e.g. pressure on gas insulations)

•  Remaining  life  indicators  established  from  fixed  maintenance  intervals.
Each  interval  is  calculated  from  a  time-constant  failure  rate  estimated  in
FMEA.

Step  1,  2  and  3  above  only  represent  the  yellow  blocks  in  Figure  D.2.  The

simplified DCI and SRLI provide a PM plan with basic scheduling capabilities.

The standard level

Step 1: Perform criticality analysis

At the standard level, FMEA is extended as FMECA, as the second block in Figure
D.1 shows. The major difference is that the severity (consequences) and the probability
need to be standardized, so that different failure modes can be compared with each other
across the asset portfolio. Specifically,

•

•

failures of asset systems, as the major consequence of failure modes, need
to be associated with failure of components. This forms the “knowledge on
failure modes”.
failure  probabilities  need  to  consider  asset  aging.  Therefore,  life  data
analysis  needs  to  be  performed  for  some  failure  modes,  in  addition  to
estimating time-constant failure rates.

According to the criticality of a failure mode, its countermeasure can be generally

selected from:

•  maintaining the asset population correctively, if the criticality is low.
•  maintaining  the  asset  population  preventively,  with  proper  scheduling

techniques, if the criticality is medium but controllable.

257

•

replacing the asset population, if the criticality is high or PM plans are not
available/feasible.

In addition, some optional action can be taken together with the maintenance and

replacements to limit the failure effects.

•
•

Install fault isolation systems to minimize the range of failures
Install  fault  location  systems  to  accelerate  CM  procedure  and  reduce
duration of outages

•  Re-rate the components permanently or dynamically, in order to reduce the

probability of triggering a failure mode.

As a result, the criticality analysis balances the risks in different parts of the asset

portfolio.

Step 2: Study root cause of critical failure modes

Failure modes with medium to high criticality are normally tackled with expensive
PM  or  replacement  plans.  Therefore,  it  is  worthwhile  to  explore  new  PM  methods  or
scheduling  methods  to  reduce  the  considerable  expenses.  Such  exploration  can  be
started  with  root  cause  studies,  which  aims  to  acquire  more  “knowledge  on  failure
mechanisms” of components.

A root-cause study frequently involves

•  Life data analysis on asset populations grouped according to certain factor

suspected to accelerate aging;

•  Accelerated tests and (destructive) diagnostic tests to reveal unknown stress

factors;

•  Pilot projects to perform diagnostic tests in field to examine possibilities to

include their outputs in the condition indication system

These activities mainly contribute to build condition indication systems in Step 3.

Step 3: Establish condition indication system

A  condition  indication  system  at  the  standard  maturity  level,  as  introduced  in

Section 4.3.2, includes:

•  diagnosis  indicators,  acquired  with  standard  procedures  from  inspections,
measurements, and/or sensors. The raw diagnostic data are interpreted with

258

proper  knowledge  rules.  Developing  and  adapting  knowledge  is  an
important responsibility of Step 3.
remaining  life  indicators,  established  from  life  data  analysis.  It  estimates
increase of failure rates, therefore takes aging into consideration.

•

•  operation  stress  indicators,  established  according  to  the  results  of

accelerated life tests.

As  Figure  D.2  shows,  the  CI  at  the  standard  level  is  already  including  all  of  its

componential indicators.

The managed level

The  managed  level  does  not  add  blocks  to  Figure  D.1  and  D.2.  Nevertheless,  the
capability  to  assemble  the  CI  at  the  end  of  Figure  D.2  only  provides  knowledge  on
condition  indexing.  This  knowledge  is  insufficient  to  support  the  managed  level  of
maintenance  scheduling.  Fortunately,  the  missing  knowledge  on  failure  modes  can  be
acquired from FTA and FMECA. Through FTA, the functional part of failure modes can
be revealed. Through FMECA, the side-effect part of failure modes can be empirically
learned.

The optimal level

The  main  upgrades  from  the  managed  level  (reliability-centered)  to  the  optimal

level (risk-based) are in two aspects:

•

•

the  failure  effects  are  measured  in  multiple  business  values,  through
applying the risk consequence matrix introduced in Chapter 2.
the costs of PM plans and other countermeasures are considered.

The  first  upgrade  will  influence  FMECA  in  Step  1.  The  second  upgrade  will
challenge the profitability of advanced measuring techniques and sensors, because they
are  expensive  to  install  and  maintain.  The  green  blocks  in  Figure  D.2  show  some
primary concern on the contribution of advanced measuring technologies to reduce risks
of failures.

Acknowledgements

Investigating  a  multidisciplinary  subject,  this  thesis  has  been  completed  with  the

contribution from a wide range of people with different backgrounds.

First all, I would like to thank  my promoter prof. dr. Johan Smit for bringing me
from China to the peaceful town of Delft in 2008, for providing me with an opportunity
to  perform  this  research  in  high  voltage  laboratory  when  cooperating  with  the
management  faculty,  for  guiding  this  research  with  his  deep  insight  in  many  difficult
moments, as well as for supporting my personal development in all feasible ways.

I am also grateful to my daily supervisor dr. ir. Dhiradj Djairam, for his enormous
enthusiasm  and  effort  to  managing  this  project  routinely,  to  review  all  pieces  of
scientific texts that I have written, to enhance my level of English and to teach me all
formalities in this university.

I appreciate many colleagues for inspiring or contributing to some sections of this

thesis. They are listed specifically as follows.

Prof. dr. hab. Edward Gulski and dr. Sander Meijer taught me the essential concepts
of asset management which are explained in Chapter 1 and 2, when they supervised me
in  the  early  stage  of  this  project.  The  thesis  of  dr.  Benjamin  Quak  was  an  important
material in these lessons.

Dr.  ir.  Peter  Morshuis  collaborated  with  me  in  most  studies  on  statistics  and
dielectrics, from which Chapter 3 was formed. The works of Rogier Jongen and Lukasz
Chmura are essential references in these studies. Additionally, Section 3.4 was finished
in  a  project  together  with  ir.  Cees  Spoorenberg  and  dr.  Zongrong  Xu  from  Smit
Transformers and dr. Xiaolin Chen, Paul van Nes, Bertus Naagen from my group.

Ir. Ravish Mehairjan inspired me with the question answered in Section 3.3 and the
maturity  method  in  Section  4.4.  Meanwhile,  his  practical  experience  on  maintenance
management  from  Stedin  was  reflected  in  Chapter  4.  Sitting  in  the  same  office  and
investigating  the  same  topic,  the  information  exchanged  between  us  is  invaluable.

281

Moreover,  my  knowledge  on  condition  management  was  also  significantly  extended
during discussions with Mahannad Al-Suhaily.

Chapter 6 was finished when I stand on the shoulder of dr. Telli van der Lei, the
first person from the management faculty that I contact frequently. She has taught me
the  basic  of system  diagrams,  discussed  with  me  in depth  the  significance of strategic
AM and developed the first case of system analysis for strategic AM.

Dr. Dorota Kurowicka, dr. Robert Ross and dr. Mark de Bruijne have reviewed and

commented on some part of this thesis.

Naturally, a happy working environment neutralizes the negative emotions caused
by  difficulties  during  this  research.  In  this  sense  I  must  thank  all  group  members,
including  those  whom  have  been  mentioned  above,  as  well  as  Roy,  Barry,  Jur,  Piotr,
Dennis,  Roman,  Thomas,  Huifei,  Alex,  Tom,  Gautam,  Winne,  Aad  and  Wim.
Additionally, I would like to thank ir. Anton Janssen, ir. Nico Steentjes, ir. Jan Buesink
and  Martijn  Feteris  for  allowing  me  to  experience  daily  practice  in  a  number  of
interesting projects of Liander.

Finally,  I  wish  to  express  my  deepest  thankfulness,  and  probably  apology,  to  my
parents, for sending their only child far away. This thesis definitely benefits from their
precaution-based  strategy  on  my  early  education  (“tiger  mother”  in  today’s  English).
Actually,  the  topic  of  long-term  risks  was  originally  motivated  by  their  belief  that
precaution  is  the  only  strategy  against  long-term  risks.  Even  though  my  conclusions
disagree with their belief, I wish they can still enjoy reading it.

faculty  of  Electrical

TU Delft, as a PhD researcher investigating “strategic asset management for flexibility
of  future  electrical  infrastructures”  together  with  Next  Generation  Infrastructure
Foundation. Since 2013, he is employed by Liander, a major Dutch DSO. Currently he
is working as a consultant on asset management strategies.
